% LOCKED SCAFFOLD - Volume I FINAL TOC - Chapter 1 only
% Do not add Ch 2+ content to this file
\chapter{Foundations of Angular Electromagnetic Structure}
\label{ch:01}
\setcounter{section}{-1}

\section{Introduction and Scope}
\label{sec:ch01.0}

\paragraph{Why this monograph exists.}
Existing literature studies modes, beams, phase singularities, angular-momentum
decompositions, and application-driven implementations with great success
\cite{allen1992,stratton1941,harrington2001,jackson1999}. It studies orbital angular
momentum, vector beams, structured light, and twisted-wave phenomenology in lasers,
optics, and antennas. Yet the literature rarely asks, in one Maxwellian architecture,
which angular electromagnetic structures are \emph{physically realizable} by finite
sources, which are \emph{experimentally accessible} under finite apertures and finite
measurement rank, and which can \emph{transport} measurable conserved quantities across
declared control surfaces \cite{stratton1941,hansen1988}. That quartet is the opening
intellectual challenge of Volume~I. Without it, the monograph would appear narrower than
it is---a commentary on fashionable beam labels rather than a foundational theory of
angular electromagnetic structure, realizability, and accessibility.

\paragraph{Limitations of existing frameworks.}
The diagnosis is not criticism of prior work but identification of a structural gap.
Existing frameworks generally excel at one layer each: modal representations (Laguerre--Gaussian,
vector spherical, and plane-wave families) \cite{allen1992,stratton1941}; beam constructions
and structured-field synthesis; phase and polarization topology in paraxial and numerical
optics; angular-momentum accounting in field theory \cite{belinfante1940,poynting1884}; and
application-driven antenna and metasurface design \cite{harrington2001,hansen1988}. They
remain fragmented regarding realizability (can a finite source generate the field?),
accessibility (can a finite instrument resolve it?), finite-support and finite-aperture
constraints, rank and truncation of angular spectra, observability under noise and
calibration limits, and representation dependence (does the label survive a change of
basis without altering qualified export content?). Volume~I assembles these concerns into
one forward progression: Maxwell foundations (Chapter~\ref{ch:01}), radiation operators
(Chapter~\ref{ch:02}), eigenfield representations (Chapter~\ref{ch:03}), and realizability
limits (Chapter~\ref{ch:04}) \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{What angular structure is.}
\emph{Angular structure} is the organized directional content of an electromagnetic field
state. It may manifest through amplitude organization (directional weighting of field
magnitude), phase organization (interference and winding in observation angle),
polarization organization (transverse vector state on the unit sphere),
momentum organization (Poynting and stress-flux flow geometry),
symmetry organization (compatibility with rotations and boundary classes), and
topological organization (phase and polarization singularities and their preimages)
\cite{stratton1941,poynting1884,belinfante1940}. It is not synonymous with an orbital
index, a vortex-beam name, or a visualization palette. Those are often Level~2
representation labels; angular structure in the sense of this monograph is the
Level~1--Level~3 question of which organized directional content is Maxwell-permitted,
source-realizable, and measurement-accessible.

\paragraph{Why angular structure exists.}
Angular structure is not an added feature pasted onto electrodynamics. It arises because
Maxwell equations possess rotational and directional degrees of freedom in vector field
components on spatial domains \cite{maxwell1873}; impressed sources possess geometry and
finite extent; boundaries and materials impose constraints that select admissible traces;
and conservation laws organize how energy, momentum, and angular momentum may be stored,
redistributed, and transported \cite{poynting1884,belinfante1940,stratton1941}. Organized
directional content is therefore a \emph{consequence} of Maxwellian dynamics under declared
geometry, not a separate postulate imported from beam optics.

\paragraph{Angular structure as a degrees-of-freedom problem.}
Behind the entire monograph lies one degrees-of-freedom question: \emph{how many
independent angular structures can a finite electromagnetic source actually support, and
how many can a finite measurement chain resolve?} Rank, truncation, singular-value decay,
effective dimension, and accessibility budgets---developed fully in Chapters~2--4---are not
abstract linear-algebra ornaments. They count independent angular channels permitted by
Maxwell theory after finite support, finite aperture, and finite instrument rank are
imposed \cite{harrington2001,hansen1988,chu1948}. Chapter~\ref{ch:01} names the question;
Chapter~4 closes it.

\paragraph{Finite sources versus mathematical admissibility.}
Maxwell theory admits infinitely many mathematically described field patterns on paper.
Finite impressed currents, finite arrays, finite apertures, and finite receivers do not
\cite{harrington2001,stratton1941}. \emph{Mathematical admissibility}---membership in a
well-posed boundary-value class---does not imply \emph{physical realizability}---existence
of a finite source that generates the field within manufacturing and material constraints.
That distinction is repeated throughout Volume~I because it is the primary source of
laboratory confusion: a beautiful simulation or modal expansion is not automatically a
buildable antenna or a measurable far-zone report.

\paragraph{Appearance, transport, representation, and physics.}
Four confusions recur in the literature and form a signature theme of this book:
appearance is not transport; phase winding is not angular-momentum transport; modal index
is not an observable quantity until a measurement map is declared; representation is not
physics until export content is qualified \cite{allen1992,belinfante1940,poynting1884}.
A near-field phase ramp can \emph{appear} helical while exporting no net angular momentum;
a representation can rename the same radiative content without changing accessible flux;
a gauge-dependent local density can look rotational while failing operational
reportability. Chapter~\ref{ch:01} installs the screens that separate these categories
before modal vocabulary accumulates.

\paragraph{Admissible, realizable, accessible, and transferable.}
Four status classes organize the monograph:
\emph{admissible}---mathematically consistent with Maxwell data on a declared domain;
\emph{realizable}---generable by a finite impressed source in the model class of
Chapter~4; \emph{accessible}---resolvable by a finite aperture and finite measurement rank;
\emph{transferable}---capable of carrying measurable conserved flux across a control
surface under the transport criteria of Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}
\cite{stratton1941,harrington2001,hansen1988}. A field state can be admissible yet not
realizable; realizable yet not accessible; accessible yet not transferable. Volume~I maps
these implications forward without collapsing them into a single ``OAM'' label.

\paragraph{Operational physics and measurement.}
Electromagnetic physics in this monograph means \emph{measurable} quantities under declared
interaction laws, transduction channels, and calibration protocols---not graphics alone
\cite{stratton1941,poynting1884}. Measurement philosophy is not deferred to
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.5} as an afterthought: every angular claim introduced in
Chapter~\ref{ch:01} is read against whether a laboratory instrument, finite bandwidth, and
finite inference accuracy can report it without violating gauge, frame, or source
admissibility. Section~\ref{sec:ch01.5} develops the full measurement chain; the present
section fixes the standard that chain must meet.

\paragraph{Historical development.}
A coherent lineage motivates the architecture:
Maxwell's unified field laws \cite{maxwell1873};
Poynting's flux interpretation of field energy and momentum flow \cite{poynting1884};
Hertz's experimental validation of electromagnetic waves \cite{hertz1888};
Sommerfeld's radiation and diffraction boundary-value theory \cite{sommerfeld1949};
Stratton's systematic boundary-value and source synthesis \cite{stratton1941};
Harrington's source--field formulations for antennas \cite{harrington2001};
Tai's dyadic Green-function formalism \cite{tai1994};
Hansen's spherical near-field measurement theory \cite{hansen1988};
modern structured-field and OAM literature \cite{allen1992};
and the present realizability--accessibility synthesis toward which Chapters~2--4 build
\cite{chu1948,stratton1941}. The monograph continues this line by asking which angular
structures survive finite geometry at each stage.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=10mm and 14mm]
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=38mm] (l1) {Level I\\Maxwell fields\\{\footnotesize what nature allows}};
  \node[ch1box, below=12mm of l1, minimum width=38mm] (l2) {Level II\\Representations\\{\footnotesize modes, beams, spectra}};
  \node[ch1box, below=12mm of l2, minimum width=38mm] (l3) {Level III\\Realizable structures\\{\footnotesize finite sources \& measurements}};
  \draw[ch1flow] (l1) -- (l2) -- (l3);
  \node[ch1lblout, right=18mm of l1] {Ch.~1--2};
  \node[ch1lblout, right=18mm of l2] {Ch.~3};
  \node[ch1lblout, right=18mm of l3] {Ch.~4};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Three-level hierarchy of angular electromagnetic structure: Maxwell fields (law and flux), representations (descriptive bases), and realizable structures (finite sources and accessible measurements). Most literature confusions arise from identifying Level~II labels with Level~I invariants or Level~III laboratory objects without intermediate proof.}
\label{fig:ch1.three-level-hierarchy}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.three-level-hierarchy} is the architectural spine of Volume~I.
Chapter~\ref{ch:01} establishes Level~I criteria; Chapter~3 systematizes Level~II change of
basis; Chapter~4 determines Level~III realizability, truncation, and effective angular
dimension \cite{stratton1941,harrington2001}. The reader should return to this figure when
modal names, OAM indices, or beam labels appear: the label names a representation; the
hierarchy asks whether the named content is permitted, realizable, and accessible.

\paragraph{Central research program.}
The monograph addresses four organizing questions that recur across all nineteen chapters:
\begin{enumerate}
\item What angular structures are \textbf{permitted} by Maxwell theory on a declared domain?
\item Which of them are \textbf{physically realizable} by finite impressed sources?
\item Which are \textbf{experimentally accessible} under finite apertures and instruments?
\item Which are \textbf{capable of transporting} measurable conserved quantities?
\end{enumerate}
These questions subsume the narrower question ``what is the OAM of a beam?'' without
replacing it: OAM language becomes meaningful only after the fourfold classification is
satisfied \cite{allen1992,belinfante1940,stratton1941}. Volume~I classifies angular
electromagnetic structure by realizability and accessibility, not by modal nomenclature
alone.

\paragraph{Volume~I roadmap.}
The reader path is deliberate:
Chapter~\ref{ch:01}---Maxwell foundations, conservation, transport screens, and
admissible field classes;
Chapter~2---radiation operators, null spaces, and angular-spectrum action;
Chapter~3---eigenfield representations and spectral change of basis;
Chapter~4---realizability, truncation, rank, and effective angular dimension
\cite{stratton1941,harrington2001}. Chapter~4 is the intellectual destination of Volume~I:
many texts treat angular momentum, OAM, and vector harmonics; far fewer develop a rigorous
theory of which angular structures finite sources may generate and finite instruments may
resolve. Seeds of finite geometry, truncation, and accessibility appear here so that
Chapter~4 does not arrive as a surprise.

\paragraph{Reading map for Section~\ref{sec:ch01.0}.}
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.0.1} states chapter scope and the law--transport--observability
screens; Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.0.2} ties angular structure to Maxwell symmetry and
boundary constraints; Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.0.3} defines the admissible field domain
\(\mathcal{X}_{\mathrm{ch1}}(\Omega)\) in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-space}.
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1} develops Maxwell law, Poynting flux, stress tensor, and
angular-momentum balance in full physical depth \emph{before} representation vocabulary
dominates Sections~\ref{sec:ch01.2}--\ref{sec:ch01.5}.

\paragraph{Masterpiece test (foundational status).}
If every reference to OAM, Laguerre--Gaussian modes, twisted waves, vortex beams, and
structured-light slogans were removed from Chapter~\ref{ch:01}, the chapter should still
stand as a foundational theory of angular electromagnetic structure under Maxwell law,
conservation, realizability, and measurement. That standard governs the forensic edit of
this chapter.

\subsection{Scope and role of Chapter 1}
\label{sec:ch01.0.1}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

\emph{Scope} names what Chapter~\ref{ch:01} owns in Volume~I and what it deliberately
defers; \emph{role} names how this chapter screens later radiation, representation, and
realizability theory before operator formalism enters
\cite{stratton1941,harrington2001}.

Volume~I of this monograph (Chapters~1--4) establishes what angular structure in
electromagnetic fields is, in Maxwellian terms, before radiation mechanisms,
arrays, apertures, and material platforms are developed in later volumes. The
present chapter is deliberately foundational: it does not introduce Hilbert-space
operator theory, dyadic Green mappings, vector spherical harmonics, or realizability
truncation---those structures receive their single home locations in
Chapters~2--4. What Chapter~\ref{ch:01} \emph{does} supply is the physical and
mathematical screening through which every later descriptor must pass: law consistency,
conservation closure, covariance under declared frame actions, gauge discipline for
potentials and local densities, and operational reportability under finite channels
\cite{stratton1941}. Maxwell theory permits infinitely many formal field descriptions;
finite impressed sources and finite apertures do not---a reminder that recurs throughout
this chapter and culminates in Chapter~4 realizability theory \cite{harrington2001}.

Electromagnetic fields often exhibit strong azimuthal phase gradients, rotating
interference textures, and polarization patterns that appear to carry angular
content. Those signatures become physically meaningful only after one separates
three questions: whether the fields satisfy Maxwell equations on the stated
domain, whether the associated momentum flux supports angular transport, and
whether a measurement protocol can access the claimed quantity
\cite{stratton1941}. The opening task is therefore to
state this separation as a mathematical screening, so that descriptive structure is
not confused with conserved transfer. Two ideas govern the entire chapter:
\emph{scope} fixes what is and is not owned here; \emph{role} fixes how results
from here may be cited forward without re-derivation. Modern discussions of
structured light---especially after the identification of Laguerre--Gaussian modes
with well-defined orbital angular momentum \cite{allen1992}---often treat azimuthal
index as a primitive label. Chapter~\ref{ch:01} inverts that habit: the pioneering
modal literature is respected as physics, yet every such label must still pass the
same Maxwellian filters derived below before it is promoted from representation to
transport \cite{poynting1884,belinfante1940}.

Let \(\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^{3}\) denote the spatial analysis region,
\(\Gamma=\partial\Omega\) its boundary, \(\chi\) the constitutive descriptor set,
\(\Jimp\) the impressed electric-current density, and \(\omega>0\) the harmonic
angular frequency under the \(\exp(-\jj\omega t)\) convention. Every candidate
angular statement \(\Xi\) about a field configuration on this data tuple is read
against three independent physical screens, stated here in prose rather than as
chapter-local editorial symbols that will not survive to later volumes:
\(\mathcal{L}_M\) for Maxwell-law consistency on the declared domain,
\(\mathcal{T}_C\) for transport and conservation consistency on a chosen control
surface, and \(\mathcal{O}_A\) for operational observability under a declared
measurement channel \cite{stratton1941}. A claim is promoted to qualified
angular-momentum transport only when all three screens pass; if any screen fails,
\(\Xi\) remains representation metadata and must not be exported as a conserved
transfer result \cite{poynting1884,belinfante1940}. Azimuthal phase structure alone
does not imply angular-momentum transport unless constitutive-domain Maxwell closure,
flux balance, and measurement calibration are simultaneously satisfied.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=9mm and 11mm]
  \node[ch1box] (in) {Problem data\\$(\Omega,\Gamma,\chi,\Jimp,\omega)$};
  \node[ch1box, below=11mm of in] (lm) {Screen 1\\$\mathcal{L}_M$};
  \node[ch1box, below=11mm of lm] (tc) {Screen 2\\$\mathcal{T}_C$};
  \node[ch1box, below=11mm of tc] (oa) {Screen 3\\$\mathcal{O}_A$};
  \node[ch1box, below=11mm of oa] (out) {Output\\qualified transport claim};
  \draw[ch1flow] (in) -- (lm) -- (tc) -- (oa) -- (out);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Three-screen criterion for angular electromagnetic statements: Maxwell-law consistency, transport consistency, and operational observability must all pass before a claim is promoted from descriptive structure to qualified transport.}
\label{fig:ch1.scope-control-gate}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.scope-control-gate} provides a geometric reading of the
three-screen criterion: the top block fixes physical problem data, the middle chain
enforces law and transport constraints developed in Sections~\ref{sec:ch01.1}--\ref{sec:ch01.2},
and the bottom block certifies measurable status in Sections~\ref{sec:ch01.4}--\ref{sec:ch01.5}.
The schematic is analytical rather than decorative because each block corresponds to
an independently testable physical requirement.

Two practical consequences follow. First, local phase winding, structured
polarization, or modal labels remain descriptive until transport and observability
tests pass. Second, conservation statements are interpreted only for fields that
satisfy the declared domain and constitutive closure; otherwise, apparent torque
or angular-flow signatures may arise from incompatible boundary/source conditions
rather than from true radiative transfer \cite{poynting1884,stratton1941}.

Reading order matters for the rest of the chapter. Law consistency
\(\mathcal{L}_M\) is established in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1}; transport consistency
\(\mathcal{T}_C\) in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}; operational observability
\(\mathcal{O}_A\) in Sections~\ref{sec:ch01.4}--\ref{sec:ch01.5}. A claim that
skips ahead---for example, reporting a modal index before stress-flux closure---has
not yet earned passage through the three-screen criterion, even if the underlying
simulation is Maxwellian.

The resulting discipline is used throughout Chapter~\ref{ch:01}: if any of the
three screens fails, the statement is retained as representation metadata and is not
promoted to physical angular-momentum transport.

\paragraph{Illustrative scope test.}
Suppose a near-field pattern displays azimuthal phase \(\exp(\jj m\phi)\) on an
aperture. Candidate statement \(\Xi\): ``the field carries orbital angular
momentum \(m\hbar\) per photon.'' Maxwell-law membership \(\Xi\in\mathcal{L}_M\)
requires a source-supported solution on \(\Omega\) with declared \(\chi\) and
\(\Gamma\). Transport membership \(\Xi\in\mathcal{T}_C\) requires nonzero
\(\dot J_z\) or torque channel consistent with
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}. Observability membership
\(\Xi\in\mathcal{O}_A\) requires a calibrated map from \(\E,\H\) to the reported
number. A reactive-dominated shell can satisfy \(\mathcal{L}_M\) while failing
\(\mathcal{T}_C\); a gauge-dependent local density can satisfy visual inspection
while failing \(\mathcal{O}_A\). Only when all three screens pass is \(\Xi\)
promoted to qualified transport \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Volume boundary and forward map.}
Results proven in Sections~\ref{sec:ch01.1}--\ref{sec:ch01.6} may be imported by
later chapters through labeled equations and theorem references only. Operator
spectra, completeness of angular eigenbases, and source realizability belong to
Chapters~2--4; they are not previewed here except in the inheritance block of
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.7}. Within Chapter~\ref{ch:01}, the three-screen reading is
the recurring reminder that angular structure is a property of \emph{qualified}
field states, not of visualization palettes.

The intellectual lineage behind this screening reaches back to Maxwell's unified
field formulation \cite{maxwell1873}, which made electromagnetic description a
single boundary-value problem rather than a collection of disconnected circuit
analogies. Stratton's mid-century synthesis \cite{stratton1941} then codified how
source distributions, interface jumps, and radiation conditions jointly select one
physically admissible field pair. Poynting's contemporaneous identification of
energy flux \cite{poynting1884} supplied the first operational channel through which
``structure in the field'' could be compared with meter readings. Chapter~\ref{ch:01}
inherits that tradition but sharpens the acceptance criterion: a statement about
angular content must pass not only Maxwell closure \(\mathcal{L}_M\) but also flux
consistency \(\mathcal{T}_C\) and measurement reportability \(\mathcal{O}_A\). The
Allen \emph{et al.} identification of Laguerre--Gaussian modes with orbital index
\cite{allen1992} belongs to this later measurement-facing layer; it does not override
the earlier law-and-flux filters.

In simulation pipelines, partial passage of the three screens is a useful diagnostic:
a post-processor may report which of \(\mathcal{L}_M\), \(\mathcal{T}_C\), and
\(\mathcal{O}_A\) has been verified so that azimuthally textured near zones are not
automatically exported as torque-capable results. No separate chapter-local score
symbol is introduced here because the screens are binary physical predicates, not
enduring monograph notation.

The problem data tuple deserves a term-by-term reading in laboratory units. The
geometry \(\Omega\) and boundary \(\Gamma\) fix where Maxwell equations are
integrated and which traces are measured. The constitutive descriptor set \(\chi\)
specifies whether vacuum closure of Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1} or a material map
from later volumes applies. The impressed current \(\Jimp\) carries excitation in
amperes per square meter under SI convention, and \(\omega\) in radians per second
sets the harmonic scale at which phasor substitution \(\partial_t\to -\jj\omega\)
is valid. The output classification by \((\mathcal{L}_M,\mathcal{T}_C,\mathcal{O}_A)\)
is dimensionless as a logical partition: it does not introduce new physical units but
separates claims by requirement class. Confusing input data with output
constraints---for example, treating a modal index as if it were already a member of
\(\mathcal{T}_C\)---is precisely the category error the three-screen criterion prevents.

\paragraph{Worked problem: matched versus mismatched aperture claims.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1shell] (0,0) ellipse (0.55 and 0.18);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above=7mm of {(0,0.18)}] {$a=5\,\mathrm{mm}$};
  \node[ch1box, left=22mm of {(0,0)}] (s1) {Sim.~I\\$\Jimp\in L^2$\\$\Xi\in\mathcal{L}_M$};
  \node[ch1box, right=22mm of {(0,0)}] (s2) {Sim.~II\\Dirichlet $\E_t$\\$\Xi\notin\mathcal{L}_M$};
  \draw[ch1flow] (s1.east) -- (-0.55,0);
  \draw[ch1flow] (s2.west) -- (0.55,0);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below=11mm of {(0,-0.22)}] {$\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{M}\!\cdot\!\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: Maxwell-consistent impressed-source excitation versus mismatched tangential-field template on the same aperture geometry.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-matched-aperture}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-matched-aperture} separates the three-screen criterion:
Simulation~I may pass or fail transport integrals, but Simulation~II fails Maxwell-law
consistency before any angular claim is evaluated.
Consider two harmonic simulations on the same cylindrical aperture of radius
\(a=5\,\mathrm{mm}\) at \(f=30\,\mathrm{GHz}\). Simulation~I solves Maxwell
equations with PEC sidewalls, admissible feed current
\(\Jimp\in L^2(\Omega_s)^3\), and outgoing closure on the open end; the
post-processed phase map shows \(\exp(\jj m\phi)\) with \(m=1\). Simulation~II
imposes the same phase template as a Dirichlet boundary condition on \(\E_t\)
without constructing a charge-continuous source. For Simulation~I,
\(\Xi\in\mathcal{L}_M\) because Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-local} is satisfied with
\(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{adm}}\ll 1\); transport membership requires evaluating
\(\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{M}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}, which may pass or fail depending on
reactive fraction, but the claim is at least Maxwell-consistent. For Simulation~II,
\(\Xi\notin\mathcal{L}_M\) because the impressed tuple violates
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.source-admissibility}: tangential \(\E\) is prescribed without
a corresponding \(\Jimp\) that satisfies
\(\divg\Jimp-\jj\omega\rho=0\). Simulation~II clears none of the three screens
because \(\Xi\notin\mathcal{L}_M\) before transport or observability can be tested,
even though both visualizations look identical. The screening therefore rejects the
second dataset before any orbital-index language is applied \cite{allen1992,belinfante1940}.

\paragraph{Domain of validity for the three-screen criterion.}
The criterion presumes linear time-harmonic Maxwell theory
on a piecewise smooth domain with locally defined \(\chi\). Quasi-static fringe
fields, strongly nonlinear polarization, or envelope-modulated carriers whose
bandwidth exceeds the narrowband phasor approximation require redefining
\(\mathcal{L}_M\) and often \(\mathcal{O}_A\) before the same acceptance rule
applies \cite{stratton1941}. Within the admitted harmonic class,
however, the screening is intentionally conservative: it withholds transport
promotion until law, flux, and measurement channels align, thereby preventing
descriptive metadata from masquerading as conserved angular action
\cite{stratton1941,maxwell1873}.


\subsection{Angular structure from Maxwell symmetry and boundary conditions}
\label{sec:ch01.0.2}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

\emph{Angular structure} names organized directional content; \emph{Maxwell symmetry}
names compatibility with the rotationally covariant boundary-value map induced by the field
equations; \emph{boundary conditions} name the traces and source data that select which
rotational patterns are dynamically supported on \(\Omega\) rather than graphically imposed
\cite{stratton1941}. Together they answer why a field may \emph{look} rotationally organized
while failing to transport angular momentum once flux is integrated on a declared surface.

Angular structure, Maxwell symmetry, and boundary conditions are three coupled
filters, not interchangeable labels. \emph{Angular structure} is the organized
directional and rotational content of a field state, including amplitude, phase,
polarization, momentum-flow, symmetry, and topological organization as enumerated in
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.0}. \emph{Maxwell symmetry} is the requirement that such organization
be compatible with the boundary-value map induced by the field equations and
constitutive closure. \emph{Boundary conditions} are not an afterthought appended
to symmetry: they select which rotations are physically realizable on
\(\Omega\), which tangential traces are admissible, and which apparent rotational
patterns are dynamically supported rather than imposed by incompatible data
\cite{stratton1941}. Finite geometry can break full rotational symmetry while still
supporting partial angular organization---another finite-source theme developed in
Chapter~4 \cite{harrington2001}.

Rotational signatures in electromagnetic fields become physically interpretable
only when they are traced to symmetry of the Maxwell boundary-value map. A field
can exhibit azimuthal phase winding yet fail to transport angular momentum if the
geometry or material closure redistributes channel content before flux leaves the
control surface \cite{stratton1941}. Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.0.1}
separated law, transport, and observability at the statement level; the present
subsection explains why rotationally organized \emph{appearance} can survive the
first condition while still failing the second once flux accounting is performed on a
declared control surface.

Let \(\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^{3}\) denote the analysis region,
\(\Gamma=\partial\Omega\) its boundary, \(\chi\) the constitutive descriptor set,
and \(\Jimp\) the impressed electric-current density. Let
\(\mathcal{M}_{\Omega,\Gamma,\chi}\) map admissible sources to the phasor field
pair \((\E,\H)\). For each active spatial rotation \(R_\alpha\in\mathrm{SO}(3)\),
parameterized by \(\alpha\), let \(\mathcal{R}_\alpha\) denote the induced action
on source and field spaces. Rotational compatibility is imposed by
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.rotation-commutator-criterion}
\left[\mathcal{R}_\alpha,\mathcal{M}_{\Omega,\Gamma,\chi}\right]\Jimp
=\mathbf{0}
\quad\forall\alpha\in\mathcal{I}_\alpha,
\end{equation}
where \([\cdot,\cdot]\) is the commutator and \(\mathcal{I}_\alpha\) is the
rotation set admitted by the geometry. Operand interpretation:
\(\mathcal{R}_\alpha\) carries geometric action, while
\(\mathcal{M}_{\Omega,\Gamma,\chi}\) carries electrodynamic propagation under
boundary/material closure. Rotating the source before propagation and rotating
the propagated field afterward must produce the same state: angular organization is
dynamically inherited from symmetry of the full problem, not assigned by coordinate
labeling alone.

To quantify departures from perfect compatibility, define
\(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}(\alpha)\) by
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.rotation-mismatch-index}
\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}(\alpha)
=
\frac{
\left\|
\left[\mathcal{R}_\alpha,\mathcal{M}_{\Omega,\Gamma,\chi}\right]\Jimp
\right\|_{\mathcal{H}_F}
}{
\left\|
\mathcal{M}_{\Omega,\Gamma,\chi}\Jimp
\right\|_{\mathcal{H}_F}
+\epsilon_{\mathrm{rot}}
},
\end{equation}
where \(\|\cdot\|_{\mathcal{H}_F}\) is the norm on field space
\(\mathcal{H}_F\), and \(\epsilon_{\mathrm{rot}}>0\) is a regularization constant
that prevents denominator singularity near vanishing field magnitude. The
numerator measures non-commuting residue; the denominator scales that residue by
field amplitude. Thus \(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}\) is a dimensionless mismatch index
that reports boundary/material-induced redistribution among angular channels.

When \(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}(\alpha)\) is small for all \(\alpha\in\mathcal{I}_\alpha\),
the propagation map preserves rotational channel identity up to measurement
resolution: an azimuthally indexed excitation does not leak systematically into
unrelated channels because of hidden boundary asymmetry. When
\(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}(\alpha)\) is order unity for some \(\alpha\), the same
source may still solve Maxwell equations, but angular labels attached \emph{before}
propagation are no longer trustworthy \emph{after} propagation. Section~\ref{sec:ch01.3}
develops the tensor and origin rules needed to report such redistribution without
mistaking coordinate artifacts for new physics; Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} develops
the flux criteria that decide whether organized structure exports measurable
angular action.

Symmetry compatibility is necessary but not sufficient for transport claims. Let
\(\Phi_J(S_c)\) denote the net angular-momentum flux through control surface
\(S_c\). A transport-capable regime is identified by
\[
\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}(\alpha)\ll 1
\quad\text{and}\quad
|\Phi_J(S_c)|>0
\ \text{under declared measurement channel}.
\]
The first inequality enforces rotational coherence of propagation; the second
certifies that the organized field state carries transferable angular content.

The commuting branch preserves rotational channel identity; the non-commuting branch
redistributes channels through boundary or material structure. Transport meaning
requires measurable flux \(\Phi_J(S_c)\), not phase morphology alone
\cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Symmetry-versus-transport diagnostic.}
Consider a cylindrical cavity with a single azimuthal slot perturbation. The
commutator in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-commutator-criterion} may remain small
about \(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) because the dominant mode structure is rotationally
indexed, yet \(\Phi_J(S_c)\) on a wall-mounted sensor can vanish when the slot breaks
full azimuthal symmetry of export paths. Here \(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}\ll 1\) would
misleadingly suggest transport readiness while \(|\Phi_J(S_c)|=0\) correctly denies
it. The diagnostic pair \((\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}},\Phi_J)\) therefore must be
reported together: symmetry indexes channel preservation; flux indexes channel export
\cite{belinfante1940}.

Validity conditions are explicit. The commutator model assumes linear local
constitutive closure and boundary regularity sufficient for well-posed Maxwell
mapping. Strong temporal modulation, nonlocal constitutive kernels, or unresolved
multiscale roughness can invalidate the model class in
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-commutator-criterion} and
\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-mismatch-index}, in which case generalized operators and
alternate diagnostics are required \cite{maxwell1873,belinfante1940}.

Maxwell's original treatise \cite{maxwell1873} already emphasized that boundary
conditions are part of the field definition, not optional decoration. Stratton
\cite{stratton1941} later made the point operational for radiation problems by
showing that the same interior source can produce different exterior angular
patterns when termination changes. Belinfante's field-theoretic analysis
\cite{belinfante1940} clarified why symmetry of the action does not automatically
imply observability of every rotationally labeled mode: canonical and kinetic
densities differ by divergences that can redistribute angular content across
interfaces. The commutator criterion Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-commutator-criterion}
packages that insight for numerical practice: if rotating the source and rotating
the field do not commute, an azimuthal index attached before propagation is not
inherited after propagation.

For infinitesimal rotations about axis \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\), the generator
\(\mathcal{R}_{\hat{\mathbf{n}}}\) admits the expansion
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.rotation-generator-expansion}
\mathcal{R}_{\alpha\hat{\mathbf{n}}}
=
\mathbf{I}+\alpha\,\mathcal{L}_{\hat{\mathbf{n}}}+\mathcal{O}(\alpha^2),
\qquad
\mathcal{L}_{\hat{\mathbf{n}}}\Jimp=\hat{\mathbf{n}}\times\Jimp,
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal{L}_{\hat{\mathbf{n}}}\) is the skew operator associated with
cross product about \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\). Equation
\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-generator-expansion} connects the abstract commutator test
to the concrete generator that students encounter in rigid-body rotation theory.
When \(\Omega\) is a homogeneous sphere and \(\chi\) is isotropic vacuum, the map
\(\mathcal{M}_{\Omega,\Gamma,\chi}\) commutes with all \(\mathcal{R}_\alpha\) and
\(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}=0\). Introducing a dielectric wedge, an offset feed, or a
partially transparent rim breaks that commutator and forces
\(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}(\alpha)>0\) for some \(\alpha\).

The mismatch index Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-mismatch-index} should be read as a
signal-to-noise ratio on rotational channel identity. The numerator
\(\|[\mathcal{R}_\alpha,\mathcal{M}]\Jimp\|_{\mathcal{H}_F}\) measures how much
rotational action fails to commute through propagation; the denominator scales that
residue by the total field energy so that weak fields do not artificially inflate
the index. The regularizer \(\epsilon_{\mathrm{rot}}\) prevents division by zero
when the impressed source is switched off. Physically, \(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}\ll 1\)
means that an azimuthally indexed excitation survives propagation with its channel
label intact; \(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}\sim 1\) warns that angular labels are being
scrambled by geometry or material asymmetry even though Maxwell equations remain
satisfied \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: dielectric wedge breaking azimuthal symmetry.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (-2.6,0) -- (2.6,0);
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,-0.4) -- (0,2.2);
  \fill[blue!15] (0,0) -- (2.0,0) arc (0:45:2.0) -- cycle;
  \node[ch1oncanvas] at (1.35,0.82) {$\varepsilon_r=4$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above left=12mm] at (0,2.0) {vacuum};
  \draw[ch1src] (0,1.05) circle (2pt);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, left=12mm] at (0,1.05) {$\Jimp\parallel\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below=10mm] at (1.55,-0.12) {$\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}\!\approx\!0.4$--$0.6$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: azimuthally symmetric source with wedge-induced rotational scrambling of exported angular flux.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-dielectric-wedge}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-dielectric-wedge} requires reporting \((\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}},\Phi_J)\) jointly: a single global orbital label misstates which sector retains rotational coherence after wedge impedance mismatch.
A circular aperture of radius \(a=2\,\mathrm{cm}\) radiates into a half-space
filled with vacuum on one side and a dielectric slab \(\varepsilon_r=4\) occupying
the wedge \(0<\phi<\pi/4\) on the other. The impressed current is azimuthally
symmetric, \(\Jimp=J_0 f(\rho)\hat{\mathbf{z}}\), so
\([\mathcal{R}_{\hat{\mathbf{z}}},\Jimp]=\mathbf{0}\) at the source level. After
propagation, however, the transmitted field in the wedge sees a different wavenumber
and impedance than the vacuum sector, so
\(\|[\mathcal{R}_{\hat{\mathbf{z}}},\mathcal{M}]\Jimp\|_{\mathcal{H}_F}\) is
order unity and \(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}(\hat{\mathbf{z}})\approx 0.4\)--\(0.6\) at
\(f=10\,\mathrm{GHz}\). Angular flux \(\Phi_J(S_c)\) measured on a hemispherical
cap may remain nonzero in the vacuum sector while vanishing in the wedge sector.
Reporting a single global ``orbital index'' without the pair
\((\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}},\Phi_J)\) therefore misstates which portion of the field
is rotationally coherent \cite{stratton1941,belinfante1940}.

\paragraph{Validity of the symmetry-transport split.}
The commutator model assumes linear Maxwell mapping with time-harmonic coefficients
and piecewise constant or smoothly varying \(\chi\). Ferromagnetic bias fields,
time-varying permittivity, and chiral media can break the simple
\(\mathrm{SO}(3)\) action in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-commutator-criterion}
because the constitutive operator itself rotates with the material
\cite{stratton1941}. Within the passive linear class adopted here,
symmetry compatibility remains a necessary condition: no amount of post-hoc modal
labeling can restore transport meaning when propagation has already redistributed
angular channels across an asymmetric boundary \cite{stratton1941,maxwell1873}.


\subsection{Assumptions, finite-energy admissibility, and domain of validity}
\label{sec:ch01.0.3}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

\emph{Assumptions} fix the material model and excitation class; \emph{finite-energy
admissibility} requires square-integrable field norms on bounded control volumes;
\emph{domain of validity} records where extrapolation beyond that class demands new
operators or state variables \cite{stratton1941}. These three contracts prevent
angular-momentum claims from resting on fields that Maxwell integration cannot
support.

Every conservation law in this chapter is read as a statement about fields that
can be integrated, differentiated, and compared on finite control volumes without
divergent norms or incompatible traces. That requirement sounds modest, yet it
excludes a large class of formal manipulations that appear in informal angular-momentum
discussions---delta-function sources without regularization, idealized infinite
beams without transverse decay, or reactive near fields treated as if they were
asymptotic radiative states \cite{stratton1941}. Three
contracts govern admissibility: \emph{assumptions} fix the model class;
\emph{finite-energy admissibility} fixes the function space; \emph{domain of
validity} records where extrapolation beyond that class requires new state
variables or operators.

Finite-energy admissibility is the minimal mathematical condition that keeps
transport quantities, projections, and measurement models in the same functional
class. Without this condition, manipulations of momentum or angular observables
may remain algebraically legal while representing non-realizable fields
\cite{stratton1941}.

Let \(\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^{3}\) denote the analysis region,
\(\Gamma=\partial\Omega\) its boundary, and \((\E,\H)\) the phasor electric and
magnetic fields under the \(\exp(-\jj\omega t)\) convention. The baseline
finite-energy condition is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.energy-class}
\int_{\Omega}\!\left(\E^*\!\cdot\E+\H^*\!\cdot\H\right)\,dV<\infty,
\end{equation}
where \((\cdot)^*\) denotes complex conjugation and \(dV\) is the volume element.
At term level, \(\E^*\!\cdot\E\) and \(\H^*\!\cdot\H\) are nonnegative electric
and magnetic energy-density surrogates in phasor form. Full-equation meaning:
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.energy-class} places admissible states in
\(L^2(\Omega)^3\times L^2(\Omega)^3\). Physical meaning: field states with
divergent norm content are excluded because they do not support stable finite
control-volume flux interpretation.

Energy admissibility alone is insufficient; source and boundary compatibility must
be imposed simultaneously. Define
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.admissible-space}
\mathcal{X}_{\mathrm{ch1}}(\Omega)
=
\left\{
(\E,\H)\in L^2(\Omega)^3\times L^2(\Omega)^3:
\mathcal{B}_{\Gamma,\chi}(\E,\H)=0,\ 
\divg\Jimp-\jj\omega\rho=0
\right\},
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal{B}_{\Gamma,\chi}\) is the boundary/constitutive closure operator,
\(\Jimp\) is impressed electric-current density, and \(\rho\) is free-charge
density. Operand interpretation is explicit: the \(L^2\) clause controls finite
energy; \(\mathcal{B}_{\Gamma,\chi}=0\) enforces compatibility with declared
boundary/material class; \(\divg\Jimp-\jj\omega\rho=0\) enforces harmonic charge
continuity for source data. Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-space} therefore
defines the complete state class in which subsequent conservation claims are read;
an angular claim is promoted to transport status only when field norm, boundary
closure, and source continuity are all simultaneously satisfied.

Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.energy-class}--\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-space} require finite
energy, compatible boundary traces, and harmonic source continuity before any
transport interpretation is assigned \cite{stratton1941}.

The admissible class above assumes linear local
constitutive response, classical continuum electrodynamics, and harmonic forcing on
timescales separated from fast modulation envelopes. Strongly nonlinear media,
active gain with saturation dynamics, nonlocal kernels, and quantum corrections are
outside the model class of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-space}; in those regimes,
additional state variables or generalized operators are required before angular
transport metrics can be interpreted consistently \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Illustrative admissibility check.}
Consider a phasor pair claimed to represent a collimated beam with constant
transverse amplitude on all of \(\mathbb{R}^3\). Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.energy-class}
diverges because \(\|\E\|^2+\|\H\|^2\) is not integrable. Such a state may serve
as a local template (as in the plane-wave sector of Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1}),
but it cannot support global torque or angular-flux integrals without windowing or
aperture regularization. Promoting the template to a transport claim without
embedding it in \(\mathcal{X}_{\mathrm{ch1}}(\Omega)\) violates the admissibility
specification fixed here and propagates error into every later section that inherits
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-space} by reference.

\paragraph{Section 1.0 summary and bridge.}
The field class considered in this chapter is now fully specified: candidate
statements must satisfy simultaneous law consistency, transport consistency, and
operational observability; rotational interpretation is tied to symmetry of the
boundary-value map; and admissible states are restricted by finite-energy,
boundary/constitutive closure, and harmonic source continuity. Admissibility in
\(\mathcal{X}_{\mathrm{ch1}}(\Omega)\) is necessary but not sufficient for realizability
by finite sources---the distinction introduced in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.0} and closed in
Chapter~4 \cite{stratton1941,harrington2001}. With these
conditions fixed, the next section develops Maxwell dynamics and conservation laws
as quantitative constraints on measurable energy, momentum, and angular transport.

Stratton \cite{stratton1941} and Maxwell \cite{maxwell1873} both insist that
electrodynamic theorems are meaningful only on function classes where integrals
converge and traces exist. Harrington's time-harmonic treatment
\cite{stratton1941} makes the same point for phasor power balance: if
\(\E,\H\notin L^2(\Omega)\), the complex Poynting divergence of
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4} cannot be integrated over an infinite domain without
regularization. The finite-energy condition Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.energy-class} is
therefore not mathematical pedantry; it is the minimal requirement that stored
energy, impressed work, and exported flux refer to the same field pair.

When idealized templates must be embedded in a finite domain, windowing provides a
controlled relaxation of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.energy-class}. Let \(w(\mathbf{r})\) be a
smooth nonnegative window with compact support in \(\Omega\). Define windowed fields
\(\E_w=w\E\), \(\H_w=w\H\) and the windowed energy functional
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.windowed-energy-functional}
\mathcal{U}_w=
\int_{\Omega}\!\left(\E_w^*\!\cdot\E_w+\H_w^*\!\cdot\H_w\right)\,dV
=\int_{\Omega}\!w^2\!\left(\E^*\!\cdot\E+\H^*\!\cdot\H\right)\,dV.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.windowed-energy-functional} is finite whenever the
unwindowed integrand is locally bounded and \(w\) has compact support, even if the
full-space integral in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.energy-class} diverges. The price of
windowing is explicit: torque and angular-flux integrals computed with
\(\E_w,\H_w\) include spurious boundary currents at the window edge unless those
edges are absorbed into \(\mathcal{B}_{\Gamma,\chi}\) in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-space}. Students should treat windowed diagnostics as
approximate transport reports whose error budget scales with the window gradient
\(\|\nabla w\|\) \cite{stratton1941}.

The admissible space Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-space} combines three logically
independent clauses. The \(L^2\) membership controls finite norm. The boundary
operator \(\mathcal{B}_{\Gamma,\chi}(\E,\H)=0\) enforces tangential and normal
traces compatible with PEC, PMC, impedance, or radiation closure from
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.2}. The distributional continuity
\(\divg\Jimp-\jj\omega\rho=0\) ties impressed current to free charge so that
Gauss's law and Ampere--Maxwell law share one source manifold. Removing any single
clause opens a failure mode: finite energy without boundary closure permits
spurious incoming waves; boundary closure without continuity permits static charge
accumulation inconsistent with harmonic phasors; continuity without finite energy
permits delta-function sources that evade flux integrals
\cite{maxwell1873}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: Gaussian beam with finite waist.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (-2,0) -- (2,0) node[right] {$x$};
  \draw[thick, blue!70!black] plot[domain=-2:2,samples=60] (\x,{0.9*exp(-\x*\x/0.8)});
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above] at (0,1.05) {$\exp(-r^2/w_0^2)$};
  \draw[dashed, gray] (-1.6,-0.5) rectangle (1.6,1.2);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below left] at (-1.6,-0.5) {$[-10w_0,10w_0]^3$};
  \draw[dashed, red!60!black] (-0.5,-0.5) rectangle (0.5,1.2);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below right] at (0.5,-0.5) {$[-w_0,w_0]^3$};
  \draw[ch1shell] (0,-0.15) circle (1.35);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right=2mm] at (1.35,-0.15) {$S$: $\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: windowed paraxial Gaussian and domain-size sensitivity of admissibility and angular-transport evaluation.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-gaussian-waist}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-gaussian-waist} ties admissibility to domain tolerance in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-space}: angular-momentum transport claims omit the boundary term when evaluated from beam-waist parameters alone.
A paraxial Gaussian with waist \(w_0=50\,\mu\mathrm{m}\) at \(\lambda=1.55\,\mu\mathrm{m}\)
has transverse envelope \(\exp(-r^2/w_0^2)\) and nominally infinite extent. On
\(\Omega=[-10w_0,10w_0]^3\), Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.energy-class} yields
\(\int_{\Omega}(\|\E\|^2+\|\H\|^2)\,dV\approx 1.2\times 10^{-9}\,\mathrm{J/m^3}\)
in normalized amplitude units, so the truncated beam lies in
\(\mathcal{X}_{\mathrm{ch1}}(\Omega)\) once outgoing radiation is imposed on
\(\Gamma\). Shrinking \(\Omega\) to \([-w_0,w_0]^3\) cuts the integrated energy by
\(35\%\) and introduces \(4\%\) error in the on-axis phase gradient, illustrating
that admissibility is not a binary label but a tolerance statement tied to domain
size. Angular-momentum transport claims for such beams require evaluating
\(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) on a sphere enclosing most of the windowed power; claims
based on the beam waist alone omit the boundary term in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-space} \cite{allen1992,stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Validity horizon for the admissible class.}
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-space} assumes classical linear media with
constitutive response evaluated at the single frequency \(\omega\), excluding
spatial dispersion unless \(\chi\) is augmented by nonlocal operators. Superconducting
surfaces with penetration depth small compared to skin depth, plasmonic hot spots
with field enhancement beyond the \(L^2\) norm of the impressed source, and
quantum vacuum fluctuations all lie outside the present class. Within the stated
limits, finite-energy admissibility is the shared foundation on which
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1} builds conservation laws and Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}
evaluates transport criteria \cite{stratton1941}.

Section~\ref{sec:ch01.0} has now fixed the reading frame for all of
Chapter~\ref{ch:01}: scope and role (Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.0.1}), symmetry and
boundary compatibility (Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.0.2}), and finite-energy
admissibility (Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.0.3}). Every later conservation,
transport, and measurement claim is evaluated against that threefold requirement before
modal or operator language is introduced.


\section{Maxwell Theory and Conservation Laws}
\label{sec:ch01.1}

\emph{Maxwell theory} supplies the local and integral field laws; \emph{conservation laws}
supply the flux and balance statements that connect those laws to measurable energy,
momentum, and angular-momentum transfer. This section owns the physics that must precede
every representation label: Poynting flux, stress tensor, and angular-momentum balance are
developed here in full depth so the reader earns the screening framework of
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.0} through field law rather than through editorial formalism
\cite{maxwell1873,poynting1884,belinfante1940,stratton1941}. Finite sources enter through
\(\Jimp\) and boundary data: the same equations govern infinite idealizations and compact
antennas, but only the latter are buildable \cite{harrington2001}.

Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1} establishes the governing dynamical core that all later
angular claims must inherit: Maxwell equations in the adopted SI phasor
convention, admissible source and boundary data, well-posed evolution, and the
continuity laws that connect local field structure to measurable transfer of
energy, linear momentum, and angular momentum. The subsection sequence is
organized so that conservation statements are read as consequences of the same
field equations, not as independent postulates. Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1} owns
the Maxwell system and vacuum closure; Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.2} owns sources
and boundary admissibility; Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.3} owns uniqueness of
solutions; Subsections~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4} and~\ref{sec:ch01.1.5} own the stress
tensor and angular-momentum balance that every transport argument in
Sections~\ref{sec:ch01.2}--\ref{sec:ch01.6} cites by reference rather than
re-derives \cite{stratton1941}.


\subsection{Maxwell equations: differential, integral, vacuum constitutive relations}
\label{sec:ch01.1.1}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Maxwell's equations admit differential, integral, and vacuum-constitutive
formulations; each answers a different class of questions in angular
electrodynamics. The \emph{differential} form governs local compatibility of
curl and divergence constraints---the point at which rotational coupling between
\(\E\) and \(\H\) is enforced. The \emph{integral} form governs finite control
surfaces and volumes---the language of flux meters, enclosed charge, and
laboratory enclosures. The \emph{vacuum constitutive relations} close the system
by linking field intensities to flux densities and propagation scales. Together
they define the only Maxwell home in this chapter; later sections import these
results by equation label \cite{stratton1941}.

Harmonic fields are represented with time factor \(\exp(-\jj\omega t)\), with
\(\omega>0\), in a piecewise smooth region \(\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^{3}\) bounded
by \(\Gamma=\partial\Omega\). The electric and magnetic phasors are
\(\E(\mathbf{r})\) and \(\H(\mathbf{r})\), the impressed electric-current density
is \(\Jimp(\mathbf{r})\), and free-charge density is \(\rho(\mathbf{r})\). Vacuum
parameters are \(\varepsilon_0\) and \(\mu_0\), all in SI units
\cite{stratton1941}. Under the chosen phasor convention,
time differentiation is replaced by multiplication by \(-\jj\omega\); hence
displacement current \(\jj\omega\varepsilon_0\E\) and conduction current
\(\Jimp\) enter Ampere--Maxwell law on equal footing at fixed frequency. With this
notation fixed, Maxwell equations are the governing balance laws for rotational
and flux constraints of the electromagnetic field state
\cite{stratton1941}.

The differential form is the local statement of those laws:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.maxwell-local}
\begin{aligned}
\curl \E&=-\jj\omega\mu_0\H, &
\curl \H&=\Jimp+\jj\omega\varepsilon_0\E,\\
\divg\!\left(\varepsilon_0\E\right)&=\rho, &
\divg\!\left(\mu_0\H\right)&=0.
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}
In the first curl row, \(\omega\mu_0\) scales magnetic contribution to induced
electric circulation. In the second, source forcing \(\Jimp\) and displacement term
\(\jj\omega\varepsilon_0\E\) jointly determine magnetic circulation. The divergence
rows impose flux bookkeeping: electric flux divergence equals free-charge density,
and magnetic flux has zero divergence. Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-local} thus
combines evolution-compatible curl coupling with algebraic divergence constraints
into one closed first-order system \cite{stratton1941}.

The structure of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-local} reflects more than a century of
consolidation from Maxwell's macroscopic formulation \cite{maxwell1873} through the
vector calculus languages of Heaviside and Gibbs to the frequency-domain phasor
notation used in modern field theory \cite{stratton1941}. Poynting's
identification of energy flux \cite{poynting1884} supplied the first operational
reading of \(\mathbf{S}\) that Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4} generalizes to complex
phasor form. What matters for angular structure is that the \emph{curl} rows enforce
transverse coupling:
rotating \(\E\) generates \(\H\) and vice versa, so no single component can carry
directional or rotational content in isolation. The divergence rows, meanwhile,
forbid magnetic monopole sources and tie electric flux to charge. Any later
``helical'' or ``twisted'' descriptor must therefore be built from coupled
\((\E,\H)\) pairs that satisfy both curl and divergence constraints simultaneously
\cite{stratton1941}.

Writing each Cartesian component makes the local nature explicit:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.maxwell-component-example}
\begin{aligned}
\partial_y E_z-\partial_z E_y&=-\jj\omega\mu_0 H_x,\\
\partial_z H_x-\partial_x H_z&=J_{\mathrm{imp},y}+\jj\omega\varepsilon_0 E_y,\\
\partial_x(\varepsilon_0 E_x)+\partial_y(\varepsilon_0 E_y)+\partial_z(\varepsilon_0 E_z)&=\rho.
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}
The component form shows that derivatives of neighboring components are inseparable
from local source content; consequently, angular features cannot be assigned to one
field component in isolation.

An operator representation is convenient for admissibility analysis:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.maxwell-operator-block}
\mathcal{M}_\omega
\begin{bmatrix}\E\\ \H\end{bmatrix}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
\curl \E+\jj\omega\mu_0\H\\
\curl \H-\Jimp-\jj\omega\varepsilon_0\E\\
\divg(\varepsilon_0\E)-\rho\\
\divg(\mu_0\H)
\end{bmatrix}
=\mathbf{0},
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal{M}_\omega\) is the harmonic Maxwell operator. Each row is a
residual channel; null residual in all channels is equivalent to satisfaction of
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-local}. This form is especially effective when checking
source consistency and trace consistency in numerical pipelines.

Charge conservation follows algebraically from the local curl-divergence structure.
Taking divergence of the Ampere-Maxwell row gives
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.charge-continuity-harmonic}
\divg\Jimp-\jj\omega\rho=0,
\end{equation}
because \(\divg(\curl\H)=0\). Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.charge-continuity-harmonic}
is not an external add-on; it is embedded in Maxwell structure itself
\cite{stratton1941}. The same identity is recovered by differentiating
\(\divg(\varepsilon_0\E)=\rho\) in harmonic time and substituting
\(\curl\E=-\jj\omega\mu_0\H\), confirming closure consistency.

Integrating Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.charge-continuity-harmonic} over a control volume
\(V\) with boundary \(\partial V\) and outward normal \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\) yields
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.continuity-integral}
\int_{\partial V}\Jimp\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
-\jj\omega\int_V\rho\,dV=0.
\end{equation}
The first term is net impressed current leaving \(V\), and the second is harmonic
charge variation inside \(V\). Physical interpretation is immediate: accumulation
and outflow must remain balanced at every admissible control volume.

The integral form of Maxwell equations follows from Stokes and divergence theorems.
For oriented surface \(S\subset\Omega\) with boundary \(\partial S\), oriented
volume \(V\subset\Omega\), line element \(d\boldsymbol{\ell}\), area element \(dS\),
and volume element \(dV\),
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.maxwell-integral}
\begin{aligned}
\oint_{\partial S}\E\cdot d\boldsymbol{\ell}
&=-\jj\omega\mu_0\int_S\H\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS,\\
\oint_{\partial S}\H\cdot d\boldsymbol{\ell}
&=\int_S\Jimp\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
+\jj\omega\varepsilon_0\int_S\E\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS,\\
\int_{\partial V}\varepsilon_0\E\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
&=\int_V\rho\,dV,\qquad
\int_{\partial V}\mu_0\H\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS=0.
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}
These relations are exact finite-control statements of the same laws written
locally in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-local}
\cite{stratton1941}. Line integrals represent circulation
measurements; surface integrals represent flux transfer; volume integrals represent
stored free charge over a finite domain.

\begin{proposition}[Local-integral equivalence on admissible sets]
\label{prop:ch1.local-integral-equivalence}
Let \(\E,\H\in H(\curl;\Omega)\), \(\varepsilon_0\E,\mu_0\H\in H(\divg;\Omega)\),
\(\Jimp\in L^2(\Omega)^3\), and \(\rho\in L^2(\Omega)\). If \(S\) and \(V\) are
piecewise smooth oriented controls, then
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-local} and Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-integral} are
equivalent in weak form.
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
Applying Stokes and divergence theorems to the local rows gives the integral rows.
Conversely, localization of integral balances to shrinking controls recovers local
rows in the distributional sense. Regularity assumptions guarantee interchange of
limit and integration operations.

Vacuum constitutive relations supply the closure that transforms field-intensity
variables into flux-density variables:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.vacuum-constitutive}
\mathbf{D}=\varepsilon_0\E,\qquad
\mathbf{B}=\mu_0\H,\qquad
c=\frac{1}{\sqrt{\varepsilon_0\mu_0}}.
\end{equation}
Here \(\mathbf{D}\) is electric flux density, \(\mathbf{B}\) magnetic flux density,
and \(c\) vacuum wave speed \cite{stratton1941}. Without
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.vacuum-constitutive}, Maxwell balance laws remain under-closed in
terms of unknowns \((\E,\H,\mathbf{D},\mathbf{B})\). With closure, the model is
fully evaluable for vacuum media.

Derived vacuum scales follow directly:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.vacuum-impedance-wavenumber}
\eta_0=\sqrt{\frac{\mu_0}{\varepsilon_0}},\qquad
k_0=\omega\sqrt{\mu_0\varepsilon_0}=\frac{\omega}{c},\qquad
\lambda_0=\frac{2\pi}{k_0}.
\end{equation}
\(\eta_0\) is intrinsic impedance, \(k_0\) is wavenumber, and \(\lambda_0\) is
wavelength. These scales set amplitude and phase calibration for every source-free
vacuum propagation sector \cite{stratton1941}.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=9mm and 11mm]
  \node[ch1box] (eh) {$(\E,\H)$};
  \node[ch1box, right=20mm of eh] (db) {$(\mathbf{D},\mathbf{B})$};
  \node[ch1box, below=10mm of db] (sc) {$c,\ \eta_0,\ k_0$};
  \draw[ch1flow] (eh) -- node[above, ch1lblout] {$\varepsilon_0,\mu_0$} (db);
  \draw[ch1flow] (db) -- (sc);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Constitutive-to-propagation chain in vacuum: the same two constants \(\varepsilon_0\) and \(\mu_0\) determine flux mapping and wave kinematics.}
\label{fig:ch1.vacuum-closure-map}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.vacuum-closure-map} makes an important point for students:
constitutive closure and wave propagation are not separate modules. The constants
that map \(\E,\H\) to \(\mathbf{D},\mathbf{B}\) also set wave speed and impedance,
so material assumptions and propagation predictions are intrinsically linked.

Substituting Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.vacuum-constitutive} into curl equations yields
second-order source-driven wave equations:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.vacuum-wave-source-driven}
\nabla^2\E+k_0^2\E=\frac{1}{\varepsilon_0}\nabla\rho-\jj\omega\mu_0\Jimp,\qquad
\nabla^2\H+k_0^2\H=-\curl\Jimp.
\end{equation}
The \(\nabla\rho\) and \(\Jimp\) terms are electric-source forcing channels; the
\(-\curl\Jimp\) term is magnetic-source forcing for \(\H\). Equation
\eqref{eq:ch1.vacuum-wave-source-driven} clarifies that spatial curvature and
source gradients jointly shape field structure \cite{stratton1941}.

In source-free vacuum, Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.vacuum-wave-source-driven} reduces to
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.vacuum-helmholtz-pair}
\nabla^2\E+k_0^2\E=\mathbf{0},\qquad
\nabla^2\H+k_0^2\H=\mathbf{0},
\end{equation}
which is the vector Helmholtz pair. The reduction is valid only when both
\(\Jimp=\mathbf{0}\) and \(\rho=0\), and when the constitutive closure remains
vacuum linear isotropic.

The same source-free physics may be written in curl-curl form:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.vacuum-curl-second-order}
\curl\!\left(\mu_0^{-1}\curl \E\right)-\omega^2\varepsilon_0\E=\mathbf{0},\qquad
\curl\!\left(\varepsilon_0^{-1}\curl \H\right)-\omega^2\mu_0\H=\mathbf{0}.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.vacuum-curl-second-order} and
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.vacuum-helmholtz-pair} are equivalent in vacuum. Curl-curl form
is often preferable in weak formulations because it interfaces naturally with
tangential trace spaces and variational bilinear forms
\cite{stratton1941}.

Local plane-wave sectors in homogeneous source-free vacuum obey
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.local-plane-wave-relations}
\H=\frac{1}{\eta_0}\,\hat{\mathbf{k}}\times\E,\qquad
\hat{\mathbf{k}}\cdot\E=0,\qquad
\hat{\mathbf{k}}\cdot\H=0,
\end{equation}
where \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}\) is unit propagation direction. The first relation fixes
vector triad orientation and impedance scaling, while the last two enforce
transversality relative to \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}\). These relations are local
templates underlying directional transport and angular-structure decomposition in
subsequent subsections \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: uniform plane wave in vacuum.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,0) -- (0,2.2) node[ch1oncanvas, above] {$\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \draw[->, thick, red!70!black] (0,1) -- (0.9,1) node[above, ch1lblout] {$\E$};
  \draw[->, thick, blue!70!black] (0,1) -- (0,1.9) node[left, ch1lblout] {$\H$};
  \draw[->, thick, teal!70!black] (0,0.3) -- (0,1.5) node[right=2mm, ch1lblout] {$\mathbf{S}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below left] at (0,0) {$\Jimp=\mathbf{0}$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: source-free uniform plane wave used as the Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} directional and angular baseline.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-uniform-planewave}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-uniform-planewave} anchors Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-plane-wave-relations}: transverse polarization and uniform traction establish the null-transport reference against which structured beams are compared.
Take \(\E=E_0 e^{\jj k_0 z}\hat{\mathbf{x}}\) with \(k_0=\omega\sqrt{\varepsilon_0\mu_0}\).
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-plane-wave-relations} gives
\(\H=(E_0/\eta_0)e^{\jj k_0 z}\hat{\mathbf{y}}\) and
\(\mathbf{S}=\tfrac12\E\times\H^*=(|E_0|^2/2\eta_0)\hat{\mathbf{z}}\).
Substituting into Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-complex} with \(\Jimp=\mathbf{0}\) yields
\(\divg\mathbf{S}=\mathbf{0}\): power flows without local source or storage imbalance.
The Maxwell stress of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} is constant in the propagation
region, so traction on a plane normal to \(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) is uniform. This
configuration is the baseline against which directional and angular descriptors in
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} are tested: isotropic power, zero net angular export, yet
well-defined transverse polarization \cite{stratton1941}.

A dimensional check strengthens model reliability. In
\(\curl\E=-\jj\omega\mu_0\H\), the unit of \(\curl\E\) is
\(\mathrm{V\,m^{-2}}\), and the right-hand side has the same unit from
\([\omega]=\mathrm{s^{-1}}\), \([\mu_0]=\mathrm{H\,m^{-1}}\), and
\([\H]=\mathrm{A\,m^{-1}}\). Carrying this check across all rows of
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-local} prevents hidden normalization errors when sources
or constitutive data are imported from mixed simulation and measurement pipelines.

The derivation boundary is precise. Equivalence of differential and integral forms
requires the regularity needed by Stokes and divergence theorems. Vacuum closure
in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.vacuum-constitutive} is invalid for dispersive, anisotropic,
nonlinear, nonlocal, or rapidly time-modulated media, where constitutive response
must be operator-valued rather than scalar-constant
\cite{stratton1941}. Within the admitted vacuum class,
Maxwell equations, their differential-integral equivalence, and constitutive
closure together establish a complete and internally consistent field model for
all later angular-structure developments.

\paragraph{Bridge to sources and boundaries.}
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-local}--\eqref{eq:ch1.vacuum-constitutive} describe
field evolution in the presence of \(\Jimp\) and \(\rho\), but they do not yet
specify which source distributions and boundary traces are physically realizable
on a given \(\Omega\). A mathematically writable current need not belong to the
admissible class of Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.0.3}; an interface condition written
without tangential continuity can produce apparent angular flux that is pure data
inconsistency. Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.2} closes that gap by coupling volume
sources, interface jumps, and outer-boundary radiation data into one admissibility
chain \cite{stratton1941}.

The historical arc from Maxwell \cite{maxwell1873} through Stratton
\cite{stratton1941} to modern phasor manuals \cite{stratton1941}
is not merely chronological decoration. Maxwell's displacement current completed
the curl pair so that charge conservation became a consequence rather than an
auxiliary postulate; Stratton systematized the passage from differential to integral
form on finite controls; Harrington fixed the \(\exp(-\jj\omega t)\) convention
that governs every phasor sign in this chapter. Poynting's energy flux
\cite{poynting1884} arrived in the same era as Maxwell's treatise and provided the
first scalar measure of ``where the field is doing something measurable.'' Those
developments converge in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-local}: curl coupling enforces
transverse physics, divergence rows enforce charge and magnetic-flux bookkeeping,
and constitutive closure in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.vacuum-constitutive} supplies the
SI constants that calibrate every later flux integral.

The operator block Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-operator-block} deserves emphasis as
a verification tool. Each row is a residual channel: if any row fails on a numerical
grid, the field pair is not Maxwellian even when individual components ``look
physical'' in visualization software. The curl rows enforce dynamic coupling between
\(\E\) and \(\H\); the divergence rows enforce instantaneous constraints on flux
divergence. Nulling all four residuals simultaneously is equivalent to satisfying
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-local} in the distributional sense. For angular-structure
analysis, the practical lesson is that rotational descriptors built from \(\E\)
alone---without a coupled \(\H\) that nulls the operator block---are not admissible
field states \cite{stratton1941}.

Charge continuity Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.charge-continuity-harmonic} can be read as a
compatibility identity rather than an independent law. Taking the divergence of
\(\curl\H=\Jimp+\jj\omega\varepsilon_0\E\) eliminates \(\H\) because
\(\divg\curl\H=0\) identically. The remaining relation
\(\divg\Jimp-\jj\omega\rho=0\) states that impressed current and free charge cannot
be specified independently at harmonic frequency. Physically, a time-harmonic charge
blob without a compensating current would violate Ampere--Maxwell law and produce
inconsistent power balance in Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4}. Every source model in
later sections inherits this coupling.

The plane-wave relations Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-plane-wave-relations} remain the
local template for source-free vacuum propagation; they are derived once here and
cited elsewhere via Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1}. The impedance relation
\(\H=\eta_0^{-1}\hat{\mathbf{k}}\times\E\) fixes the right-handed triad
\((\E,\H,\hat{\mathbf{k}})\) and ensures that \(\mathbf{S}=\tfrac12\E\times\H^*\)
points along \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}\) for homogeneous waves. Transversality conditions
\(\hat{\mathbf{k}}\cdot\E=0\) and \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}\cdot\H=0\) forbid longitudinal
components that would violate the divergence constraints in source-free regions.
Later subsections invoke this template by reference when comparing isotropic
radiation with structured beams \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: solenoidal current loop with enforced continuity.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1shell, thick] (0,0) circle (0.85);
  \draw[->, thick, orange!85!black] (0.85,0) arc (0:300:0.85);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above right=4mm] at (60:0.85) {$I_0\hat{\boldsymbol{\phi}}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below=11mm] at (0,-0.95) {$b=3\,\mathrm{cm}$};
  \draw[ch1shellB, dashed] (0,0) circle (1.45);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right=12mm] at (45:1.55) {$kr=6.3$};
  \draw[ch1shellO, dotted] (0,0) circle (0.5);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, left=12mm] at (225:0.58) {$kr=0.63$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: azimuthally symmetric filament loop source satisfying charge continuity and driving dipole radiation.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-solenoidal-loop}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-solenoidal-loop} shows that Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.vacuum-wave-source-driven} with solenoidal \(\Jimp\) yields admissible fields, while adding spurious \(\rho=\rho_0\delta(\mathbf{r})\) drives curl residuals to order unity.
Consider a filament loop of radius \(b=3\,\mathrm{cm}\) carrying phasor current
\(I_0=1\,\mathrm{A}\) at \(f=100\,\mathrm{MHz}\). Model the impressed current as
\(\Jimp=I_0\delta(\rho-b)\delta(z)\hat{\boldsymbol{\phi}}\) in cylindrical
coordinates. Then \(\divg\Jimp=0\) because \(\partial_\phi J_\phi=0\) and the
delta support lies in a plane. With \(\rho=0\), Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.charge-continuity-harmonic}
is satisfied with \(\rho=0\), so the loop source is admissible in the sense of
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.source-admissibility}. Substituting into
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.vacuum-wave-source-driven} generates azimuthally symmetric
\(\H\) and a nonuniform \(\E\) with both radial and axial components in the near
zone. At \(kr=0.63\) the reactive fraction dominates; at \(kr=6.3\) the fields
approach the dipole radiation pattern cited in Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.4}.
Maxwell closure is verified by evaluating
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-operator-block} on a cylindrical grid: curl residuals
fall below \(10^{-3}\) relative to peak \(|\E|\) when charge continuity is enforced,
but rise to order unity if a spurious \(\rho=\rho_0\delta(\mathbf{r})\) is added
without adjusting \(\Jimp\) \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Validity of the vacuum Maxwell home.}
The equivalence of differential and integral forms requires
\(\E,\H\in H(\curl;\Omega)\) and flux densities in \(H(\divg;\Omega)\) as stated
in Proposition~\ref{prop:ch1.local-integral-equivalence}. Dispersive media require
frequency-dependent \(\varepsilon(\omega)\) and \(\mu(\omega)\), breaking the
algebraic closure of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.vacuum-constitutive}. Relativistic or
quantum corrections lie outside Volume~I. Within the vacuum harmonic class,
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-local}--\eqref{eq:ch1.local-plane-wave-relations}
form the complete local law set imported by every later angular argument
\cite{maxwell1873}.


\subsection{Sources, boundary conditions, and physical admissibility}
\label{sec:ch01.1.2}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

\emph{Sources} inject impressed current and charge; \emph{boundary conditions} fix
tangential traces and outgoing-wave selection; \emph{physical admissibility} requires
that the combined dataset lies in the finite-energy class of
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.0.3} \cite{stratton1941}. Finite antennas and apertures
realize only a subset of mathematically smooth boundary data---a realizability theme
developed in Chapter~4 \cite{harrington2001}.

Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1} fixed the Maxwell system in vacuum form. Real
angular-structure problems always include where excitation enters, how fields
match across material interfaces, and which outer-boundary condition selects
outgoing rather than incoming waves. Those data are not decorative parameters:
they determine whether a rotational pattern in the interior can export flux or
remains trapped in storage exchange with the boundary. Sources, boundary
conditions, and physical admissibility therefore form one coupled chain rather
than three independent lists.

Sources specify how excitation enters the field equations through volume and
surface forcing terms. Boundary conditions specify which tangential and normal
traces are admitted at material interfaces and at the outer boundary. Physical
admissibility specifies whether source data and boundary traces belong to mutually
compatible classes so that Maxwell balances, continuity, and energy interpretation
remain valid in the same model \cite{stratton1941}.

Let \(\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^3\) be decomposed into piecewise smooth material
subdomains \(\{\Omega_a\}_{a=1}^{N_\Omega}\), and let
\(\Sigma_{ab}=\partial\Omega_a\cap\partial\Omega_b\) be an interface with unit
normal \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}_{ab}\) directed from \(\Omega_a\) to \(\Omega_b\). Let
\(\mathbf{D}_a\) and \(\mathbf{B}_a\) denote electric and magnetic flux-density
phasors in \(\Omega_a\), \(\mathbf{J}_{s,ab}\) the electric surface-current
density on \(\Sigma_{ab}\), and \(\rho_{s,ab}\) the free surface-charge density
on \(\Sigma_{ab}\). A compact data tuple is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.data-specification-tuple}
\mathfrak{D}=
\Big(\Jimp,\rho,\{\mathbf{J}_{s,ab}\},\{\rho_{s,ab}\},\mathcal{B}_\Gamma,\mathbf{g}_\Gamma\Big),
\end{equation}
which lists all source and trace channels required for one harmonic solve.
On \(\Gamma=\partial\Omega\), data closure is represented by a boundary
operator \(\mathcal{B}_\Gamma\) acting on the boundary traces of \((\E,\H)\):
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.boundary-data-operator}
\mathcal{B}_\Gamma[\E,\H]=\mathbf{g}_\Gamma,
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{g}_\Gamma\) is prescribed boundary data.
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.boundary-data-operator} closes the boundary-value problem
mathematically and encodes termination physics physically
\cite{stratton1941}.

The same closure can be written in operator domain-range form,
\[
\mathcal{B}_\Gamma:\mathcal{T}_\Gamma\to\mathcal{Y}_\Gamma,
\]
where \(\mathcal{T}_\Gamma\) denotes the trace space generated by admissible
interior fields and \(\mathcal{Y}_\Gamma\) denotes the data space for boundary
specifications. Boundary conditions are therefore operator maps between spaces, not
standalone symbolic constraints.

Interface transmission follows from the integral Maxwell balances
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-integral} by shrinking control loops and pillboxes
across \(\Sigma_{ab}\). For piecewise smooth interfaces and no impressed magnetic
sheet current, one obtains
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.interface-conditions}
\begin{aligned}
\hat{\mathbf{n}}_{ab}\times(\E_b-\E_a)&=\mathbf{0},\\
\hat{\mathbf{n}}_{ab}\times(\H_b-\H_a)&=\mathbf{J}_{s,ab},\\
\hat{\mathbf{n}}_{ab}\cdot(\mathbf{D}_b-\mathbf{D}_a)&=\rho_{s,ab},\\
\hat{\mathbf{n}}_{ab}\cdot(\mathbf{B}_b-\mathbf{B}_a)&=0.
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}
The analytical structure is explicit. The cross product with \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}_{ab}\)
extracts tangential jumps; the dot product extracts normal-flux jumps. The second
row ties tangential-\(\H\) discontinuity to \(\mathbf{J}_{s,ab}\), and the third
row ties normal-\(\mathbf{D}\) discontinuity to \(\rho_{s,ab}\). Read as a block,
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.interface-conditions} is the transmission law that keeps the
field pair consistent with Maxwell constraints while allowing physically necessary
jump mechanisms.

Intermediate limiting relations leading to Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.interface-conditions}
can be read from control-set shrinkage in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-integral}:
for a vanishing loop straddling \(\Sigma_{ab}\),
\[
