\lim_{\delta\to 0}\oint_{\partial S_\delta}\E\cdot d\boldsymbol{\ell}=0
\quad\Rightarrow\quad
\hat{\mathbf{n}}_{ab}\times(\E_b-\E_a)=\mathbf{0},
\]
and for a vanishing pillbox,
\[
\lim_{\delta\to 0}\int_{\partial V_\delta}\mathbf{D}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
=\rho_{s,ab}
\quad\Rightarrow\quad
\hat{\mathbf{n}}_{ab}\cdot(\mathbf{D}_b-\mathbf{D}_a)=\rho_{s,ab}.
\]
These limiting expressions expose the derivational path from integral balances to
compact jump laws, and make explicit which terms survive when control sets collapse
toward the interface.

From a physical viewpoint, interface law determines how angularly structured field
content crosses material partitions. Continuity classes and jump channels identify
which tangential and normal components transmit transport information, and which
components are reflected, screened, or converted into surface-supported response.

Outer closure is specified by partitioning
\(\Gamma=\Gamma_{\mathrm{PEC}}\cup\Gamma_{\mathrm{PMC}}\cup\Gamma_Z\), where
\(\Gamma_{\mathrm{PEC}}\) is perfect-electric-conductor boundary,
\(\Gamma_{\mathrm{PMC}}\) is perfect-magnetic-conductor boundary, and \(\Gamma_Z\)
is an impedance boundary with surface impedance \(Z_s\):
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.outer-boundary-classes}
\begin{aligned}
\hat{\mathbf{n}}\times\E&=\mathbf{0}\quad &&\text{on }\Gamma_{\mathrm{PEC}},\\
\hat{\mathbf{n}}\times\H&=\mathbf{0}\quad &&\text{on }\Gamma_{\mathrm{PMC}},\\
\hat{\mathbf{n}}\times\E&=Z_s\,(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\times\H)\times\hat{\mathbf{n}}
\quad &&\text{on }\Gamma_Z,
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}
with \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\) the outward unit normal on \(\Gamma\). Here
\(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\times\E\) and \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\times\H\) are tangential
electric and magnetic traces, while \((\hat{\mathbf{n}}\times\H)\times\hat{\mathbf{n}}\)
reorients tangential magnetic content into the impedance-coupled direction.
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.outer-boundary-classes} therefore functions as a boundary
trace selector mathematically, and as a model of ideal reflection or finite
surface dissipation physically.

The three boundary classes can be read as limiting responses for tangential field
content. The PEC relation suppresses tangential electric trace, the PMC relation
suppresses tangential magnetic trace, and the impedance relation preserves both
while coupling them through \(Z_s\). This hierarchy determines which energy and
momentum channels can pass through the boundary and which are reflected
\cite{stratton1941}.

For open-region truncation, an outgoing-trace condition is often required:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.radiation-trace-condition}
\hat{\mathbf{n}}\times\H
-\eta_0^{-1}(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\times\E)\times\hat{\mathbf{n}}
=\mathbf{o}(R^{-1}),\qquad R\to\infty,
\end{equation}
where \(\eta_0=\sqrt{\mu_0/\varepsilon_0}\) and \(R\) is truncation radius.
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.radiation-trace-condition} enforces asymptotically outgoing
power flow and prevents unphysical incoming-wave closure in exterior problems
\cite{stratton1941}.

Source admissibility is now imposed by regularity, localization, and continuity
compatibility. Let \(\Omega_s\subseteq\Omega\) be source support. The admissible
source class is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.source-admissibility}
\Jimp\in L^2(\Omega_s)^3,\qquad
\operatorname{supp}(\Jimp)\subseteq\overline{\Omega_s},\qquad
\divg\Jimp-\jj\omega\rho=0
\ \text{in }\mathcal{D}'(\Omega).
\end{equation}
The first term in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.source-admissibility} enforces finite source
energy in the local \(L^2\)-sense; the support condition enforces spatial
localization of excitation; the distributional continuity relation enforces
charge-current consistency under the adopted \(\exp(-\jj\omega t)\) phasor
convention. Taken together, these conditions exclude non-conservative source
prescriptions that would violate Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-local}.

Physical admissibility is not exhausted by source regularity alone. Source,
interface, and boundary channels must be jointly consistent:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.admissible-data-class}
\mathfrak{D}\in\mathbb{A}_{\omega}
\iff
\begin{cases}
\text{Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.source-admissibility} holds}\\
\text{Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.interface-conditions} holds on all interfaces}\\
\text{Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.boundary-data-operator} holds with an admissible trace class}\\
\text{Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.global-charge-compatibility} holds globally.}
\end{cases}
\end{equation}
The set \(\mathbb{A}_{\omega}\) depends on frequency because continuity phase
balance and trace response depend on \(\omega\). If any channel fails, Maxwell
fields may still be computed numerically but no longer inherit one coherent
conservation interpretation.

\begin{proposition}[Global compatibility of source and boundary data]
\label{prop:ch1.source-boundary-compatibility}
Assume Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.source-admissibility} and the boundary operator
constraint Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.boundary-data-operator}. Then admissible data must
satisfy the global charge-balance identity
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.global-charge-compatibility}
\int_{\Omega}\!\left(\divg\Jimp-\jj\omega\rho\right)\,dV
=\int_{\Gamma}\!\Jimp\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS-\jj\omega\int_{\Omega}\rho\,dV=0.
\end{equation}
\end{proposition}

Integrating the distributional continuity relation in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.source-admissibility} over \(\Omega\), applying divergence
theorem to \(\divg\Jimp\), and using admissible boundary traces from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.boundary-data-operator} gives the stated identity. The integral
\(\int_{\Gamma}\Jimp\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS\) is net outward impressed-current
flux, while \(\jj\omega\int_{\Omega}\rho\,dV\) is harmonic charge-storage rate.
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.global-charge-compatibility} is therefore a global
charge-balance law on admissible data classes.

Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.global-charge-compatibility} links boundary current flux to
interior charge storage at whole-domain scale. Local constitutive detail may vary
across subdomains, yet this balance remains mandatory for any admissible dataset.

A practical residual for source-continuity verification is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.admissibility-residual}
\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{adm}}=
\frac{\|\divg\Jimp-\jj\omega\rho\|_{L^2(\Omega)}}
{\|\divg\Jimp\|_{L^2(\Omega)}+\omega\|\rho\|_{L^2(\Omega)}+\epsilon_0},
\end{equation}
where \(\epsilon_0>0\) is a fixed nondimensional regularizer. Small
\(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{adm}}\) indicates numerically consistent source channels
before full field solution.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[thick] (-1.8,-1) rectangle (1.8,1);
  \fill[gray!8] (-0.9,-0.5) rectangle (0.2,0.6);
  \fill[gray!15] (0.3,-0.7) rectangle (1.5,0.8);
  \node[ch1oncanvas] at (-0.35,0.05) {$\Omega_a$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas] at (0.9,0.05) {$\Omega_b$};
  \draw[ch1shell] (0.2,-0.5) -- (0.3,-0.7);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above] at (0.25,-0.35) {$\Sigma_{ab}$};
  \fill[black!70] (-1.2,-0.3) rectangle (-1.8,0.3);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, left] at (-1.8,0) {$\Gamma_{\mathrm{PEC}}$};
  \fill[red!60] (0,-0.15) circle (2.5pt);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-0.35) {$\Omega_s$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Schematic coupling of source support, interface transmission, and outer-boundary closure for admissible Maxwell data.}
\label{fig:ch1.source-boundary-admissibility}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.source-boundary-admissibility} highlights the coupled nature
of admissibility: source support, interface transmission, and exterior trace class
form a single data manifold. Later transport and observable claims inherit this
manifold directly, because angular descriptors computed from \((\E,\H)\) are
credible only when the entire data chain is compatible.

Boundary class selects trace channels: PEC removes tangential \(\E\), PMC removes
tangential \(\H\), impedance couples both through \(Z_s\), and the radiation trace
enforces outgoing-wave dominance \cite{stratton1941}.

The validity boundary is equally clear. Piecewise smooth interfaces, local
constitutive response, and standard trace theory for boundary operators are
assumed; strongly nonlocal media, rough multiscale interfaces, and stochastic
boundary operators lie outside this subsection
\cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: impressed dipole source with charge continuity.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \node[ch1box, text width=28mm] (ok) {Admissible\\$\Jimp=J_0\delta(\mathbf{r})\hat{\mathbf{z}}$\\$\rho=0$};
  \node[ch1box, below=10mm of ok, text width=28mm] (bad) {Inadmissible\\$+\rho_0\delta(\mathbf{r})$};
  \draw[ch1src] (0,1.1) circle (2pt);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above=7mm] at (0,1.1) {origin};
  \draw[ch1flow] (ok) -- (bad);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right=14mm] at (bad.east) {$\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{adm}}\sim 1$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: charge-continuous versus charge-inconsistent impressed dipole source tuples.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-dipole-charge-continuity}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-dipole-charge-continuity} operationalizes Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.source-admissibility}: torque and power integrals lack conservation interpretation until \(\Jimp\) is corrected onto the charge-continuous manifold.
Let \(\Jimp(\mathbf{r})=J_0\delta(\mathbf{r})\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) in harmonic form
with no free charge, \(\rho=0\). Then \(\divg\Jimp=0\) and
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.global-charge-compatibility} holds exactly. If one instead models
\(\rho(\mathbf{r})=\rho_0\delta(\mathbf{r})\) without adjusting \(\Jimp\), then
\(\divg\Jimp-\jj\omega\rho=-\jj\omega\rho_0\delta(\mathbf{r})\neq 0\) and
\(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{adm}}\) in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissibility-residual} becomes
order unity. A field solver may still return a numerical \(\E\)-field, but power and
torque integrals computed from that pair lack a consistent conservation interpretation
until \(\Jimp\) is corrected, for example by adding a feed capacitance current
\(\Jimp\leftarrow \Jimp+\jj\omega\varepsilon_0\E\) in the impressed-source slot
\cite{stratton1941}. This is the operational meaning of
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.source-admissibility}: the source tuple must lie on the
charge-continuous manifold before angular transport diagnostics are applied.

\paragraph{Worked problem: rectangular waveguide TE\(_{10}\) boundary closure.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[thick] (-1.2,-0.7) rectangle (1.2,0.7);
  \draw[blue!60, thick] plot[domain=-1.1:1.1,samples=40] (-1.1+2.2*\x/2.2,{0.55*sin(180*\x/2.2)});
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above] at (0,0.75) {TE$_{10}$};
  \draw[ch1flow, ->] (-1.5,0) -- (-1.2,0);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, left] at (-1.5,0) {$\Jimp$ feed};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-0.85) {$a\times b$, PEC};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: TE\(_{10}\) rectangular guide with PEC sidewalls and charge-compatible feed aperture.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-te10-waveguide}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-te10-waveguide} warns against applying radiative transport diagnostics below cutoff: evanescent guide fields remain storage-dominated until Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.radiation-trace-condition} is revisited.
Consider a hollow rectangular guide with cross section \(a\times b\) and PEC walls.
The dominant TE\(_{10}\) mode has
\(\E=E_0\sin(\pi x/a)\,e^{\jj\beta z}\hat{\mathbf{y}}\) with cutoff
\(\omega_c=\pi c/a\) and propagation constant
\(\beta=\sqrt{k_0^2-(\pi/a)^2}\) for \(k_0>\pi/a\). Tangential \(\E\) vanishes on
\(x=0,a\) and \(y=0,b\), satisfying Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.outer-boundary-classes} on
\(\Gamma_{\mathrm{PEC}}\). The impressed current that launches this mode is
confined to the feed aperture at \(z=0\); with \(\Jimp\in L^2(\Omega_s)^3\) and
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.global-charge-compatibility} enforced at the feed, the data tuple
belongs to \(\mathbb{A}_\omega\). At \(f=10\,\mathrm{GHz}\) with \(a=2.3\,\mathrm{cm}\),
\(\omega_c\approx 6.5\,\mathrm{GHz}\) and the mode propagates with
\(\beta\approx 0.79\,k_0\). A student who applies angular-transport diagnostics to
the evanescent field below cutoff without revisiting
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.radiation-trace-condition} confuses storage-dominated guide
physics with radiative export \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Bridge to well-posedness.}
Source and boundary admissibility specify \emph{what data} may enter Maxwell
theory; they do not yet guarantee that those data select a \emph{unique} field
state. Without uniqueness, two distinct field pairs could produce identical power
through an aperture yet different torque about the same axis, destroying the
identifiability required for angular transport claims. Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.3}
closes that logical gap before conservation laws are derived in
Subsections~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4} and~\ref{sec:ch01.1.5} \cite{stratton1941}.


Collin's guided-wave theory \cite{stratton1941} and Stratton's boundary-value
treatment \cite{stratton1941} both stress that Maxwell equations in the volume fix
the field only up to a compatible boundary supplement. A perfectly conducting wall
removes tangential electric field; an impedance sheet couples tangential \(\E\) and
\(\H\); an open radiation boundary selects outgoing rather than incoming spherical
waves. Each choice alters which angular modes are trapped and which can export flux
through \(\Gamma\). The data tuple Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.data-specification-tuple}
lists every channel---volume \(\Jimp\), surface \(\mathbf{J}_{s,ab}\), interface
\(\rho_{s,ab}\), and outer trace \(\mathbf{g}_\Gamma\)---so that no excitation
path is left implicit.

Interface conditions Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.interface-conditions} follow from the same
integral identities Maxwell \cite{maxwell1873} wrote for pillboxes and Amperian
loops. The tangential \(\E\) continuity row states that electric potential remains
single-valued across a neutral interface without magnetic sheet current. The
tangential \(\H\) jump equals \(\mathbf{J}_{s,ab}\), encoding how surface current
sources magnetic circulation. Normal \(\mathbf{D}\) and \(\mathbf{B}\) jumps tie
to surface charge and enforce absence of magnetic monopoles. For angular transport,
the critical row is often the tangential \(\H\) jump: an azimuthally varying
\(\mathbf{J}_{s,ab}\) can inject orbital-like structure at an interface even when
volume \(\Jimp\) is symmetric \cite{stratton1941}.

The radiation trace Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.radiation-trace-condition} deserves a
physical reading in SI units. The left side is tangential magnetic field minus an
electric-field term scaled by \(\eta_0^{-1}\); the right side vanishes as
\(R\to\infty\) for outgoing fields. Violating this condition is equivalent to
injecting incoming energy from infinity, which corrupts both
\(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) and \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) integrals in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}.
Near-field scanners that truncate the domain too close to the source must replace
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.radiation-trace-condition} with an absorbing boundary whose
reflection coefficient is small enough that \(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{adm}}\) in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissibility-residual} remains below tolerance
\cite{stratton1941}.

Power dissipated on an impedance boundary \(\Gamma_Z\) follows from the complex
Poynting theorem of Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4}. The cycle-averaged outward power
density normal to \(\Gamma_Z\) is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.impedance-boundary-power}
P_Z=
\frac{1}{2}\Re\!\left\{
\hat{\mathbf{n}}\cdot\left(\E\times\H^*\right)
\right\}
=\frac{1}{2}\Re\!\left\{
Z_s\left|(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\times\H)\times\hat{\mathbf{n}}\right|^2
\right\},
\end{equation}
where the second equality uses Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.outer-boundary-classes}. When
\(\Re(Z_s)>0\), Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.impedance-boundary-power} is nonnegative and
supports the passive uniqueness route of Proposition~\ref{prop:ch1.uniqueness-passive-closure}.
When \(\Re(Z_s)<0\) for active surfaces, admissibility must be revisited because
energy can be injected through the boundary \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: dielectric half-space with surface charge.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \fill[blue!12] (-2,-1.2) rectangle (2,0);
  \fill[gray!6] (-2,0) rectangle (2,1.2);
  \draw[thick] (-2,0) -- (2,0);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below left] at (-1.5,-0.5) {$\Omega_2$, $\varepsilon_r{=}2.25$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above left] at (-1.5,0.5) {$\Omega_1$ vacuum};
  \draw[->, thick, red!70!black] (0,0.5) -- (0,1.1);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (0.15,0.8) {$\E,\ \Gamma,\ \tau$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: matched dielectric half-space with charge-free interface and transmitted wavenumber \(k_2=k_0\sqrt{\varepsilon_r}\).}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-dielectric-halfspace}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-dielectric-halfspace} verifies Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.global-charge-compatibility} with \(\Jimp=\mathbf{0}\): zero net \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) on \(\Sigma_{12}\) coexists with nonzero reactive \(\Pi_{\mathrm{re}}\) in the interface near zone.
Let \(\Omega_1\) be vacuum and \(\Omega_2\) a semi-infinite dielectric with
relative permittivity \(\varepsilon_r=2.25\), sharing interface
\(\Sigma_{12}\) at \(z=0\). A normally incident plane wave from Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1}
with \(\E=E_0 e^{\jj k_0 z}\hat{\mathbf{x}}\) produces reflected and transmitted
amplitudes \(E_r=\Gamma E_0\), \(E_t=\tau E_0\) with
\(\Gamma=(1-\sqrt{\varepsilon_r})/(1+\sqrt{\varepsilon_r})\) and
\(\tau=1+\Gamma\). No free surface charge sits on \(\Sigma_{12}\), so
\(\rho_{s,12}=0\) and the third row of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.interface-conditions}
holds. Tangential \(\E\) continuity gives \(E_0(1+\Gamma)=E_t\), and the
\(\H\) jump with \(\mathbf{J}_{s,12}=\mathbf{0}\) fixes the transmitted wave
vector in \(\Omega_2\) as \(k_2=k_0\sqrt{\varepsilon_r}\). Global charge
compatibility Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.global-charge-compatibility} is satisfied with
\(\Jimp=\mathbf{0}\). Angular transport on \(\Sigma_{12}\) vanishes because the
incident wave carries no net \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\), yet local \(\Pi_{\mathrm{re}}\)
is nonzero in the reactive near zone of the interface, previewing the regime split
of Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.4} \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Validity of the admissible-data manifold.}
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-data-class} presumes piecewise smooth interfaces,
local linear constitutive laws, and trace spaces compatible with
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.boundary-data-operator}. Rough surfaces modeled by effective
impedance, stochastic boundary operators, and nonlinear surface plasmon response
require enriched data classes before torque integrals inherit the same conservation
interpretation \cite{stratton1941}. Within the stated class,
\(\mathbb{A}_\omega\) is the manifold on which uniqueness, Poynting balance, and
angular transport claims remain mutually consistent \cite{maxwell1873}.


\subsection{Initial-boundary value problems and uniqueness}
\label{sec:ch01.1.3}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Initial-boundary specification and solution uniqueness are distinct questions
that must be joined before transport claims become identifiable. An
\emph{initial-boundary value problem} specifies both time initialization and
boundary trace class for Maxwell evolution. \emph{Uniqueness} asks whether one
admissible dataset can generate more than one electromagnetic state. Without
uniqueness, subsequent statements on power, momentum, and angular transport lose
physical identifiability \cite{stratton1941}.

Start from the time-domain formulation in a region \(\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^3\)
with boundary \(\Gamma\). Let \(\E(\mathbf{r},t)\), \(\H(\mathbf{r},t)\) be real
fields, \(\mathbf{D}\), \(\mathbf{B}\) flux densities, \(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{imp}}\)
impressed source, and \(\rho\) free-charge density:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.ibvp-time-domain}
\begin{aligned}
\curl \E(\mathbf{r},t)&=-\partial_t\mathbf{B}(\mathbf{r},t),\\
\curl \H(\mathbf{r},t)&=\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{imp}}(\mathbf{r},t)+\partial_t\mathbf{D}(\mathbf{r},t),\\
\divg \mathbf{D}(\mathbf{r},t)&=\rho(\mathbf{r},t),\qquad
\divg \mathbf{B}(\mathbf{r},t)=0.
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}
Initial data are \(\E(\mathbf{r},0)=\E_0(\mathbf{r})\),
\(\H(\mathbf{r},0)=\H_0(\mathbf{r})\), and boundary data are imposed through a
trace operator \(\mathcal{B}_\Gamma\). Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.ibvp-time-domain}
specifies local dynamics, but the pair \((\E_0,\H_0)\) and boundary trace class
determine which trajectory is selected from all formally admissible trajectories
\cite{stratton1941}.

For monochromatic steady operation with factor \(\exp(-\jj\omega t)\), the same
problem reduces to a frequency-domain boundary value problem:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.ibvp-harmonic-bvp}
\begin{aligned}
\curl \E&=-\jj\omega\mathbf{B},\qquad
\curl \H=\Jimp+\jj\omega\mathbf{D},\\
\divg \mathbf{D}&=\rho,\qquad
\divg \mathbf{B}=0,\qquad
\mathcal{B}_\Gamma[\E,\H]=\mathbf{g}_\Gamma.
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}
Hence, in the phasor regime, initial data enter only through consistency with the
selected harmonic branch; boundary traces and constitutive model then govern
solvability and uniqueness for each \(\omega\) \cite{stratton1941}.

To formulate uniqueness, consider two solutions
\((\E_1,\H_1)\), \((\E_2,\H_2)\) for the same dataset
\((\Jimp,\rho,\mathcal{B}_\Gamma,\mathbf{g}_\Gamma)\), and define
\(\Delta\E=\E_1-\E_2\), \(\Delta\H=\H_1-\H_2\). The difference fields satisfy the
homogeneous system
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.uniqueness-difference-system}
\begin{aligned}
\curl \Delta\E&=-\jj\omega\mu\,\Delta\H,\qquad
\curl \Delta\H=\jj\omega\varepsilon\,\Delta\E,\\
\divg(\varepsilon\Delta\E)&=0,\qquad
\divg(\mu\Delta\H)=0,\qquad
\mathcal{B}_\Gamma[\Delta\E,\Delta\H]=\mathbf{0},
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}
for constitutive parameters \(\varepsilon,\mu\) in the chosen model class. This
homogeneous reduction is the precise mathematical content of uniqueness testing
\cite{stratton1941}.

The uniqueness statement is therefore
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.uniqueness-null}
\mathcal{B}_{\chi,\Gamma}[\Delta\E,\Delta\H]=0
\Longrightarrow
\Delta\E=\mathbf{0},\ \Delta\H=\mathbf{0},
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal{B}_{\chi,\Gamma}\) denotes Maxwell operator with constitutive set
\(\chi\) and boundary class \(\Gamma\) fixed. Left side means zero residual under
homogeneous forcing; right side means trivial null branch only. Equation
\eqref{eq:ch1.uniqueness-null} is therefore a null-space condition on the
homogeneous operator, and physically it states that fixed data define one field
state rather than a family of hidden alternatives.

An energy identity for the difference state provides the key proof channel. Form
the complex Poynting vector
\(\mathbf{S}_\Delta=\tfrac12\Delta\E\times\Delta\H^{*}\), integrate over
\(\Omega\), and use divergence theorem:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.uniqueness-energy-identity}
\int_{\Gamma}\hat{\mathbf{n}}\cdot\mathbf{S}_\Delta\,dS
=-\frac12\int_{\Omega}\Delta\E\cdot\Delta\Jimp^{*}\,dV
-\jj\omega\!\int_{\Omega}\!\left(u_{\Delta H}-u_{\Delta E}\right)dV,
\end{equation}
where \(u_{\Delta E}=\tfrac14\varepsilon|\Delta\E|^{2}\),
\(u_{\Delta H}=\tfrac14\mu|\Delta\H|^{2}\), and \(\Delta\Jimp=\mathbf{0}\) for the
homogeneous problem. Boundary classes determine the real part of the surface term:
for passive traces it is nonnegative outgoing dissipation or radiation, while for
ideal lossless closed boundaries it vanishes \cite{stratton1941}.

A sufficient coercive estimate is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.uniqueness-estimate}
\Re\!\left\langle
\mathcal{B}_{\chi,\Gamma}[\Delta\E,\Delta\H],(\Delta\E,\Delta\H)
\right\rangle
\ge c_{\chi,\Gamma}
\left(\|\Delta\E\|_{L^2(\Omega)^3}^{2}+\|\Delta\H\|_{L^2(\Omega)^3}^{2}\right),
\end{equation}
with coercivity constant \(c_{\chi,\Gamma}>0\). If the residual is zero, the left
side is zero; positivity of \(c_{\chi,\Gamma}\) forces both norms to vanish, hence
\(\Delta\E=\Delta\H=\mathbf{0}\). Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.uniqueness-estimate}
therefore converts analytical coercivity into physical identifiability.

\begin{proposition}[Uniqueness under passive admissible closure]
\label{prop:ch1.uniqueness-passive-closure}
Assume: (i) admissible source-boundary data from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-data-class}; (ii) passive constitutive response
\(\Re(\varepsilon)\ge 0\), \(\Re(\mu)\ge 0\) in \(\Omega\); (iii) boundary class
with nonnegative real power flux in the homogeneous difference problem. Then the
solution of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.ibvp-harmonic-bvp} is unique.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
Let \(\mathcal{F}_1=(\E_1,\H_1)\) and \(\mathcal{F}_2=(\E_2,\H_2)\) be two solutions
for the same data tuple. Set \(\Delta\E=\E_1-\E_2\), \(\Delta\H=\H_1-\H_2\). By
linearity, Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.uniqueness-difference-system} holds with
\(\Delta\Jimp=\mathbf{0}\). Insert \(\mathbf{S}_\Delta=\tfrac12\Delta\E\times\Delta\H^*\)
into Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.uniqueness-energy-identity}. Passivity and the stated boundary
class force \(\Re\int_\Gamma\hat{\mathbf{n}}\cdot\mathbf{S}_\Delta\,dS\ge 0\) with
zero forcing, hence \(\Delta\E=\Delta\H=\mathbf{0}\) \cite{stratton1941}.
\end{proof}

Uniqueness is frequency dependent because the homogeneous operator spectrum depends
on \(\omega\). Near resonant or poorly damped eigenfrequencies, null-space
amplification can occur unless radiation, loss, or additional constraints suppress
nontrivial homogeneous modes \cite{stratton1941}. A
practical indicator is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.uniqueness-condition-index}
\kappa_{\mathrm{uniq}}(\omega)=
\frac{1}{\inf_{\|(\mathbf{u},\mathbf{v})\|=1}
\|\mathcal{B}_{\chi,\Gamma,\omega}[\mathbf{u},\mathbf{v}]\|},
\end{equation}
where large \(\kappa_{\mathrm{uniq}}(\omega)\) indicates weak coercivity and
increased sensitivity to modeling and discretization errors.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=9mm and 11mm]
  \node[ch1box] (ibvp) {IBVP data\\$(\E_0,\H_0)$, $\chi$, $\mathcal{B}_\Gamma$};
  \node[ch1box, right=22mm of ibvp] (sol) {Solutions $\mathcal{F}_1,\mathcal{F}_2$};
  \node[ch1box, below=10mm of sol] (diff) {$\Delta\E,\Delta\H$ homogeneous};
  \node[ch1box, below=10mm of diff] (zero) {$\Delta\E=\mathbf{0}$};
  \draw[ch1flow] (ibvp) -- (sol) -- (diff) -- (zero);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Conceptual map from IBVP specification to uniqueness: only when homogeneous difference fields are forced to zero does one dataset produce one physical state.}
\label{fig:ch1.uniqueness-map}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.uniqueness-map} emphasizes a pedagogical point often missed in
first exposure: uniqueness is not asserted directly from Maxwell equations alone,
but from Maxwell equations plus admissible constitutive and boundary closure. The
difference-system route is therefore central rather than optional.

Within the admitted class, however, the initial-boundary formulation and
uniqueness criterion guarantee that one admissible dataset corresponds to one
physically interpretable electromagnetic state. Strong gain, ill-conditioned
discretizations, or non-selfadjoint inverse closures can still produce multiple
numerical branches; those cases require augmented analysis beyond passive coercivity
\cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: PEC cavity uniqueness.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[thick] (-1.3,-0.8) rectangle (1.3,0.8);
  \draw[blue!50, dashed] (-1.1,-0.6) -- (1.1,0.6);
  \draw[blue!50, dashed] (-1.1,0.6) -- (1.1,-0.6);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-0.95) {PEC $(m,n,p)$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above right] at (0.9,0.5) {$\Delta\E\to\mathbf{0}$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: passive PEC cavity with fixed mode triplet \((m,n,p)\) and uniqueness via the energy identity.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-pec-cavity-uniqueness}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-pec-cavity-uniqueness} closes with Proposition~\ref{prop:ch1.uniqueness-passive-closure}: visual nodal structure does not imply ambiguity once frequency and \((m,n,p)\) are pinned.
Consider a rectangular cavity \(\Omega=[0,a]\times[0,b]\times[0,c]\) with perfectly
conducting walls (\(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\times\E=\mathbf{0}\)) and no impressed sources
(\(\Jimp=\mathbf{0}\), \(\rho=0\)). Seek time-harmonic modes
\(\E(\mathbf{r})=\mathbf{E}_{mnp}\sin(k_xx)\sin(k_yy)\sin(k_zz)\) satisfying
\(\nabla^2\E+k_0^2\E=\mathbf{0}\) with \(k_x= m\pi/a\), \(k_y=n\pi/b\),
\(k_z=p\pi/c\). Uniqueness of the excited branch at fixed \((m,n,p)\) follows from
Proposition~\ref{prop:ch1.uniqueness-passive-closure}: subtract two candidate
solutions to form \(\Delta\E\), apply
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.uniqueness-energy-identity}, and note that passive PEC walls make
\(\Re\int_{\Gamma}\hat{\mathbf{n}}\cdot\mathbf{S}_\Delta\,dS\ge 0\) while the
volume source term vanishes. The only compatible homogeneous branch is
\(\Delta\E=\mathbf{0}\) \cite{stratton1941}.

For the lowest nonzero TM mode \(m=n=1\), \(p=0\), the eigenfrequency follows from
\(k_0^2=k_x^2+k_y^2=(\pi/a)^2+(\pi/b)^2\), hence
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.worked-cavity-frequency}
f_{110}=\frac{c}{2\pi}\sqrt{\left(\frac{\pi}{a}\right)^2+\left(\frac{\pi}{b}\right)^2}.
\end{equation}
With \(a=b=0.1\,\mathrm{m}\), Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-cavity-frequency} gives
\(f_{110}\approx 2.12\,\mathrm{GHz}\). The corresponding field
\(\mathbf{E}_{110}=E_0\sin(\pi x/a)\sin(\pi y/b)\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) has vanishing
normal component on all PEC walls, so the boundary operator in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.boundary-data-operator} is satisfied. Applying
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.uniqueness-energy-identity} to the difference field
\(\Delta\E=\E^{(1)}-\E^{(2)}\) at fixed excitation frequency yields
\[
\int_\Omega \Re\!\left\{\Delta\E\cdot\curl\Delta\H^*\right\}dV
+\Re\int_{\Gamma_{\mathrm{PEC}}}\hat{\mathbf{n}}\cdot\mathbf{S}_\Delta\,dS=0.
\]
The volume integral vanishes for homogeneous cavity modes, and passive PEC walls
make the surface term nonnegative, forcing \(\Delta\E=\mathbf{0}\). A student
reading only the mode shape might confuse visual nodal structure with ambiguity;
the energy identity shows that, once \((m,n,p)\) and frequency are fixed, no
second physically distinct solution exists in the admitted class
\cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: two PEC solutions with identical aperture power.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \node[ch1box] (f1) {$\E^{(1)}$};
  \node[ch1box, right=16mm of f1] (f2) {$\E^{(2)}$};
  \node[ch1box, below=10mm of f1] (p) {equal aperture power};
  \draw[ch1flow] (f1) -- (p);
  \draw[ch1flow] (f2) -- (p);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below=11mm of p] {$\Delta\E=\E^{(1)}-\E^{(2)}\to\mathbf{0}$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: uniqueness layer preventing degenerate modal superpositions from masquerading as power-meter ambiguity.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-pec-identical-power}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-pec-identical-power} applies Proposition~\ref{prop:ch1.uniqueness-passive-closure}: identical aperture power does not certify distinct physical branches once the excitation vector selects a single admissible solution.
On a closed PEC cavity, suppose two distinct eigenmodes \(\E^{(1)}\) and
\(\E^{(2)}\) at the same frequency are both mathematically constructible from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.ibvp-harmonic-bvp} but only one is selected by the feed
geometry. Without the uniqueness argument of Proposition~\ref{prop:ch1.uniqueness-passive-closure},
a power meter on a small coupling aperture could report identical
\(\int_S\Re(\mathbf{S}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}})\,dS\) for both branches while torque
about \(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) differs by modal parity. The difference field
\(\Delta\E=\E^{(1)}-\E^{(2)}\) satisfies homogeneous Maxwell equations; passive PEC
walls force \(\Re\int_\Gamma\hat{\mathbf{n}}\cdot\mathbf{S}_\Delta\,dS\ge 0\) in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.uniqueness-energy-identity}, collapsing \(\Delta\E\) to zero once
the excitation vector is fixed. Uniqueness is therefore the identifiability layer
that prevents degenerate modal superpositions from masquerading as measurement
ambiguity \cite{maxwell1873,stratton1941}.

Uniqueness theory for Maxwell systems matured alongside cavity and waveguide
analysis in Stratton \cite{stratton1941} and Harrington \cite{stratton1941}.
The physical stake is identifiability: if two distinct field pairs satisfy the same
sources and boundaries, power meters and torque sensors lose their claim to measure
a unique electromagnetic state. Maxwell's equations are linear, so the difference
of two solutions satisfies the homogeneous system
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.uniqueness-difference-system}; uniqueness is the statement that
this homogeneous branch admits only the zero solution under the chosen boundary class.

The energy identity Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.uniqueness-energy-identity} exposes how
boundary conditions enter the proof. The surface integral of
\(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\cdot\mathbf{S}_\Delta\) is the cycle-averaged power carried by
difference fields across \(\Gamma\). On passive outgoing boundaries, its real part
is nonnegative, representing radiation or dissipation of difference energy. On PEC
walls, tangential \(\Delta\E=\mathbf{0}\) forces the Poynting contribution to
vanish. With homogeneous sources, the volume term
\(-\jj\omega\int(u_{\Delta H}-u_{\Delta E})\,dV\) is purely reactive exchange between
electric and magnetic storage channels of the difference field. Coercivity
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.uniqueness-estimate} converts this balance into a norm bound:
zero residual under the operator implies zero field \cite{stratton1941}.

Near resonances, uniqueness weakens unless loss or radiation suppresses homogeneous
modes. Define the resonance proximity factor
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.uniqueness-resonance-factor}
\nu_{\mathrm{res}}(\omega)=
\frac{
\left|k_0(\omega)-k_{0,\mathrm{res}}\right|
}{
\Delta k_{\mathrm{loss}}
+\epsilon_\nu
},
\end{equation}
where \(k_{0,\mathrm{res}}\) is a cavity or scatterer resonance wavenumber and
\(\Delta k_{\mathrm{loss}}\) is the linewidth from material or radiation loss.
When \(\nu_{\mathrm{res}}\ll 1\), \(\kappa_{\mathrm{uniq}}\) in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.uniqueness-condition-index} grows and numerical solvers may
return multiple nearly degenerate branches unless frequency is pinned and loss is
explicit \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: weakly damped cavity near resonance.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[thick] (-1.2,-0.7) rectangle (1.2,0.7);
  \draw[red!70!black, thick] (0.9,0) -- (1.2,0.25) -- (1.2,-0.25) -- cycle;
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (1.35,0) {leak $A$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above] at (0,0.85) {$f\approx f_{110}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-0.95) {$\nu_{\mathrm{res}}\approx 0.03$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: high uniqueness-condition index near a weakly damped cavity resonance with aperture leak.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-weakly-damped-cavity}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-weakly-damped-cavity} requires reporting \(\nu_{\mathrm{res}}\) alongside torque: when \(\kappa_{\mathrm{uniq}}\) exceeds \(30\), angular torque reports inherit the same \(10^{-2}\) branch ambiguity unless outgoing aperture closure is enforced.
Revisit the rectangular cavity of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-cavity-frequency} with
\(a=b=0.1\,\mathrm{m}\) and \(f_{110}\approx 2.12\,\mathrm{GHz}\). Introduce a
small radiation leak through an aperture of area \(A=10^{-4}\,\mathrm{m^2}\) so
that \(\Delta k_{\mathrm{loss}}\approx 2\pi\times 10^6\,\mathrm{rad/s}\). At
\(f=2.119\,\mathrm{GHz}\), \(\nu_{\mathrm{res}}\approx 0.03\) and
\(\kappa_{\mathrm{uniq}}\) exceeds \(30\). Two numerical meshes with slightly
different aperture discretizations can then produce \(\Delta\E\) with
\(\|\Delta\E\|/\|\E\|\sim 10^{-2}\) even though both satisfy
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.ibvp-harmonic-bvp} to within tolerance. Angular torque reports
computed from such near-degenerate pairs differ by the same fraction. The remedy
is to report \(\nu_{\mathrm{res}}\) alongside torque and to enforce outgoing
closure on the aperture so that Proposition~\ref{prop:ch1.uniqueness-passive-closure}
applies \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Validity of the uniqueness layer.}
Passive coercivity fails for active media with net gain, for perfectly lossless
closed cavities at exact eigenfrequencies, and for ill-posed inverse problems where
\(\mathcal{B}_\Gamma\) is under-specified \cite{stratton1941}. Within
the admissible passive class of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissible-data-class},
uniqueness guarantees that conservation integrals in Subsections~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4}
and~\ref{sec:ch01.1.5} refer to a single field branch, which is prerequisite for
any angular transport diagnostic in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} \cite{maxwell1873}.


\subsection{Energy, linear momentum, and Maxwell stress tensor}
\label{sec:ch01.1.4}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Subsections~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1}--\ref{sec:ch01.1.3} fixed the Maxwell system, source
and boundary admissibility, and uniqueness of the boundary-value map. The
cycle-averaged energy and momentum budgets developed below turn those field
solutions into flux quantities measurable on finite surfaces. Energy (storage and
outward flow), linear momentum (directed mechanical action carried by the field),
and the Maxwell stress tensor (the local bilinear map that converts field
intensity to traction on a surface) enter as one coupled derivation. Angular-momentum
arguments in Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.5} inherit \(\mathbf{T}\) from here by
reference; they do not introduce an independent mechanical postulate
\cite{stratton1941}. In the
\(\exp(-\jj\omega t)\) phasor convention, define
\(\mathbf{S}=\tfrac{1}{2}\E\times\H^*\), where \((\cdot)^*\) is complex
conjugation. Define stored-energy densities
\(u_E=\tfrac{1}{4}\varepsilon_0\E\cdot\E^*\) and
\(u_H=\tfrac{1}{4}\mu_0\H\cdot\H^*\). The complex power balance is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.poynting-complex}
\divg\mathbf{S}
=-\frac{1}{2}\E\cdot\Jimp^*
-\jj\omega\left(u_H-u_E\right).
\end{equation}
Term-level interpretation is explicit. The divergence term gives net power outflow
density from a differential control volume. The source term
\(\E\cdot\Jimp^*/2\) is electromagnetic work rate on impressed current. The term
\(-\jj\omega(u_H-u_E)\) is reactive exchange between magnetic and electric storage
channels. Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-complex} therefore couples transport,
source work, and storage imbalance in one local conservation statement.

Poynting's original energy-flux vector \cite{poynting1884} predates the complex
phasor form but already encoded the central idea: electromagnetic energy moves
through space in a direction determined by the cross product of field intensities.
The cycle-averaged generalization \(\mathbf{S}=\tfrac12\E\times\H^*\) used here is
the harmonic specialization standard in time-harmonic field theory
\cite{stratton1941}. When the same fields interact with matter,
momentum bookkeeping acquires additional subtlety: Minkowski's symmetric
energy-momentum tensor for material media \cite{minkowski1908} and Abraham's
alternative stress form disagree on how much momentum is stored in a dielectric,
yet both reduce to the vacuum Maxwell stress in source-free exterior regions
where this chapter's angular-transport criteria are evaluated
\cite{stratton1941}. Volume~I therefore develops transport on
vacuum-side control surfaces first; material-specific momentum splits enter through
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.5.1} only where detectors and absorbers require them.

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.poynting-real-imag-split}
\begin{aligned}
\divg\Re(\mathbf{S})&=-\frac12\Re\!\left(\E\cdot\Jimp^*\right),\\
\divg\Im(\mathbf{S})&=-\frac12\Im\!\left(\E\cdot\Jimp^*\right)-\omega(u_H-u_E).
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-real-imag-split} separates transported average
power from reactive storage exchange under one harmonic field state.

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.power-balance-integral}
\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{S}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
=-\frac12\int_V\E\cdot\Jimp^*\,dV
-\jj\omega\int_V(u_H-u_E)\,dV.
\end{equation}
This control-volume relation provides direct finite-set interpretation for probe
and aperture measurements.

Splitting into real and imaginary parts gives the measurement-relevant separation:
\(\Re(\mathbf{S})\) controls net transported power density and
\(\Im(\mathbf{S})\) controls reactive circulation associated with local
near-field energy exchange. This distinction prevents reactive phase structure from
being misread as long-range momentum transport.

Linear momentum flux is represented through the time-averaged Maxwell stress
tensor. In vacuum its dyadic form is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress}
\mathbf{T}
=\frac{1}{2}\Re\!\left[
\varepsilon_0\,\E\E^*
+\mu_0\,\H\H^*
-\frac{1}{2}\mathbf{I}
\left(\varepsilon_0\,\E\cdot\E^*+\mu_0\,\H\cdot\H^*\right)
\right],
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{I}\) is the identity dyad and juxtaposition such as \(\E\E^*\)
is dyadic product. Operand by operand, the first two dyads are directional field
intensity contributions, while the isotropic subtraction term
\(\tfrac{1}{2}\mathbf{I}(\cdots)\) removes trace pressure so that directional
tractions are represented correctly. The real-part operator keeps physically
observable time-averaged transfer.

As a complete object, Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} maps electromagnetic
field state to mechanical traction on material control surfaces. For unit normal
\(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\), the traction density is
\(\mathbf{t}=\mathbf{T}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\), and the net force on closed
surface \(\partial V\) is \(\mathbf{F}=\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{T}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS\).
No new numbered equation is introduced for these definitions because they are
direct functional uses of the already-established stress tensor.

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.linear-momentum-balance}
\divg\mathbf{T}+\mathbf{f}_{\mathrm{em}}=\mathbf{0},
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{f}_{\mathrm{em}}\) denotes the time-averaged force density
delivered by the field to matter. This equation is the local momentum-balance
counterpart of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-complex}.

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.energy-momentum-directional-ratio}
\Pi_{\hat{\mathbf{q}}}
:=
\frac{\hat{\mathbf{q}}\cdot\Re(\mathbf{S})}
     {c\,\hat{\mathbf{q}}\cdot(\mathbf{T}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{q}})+\epsilon_\Pi},
\end{equation}
for unit probe direction \(\hat{\mathbf{q}}\), vacuum speed \(c\), and fixed
nondimensional regularizer \(\epsilon_\Pi>0\). \(\Pi_{\hat{\mathbf{q}}}\) quantifies
directional agreement between energetic and mechanical transport channels.

\begin{proposition}[Energy-momentum consistency under passive closure]
\label{prop:ch1.energy-momentum-consistency}
Assume admissible data \(\mathfrak{D}\in\mathbb{A}_\omega\), passive boundary
closure, and vacuum constitutive law. Then finite exported real power through
\(\partial V\) implies finite integrated electromagnetic traction on \(\partial V\),
with both channels determined by the same solution branch \((\E,\H)\).
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-complex} and Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} are
quadratic forms of \((\E,\H)\). Under admissibility and passivity, field norms are
finite in the control-volume class. Therefore both surface integrals remain finite,
and both are consistent descriptions of transport generated by one field state.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[thick] (-1.5,-1) rectangle (1.5,1);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above] at (0,1.05) {$\partial V$};
  \draw[->, teal!70!black, thick] (0,0.3) -- (0,1) node[right, ch1lblout] {$\mathbf{S}$};
  \draw[->, orange!85!black, thick] (-0.8,0) -- (-1.3,0) node[above, ch1lblout] {$\mathbf{T}\!\cdot\!\hat{\mathbf{n}}$};
  \fill[red!50] (0,0) circle (2pt);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-0.2) {$\Jimp$ work};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Control-volume relation between complex power balance and linear-momentum traction flux.}
\label{fig:ch1.energy-momentum-control}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.energy-momentum-control} links the two balance channels on the
same control set: \(\mathbf{S}\) governs energetic exchange and \(\mathbf{T}\)
governs mechanical traction exchange. Reading them together is essential when field
patterns exhibit strong spatial structure, because power circulation and momentum
transfer need not be co-located.

Equations~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-complex} and
\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} presuppose harmonic averaging in passive media with
local \(\varepsilon,\mu\). Dispersive, anisotropic, or strongly time-varying
constitutive laws modify both storage terms and traction maps; active boundaries
can inject net gain that invalidates the passive-balance route used in
Proposition~\ref{prop:ch1.uniqueness-passive-closure}
\cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: momentum flux through transverse versus closed surfaces.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,0) -- (0,2);
  \draw[fill=gray!10] (0,0) circle (0.55);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (0.6,0) {disk: $\mathbf{t}=\mathbf{0}$};
  \draw[ch1shell, dashed] (0,0) arc (180:0:1.2);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above] at (0,1.25) {cap: $\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{em}}/c^2$};
  \draw[->, thick] (0,0.2) -- (0,1.5) node[right, ch1lblout] {$\mathbf{S}\parallel\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: identical fields yielding zero normal traction on a transverse disk but nonzero momentum flux through an enclosing cap.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-transverse-vs-closed}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-transverse-vs-closed} encodes the geometry dependence of momentum reports: Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-aperture-power} counts power through the disk, while normal traction vanishes because \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\parallel\hat{\mathbf{z}}\parallel\) propagation.
Using the uniform plane-wave solution established in
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1}, take \(\mathbf{S}=(|E_0|^2/2\eta_0)\hat{\mathbf{z}}\)
and evaluate power through a disk of radius \(a\) normal to \(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\):
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.worked-aperture-power}
P(a)=\int_0^{2\pi}\!\!\int_0^a \Re(\mathbf{S}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{z}})\,\rho\,d\rho\,d\phi
=\frac{\pi a^2 |E_0|^2}{2\eta_0}.
\end{equation}
The Maxwell stress of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} gives traction
\(\mathbf{t}=\mathbf{T}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{z}}=\mathbf{0}\) on the transverse disk:
no normal momentum flux through an aperture whose normal is parallel to propagation.
On a hemispherical cap enclosing the same beam, integrated traction is nonzero and
equals the intercepted fraction of \(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{em}}/c^2\)
\cite{poynting1884}. Momentum reports therefore require
explicit surface geometry---identical \(\E,\H\) yield different integrated traction
on transverse, oblique, or closed surfaces \cite{stratton1941}.

The Maxwell stress tensor Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} inherits a long
measurement history. Poynting \cite{poynting1884} identified energy flow before a
symmetric stress tensor was standard; Minkowski \cite{minkowski1908} and Abraham
\cite{abraham1910} later debated how material bodies translate field momentum into
mechanical force, a controversy that persists in dielectric waveguide theory. For
vacuum-side control surfaces used in this chapter, the Abraham and Minkowski forms
coincide with Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress}, so linear momentum flux and
traction are unambiguous once \((\E,\H)\) are fixed by the admissible boundary-value
problem \cite{stratton1941}. Stratton's treatment \cite{stratton1941}
remains the canonical reference for converting dyadic products \(\E\E^*\) and
\(\H\H^*\) into measurable surface forces.

The trace of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} equals the cycle-averaged electromagnetic
pressure intensity:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.stress-trace-pressure}
\operatorname{Tr}\mathbf{T}
=-\frac{1}{4}\left(\varepsilon_0\|\E\|^2+\mu_0\|\H\|^2\right)
=-\frac{1}{2}\left(u_E+u_H\right),
\end{equation}
where \(u_E,u_H\) are the phasor energy densities defined below
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-complex}. Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.stress-trace-pressure}
shows that isotropic pressure is tied to total stored energy density, while
directional traction comes from the traceless part of the dyadic. In a uniform
plane wave from Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1}, the traceless and trace parts
combine so that radiation pressure on a perfectly absorbing surface equals
\(2P/cA\) in the propagation direction \cite{poynting1884}.

Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-complex} admits a term-by-term harmonic reading.
The divergence of \(\mathbf{S}\) measures net power leaving an infinitesimal volume.
The source term \(-\tfrac12\E\cdot\Jimp^*\) is the complex conjugate work done on
impressed current; its real part is dissipated or radiated power, its imaginary part
reactive exchange with the source. The storage term \(-\jj\omega(u_H-u_E)\) captures
oscillation between electric and magnetic reservoirs without implying net outward
transport. Reactive dominance in this term is precisely what Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.4}
will quantify through \(\chi(r)\) \cite{chu1948}.

The directional ratio Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.energy-momentum-directional-ratio} compares
Poynting flux projected on \(\hat{\mathbf{q}}\) with normal traction on the same
direction. For propagating vacuum waves aligned with \(\hat{\mathbf{q}}\),
\(\Pi_{\hat{\mathbf{q}}}\approx 1/c\) up to the regularizer \(\epsilon_\Pi\), reflecting
the standard \(S/c^2\) momentum density relation. Deviations from unity flag regimes
where energy and momentum flux are not co-directed---common in reactive near zones
where \(\Im(\mathbf{S})\) is large \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: reactive sphere versus radiative shell.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1src] (0,0) circle (2pt);
  \draw[ch1shellO, dotted] (0,0) circle (0.5);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (0.5,0) {$kr{=}0.5$};
  \draw[ch1shell] (0,0) circle (1.0);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (1.0,0.15) {$kr{=}3$};
  \draw[ch1shellB] (0,0) circle (1.55);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (1.55,0.2) {$kr{=}10$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-1.75) {$|Q_{\mathrm{re}}|\gg P_{\mathrm{rad}}$ inner};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: concentric evaluation shells showing reactive storage dominance versus radiative export for a small dipole.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-reactive-vs-radiative}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-reactive-vs-radiative} motivates pairing angular-momentum reports with regime indices: on the \(kr=0.5\) shell, oscillatory traction does not integrate to steady force proportional to \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}/c\).
Consider a small electric dipole at \(ka=0.5\) and evaluate fields on concentric
spheres at \(kr=0.5,3,10\). On the inner sphere, Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-real-imag-split}
gives \(\int_{4\pi}\Re(\mathbf{S}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}})\,dS=P_{\mathrm{rad}}\ll
\int_{4\pi}|\Im(\mathbf{S}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}})|\,dS=|Q_{\mathrm{re}}|\), so local
power balance is dominated by storage exchange. The Maxwell stress traction on a
small patch is oscillatory and does not integrate to a steady force proportional to
\(P_{\mathrm{rad}}/c\). On the \(kr=10\) shell, \(\Re(\mathbf{S})\) dominates,
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-aperture-power} applies to the plane-wave sector, and
\(\Pi_{\hat{\mathbf{q}}}\) approaches the vacuum ratio. This contrast previews why
angular-momentum reports must carry regime indices alongside stress integrals
\cite{stratton1941,chu1948}.

\paragraph{Validity of the energy-momentum pair.}
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-complex} and~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} assume
harmonic time averaging, local linear constitutive laws, and passive boundaries
unless gain is explicitly modeled. Dispersive media require frequency derivatives of
\(\varepsilon,\mu\) in the storage terms; pulsed excitation requires time-domain
Poynting before cycle averaging \cite{stratton1941}.
Within the admitted harmonic vacuum class, the pair supplies the energetic and
mechanical flux channels that Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.5} rotates into angular
form \cite{minkowski1908,maxwell1873}.


\subsection{Angular momentum conservation and torque balance}
\label{sec:ch01.1.5}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Angular structure becomes a transport claim only when rotational flux and mechanical
torque can be balanced on the same control volume. Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4}
supplied \(\mathbf{T}\) and the linear-momentum budget; the present subsection
supplies the rotational analogue: angular-momentum density \(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\),
angular-flux tensor \(\mathbf{M}=\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{T}\), and the torque channel
through which field rotational content couples to matter. Every directional transport
test in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}, every covariance shift in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.3},
and every torque measurement model in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.5} cites
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-momentum-balance} and
\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral} rather than re-deriving them
\cite{stratton1941}.

Angular-momentum conservation and torque balance are coupled requirements.
Conservation means that rotational field storage and rotational flux obey a local
balance law derived from Maxwell dynamics. Torque balance means that any net
change in field angular momentum is accounted by mechanical torque delivered to
matter. Without this pair, visual phase winding cannot be interpreted as physical
rotational transport \cite{poynting1884,belinfante1940}.

Let \(\mathbf{r}\) be position vector measured from a declared origin
\(\mathbf{r}_0\), let \(\mathbf{T}\) be Maxwell stress tensor from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress}, and define angular-flux tensor
\(\mathbf{M}=\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{T}\). The local rotational balance law is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.angular-momentum-balance}
\frac{\partial\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}}{\partial t}
+\divg\mathbf{M}
=-\boldsymbol{\tau}_{\mathrm{mech}},
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\) is electromagnetic angular-momentum density and
\(\boldsymbol{\tau}_{\mathrm{mech}}\) is mechanical torque density on matter.
\(\partial_t\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\) tracks local rotational storage variation,
\(\divg\mathbf{M}\) tracks net angular-flux leakage, and the right-hand side is
field-to-matter exchange channel \cite{stratton1941}.

Integrated control-volume form over \(V\subset\Omega\) with boundary
\(\partial V\) and outward normal \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\) is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}
\frac{d}{dt}\int_V \mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV
+\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{M}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
=-\int_V \boldsymbol{\tau}_{\mathrm{mech}}\,dV.
\end{equation}
The first term is stored angular content inside \(V\); the second is exported or
imported angular flux through \(\partial V\); the right term is net mechanical
torque uptake by enclosed matter.

In linear isotropic vacuum, a useful density split is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.angular-density-split}
\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}
=\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{em}}+\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{em}},\qquad
\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{em}}=\varepsilon_0\mu_0\,\Re(\mathbf{S}),
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{em}}\) is linear-momentum density and
\(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{em}}\) is spin-associated local contribution. The first term
is orbital-type contribution about \(\mathbf{r}_0\), while \(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{em}}\)
captures intrinsic polarization-linked contribution under the adopted averaging
framework \cite{stratton1941}.

Belinfante's 1940 field-theoretic analysis \cite{belinfante1940} clarified why such
splits are inevitable rather than optional. Canonical momentum density and kinetic
(symmetric) momentum density differ by the divergence of a spin current; angular
momentum density correspondingly decomposes into an orbital part
\(\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{kin}}\) plus an intrinsic spin part. The
split in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-density-split} is one Maxwellian realization of
that structure for electromagnetic fields, not a separate postulate. Beth's 1936
measurement of torque on a wave plate under circular polarization
\cite{beth1936} provided early experimental support that the spin channel carries
mechanical consequences, while later orbital-indexed beam experiments
\cite{allen1992} extended the vocabulary to spatial phase gradients. Chapter~\ref{ch:01}
unifies these lineages under one conservation law: whichever historical label is
used, closure must follow Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}.

For torque observables about axis \(\hat{\mathbf{u}}\), define
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.torque-observable}
\tau_{\hat{\mathbf{u}}}(V)
=-\hat{\mathbf{u}}\cdot
\left(
\frac{d}{dt}\int_V \mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV
+\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{M}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
\right),
\end{equation}
which is the axis-projected version of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}.
This form makes measurement geometry explicit: torque is a projected transport
functional, not a scalar label inferred from phase pictures.

Directional rotational-transport concentration can be quantified by
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.angular-flux-directional-index}
\xi_J(\theta,\phi;r)
:=
\frac{\left\|\mathbf{M}(r,\theta,\phi)\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}}\right\|}
     {\int_{4\pi}\left\|\mathbf{M}(r,\theta',\phi')\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}}'\right\|\,d\Omega'+\epsilon_J},
\end{equation}
with \(\epsilon_J>0\) fixed nondimensional regularizer. \(\xi_J\) identifies which
solid-angle channels dominate exported angular flux and therefore dominate
direction-selective torque response.

\begin{proposition}[Torque observability criterion]
\label{prop:ch1.torque-observable-criterion}
Consider two admissible states \(\mathcal{F}_1,\mathcal{F}_2\) at common radius
\(r\), with equal total radiated power but different directional angular-flux
densities \(\mathbf{M}_1\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}}\neq
\mathbf{M}_2\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}}\) on a nonzero-measure subset of \(4\pi\). Then
there exists at least one physically admissible interaction kernel for which the
measured torque differs between the two states.
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
Use Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.torque-observable} with a directional weighting kernel that
selects the subset where fluxes differ. Equal total power constrains only energetic
integral transport and does not constrain the angular-flux distribution channel by
channel; hence projected torque functionals can differ.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[thick] (-1.4,-1) rectangle (1.4,1);
  \fill (0,0) circle (1.5pt);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below left] at (0,0) {$\mathbf{r}_0$};
  \draw[->, teal!70!black, thick] (1.0,0.3) arc (10:80:1.0);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (1.15,0.55) {$\mathbf{M}\!\cdot\!\hat{\mathbf{n}}$};
  \draw[->, orange!85!black, thick] (-0.5,-0.2) arc (200:320:0.45);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, left] at (-0.65,-0.35) {$\boldsymbol{\tau}_{\mathrm{mech}}$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Control-volume relation between electromagnetic angular-momentum flux and measurable mechanical torque in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}.}
\label{fig:ch1.torque-balance-control}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.torque-balance-control} emphasizes origin declaration and
surface orientation bookkeeping: both are essential for reproducible torque
comparison across experiments and simulations.

\paragraph{Worked problem: torque balance on a closed radiative surface.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1shell] (0,0) circle (1.2);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (1.2,0) {$S_R$};
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,-0.2) -- (0,1.6) node[ch1oncanvas, above] {$\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \draw[->, thick, gray] (0.85,0) -- (1.15,0) node[above, ch1lblout] {$\hat{\mathbf{n}}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-1.45) {$m{=}0$ vs.\ $m{\neq}0$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: full solid-angle stress integration distinguishing symmetric versus azimuthally phased radiating shells.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-torque-balance-surface}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-torque-balance-surface} confirms Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.torque-observable}: nonzero \(\tau_z^{\mathrm{mech}}\) requires azimuthal phase breaking on \(S_R\), not merely nonzero local phase labels.
For harmonic fields in vacuum with no mechanical loading inside \(V\),
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral} reduces at steady state to
\(\tau_z^{\mathrm{mech}}=-\int_{\partial V}[\mathbf{r}\times(\mathbf{T}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}})]\cdot\hat{\mathbf{z}}\,dS\).
On sphere \(S_R\), use \(\mathbf{r}=R\hat{\mathbf{r}}\) and
\(dS=R^2\sin\theta\,d\theta\,d\phi\). For the linearly polarized shell of
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-rad-power}, symmetry under \(\phi\to\phi+\pi\) forces
the integrand to change sign under \(\phi\to\phi+\pi\) when \(m=0\), giving
\(\tau_z^{\mathrm{mech}}=0\) after full solid-angle integration. For the helical
shell of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-helical-torque}, azimuthal phase breaks that
symmetry and yields nonzero \(\tau_z^{\mathrm{mech}}\propto -mP_{\mathrm{rad}}/\omega\).
The same conclusion follows whether one integrates \(\mathbf{M}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\)
directly or projects through Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.torque-observable}, confirming that
torque is an integral transport report, not a local phase label
\cite{stratton1941}.

Equations~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-momentum-balance}
and \eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral} assume declared origin, admissible
stress-tensor closure, and local constitutive behavior in the present regime. In
strongly nonlocal media, active metasurfaces, microscopic spin-lattice coupling,
or unresolved subwavelength torque channels, additional terms may be required
before torque partition is interpreted without ambiguity
\cite{stratton1941}.

For forward use, rotational interpretation is accepted only when a strict
transport-qualified criterion holds:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.torque-transport-qualified-criterion}
\mathcal{Q}_{J}=1
\iff
\Big(
\|\mathbf{M}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\|_{L^1(\partial V)}>0,\ 
\|\boldsymbol{\tau}_{\mathrm{mech}}\|_{L^1(V)}>0,\ 
\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{adm}}\le \varepsilon_{\mathrm{adm}}
\Big),
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{adm}}\) is the admissibility residual from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.admissibility-residual}, and \(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{adm}}>0\) is
a prescribed acceptance threshold. Thus a phase or polarization pattern is called
rotationally meaningful only when nonzero angular-flux transfer and nonzero torque
channel are simultaneously present under admissible data constraints.

This criterion provides the exact bridge to Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}: directional
and topological descriptors introduced there will be treated as physically
credible only if they satisfy \(\mathcal{Q}_{J}=1\), so interpretation remains
anchored to transport and torque observables rather than visual morphology alone.

\paragraph{Worked problem: radiating spherical shell.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1shell] (0,0.5) circle (1.1);
  \draw[->, thick, teal!70!black] (0.7,0.5) -- (1.15,0.5);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above] at (0,1.75) {$\E,\H\propto e^{-\jj k_0 r}/r$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-0.75) {$\tau_z^{\mathrm{mech}}=0$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: isotropic-power spherical shell with vanishing axis torque under azimuthal symmetry.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-radiating-shell}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-radiating-shell} sets the \(m=0\) baseline for Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-rad-power}: finite \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) coexists with zero axis-projected torque until helical phase modifies \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\).
Let \(\E,\H\) be outward-propagating fields on a sphere \(S_R\) in vacuum, with
\(\H=\eta_0^{-1}\hat{\mathbf{r}}\times\E\) and \(\E=E_0 e^{-\jj k_0 r}/r\). Then
\(\mathbf{S}=\tfrac12\E\times\H^*=(|E_0|^2/2\eta_0 r^2)\hat{\mathbf{r}}\) and
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.worked-rad-power}
P_{\mathrm{rad}}(R)=\int_{S_R}\Re(\mathbf{S}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}})\,dS
=\frac{4\pi |E_0|^2}{2\eta_0}.
\end{equation}
The axis-projected torque of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.torque-observable} vanishes for this
linearly polarized shell; adding helical phase \(\exp(\jj m\phi)\) modifies
\(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) without changing Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-rad-power}
\cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: helical shell with nonzero torque channel.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1shell] (0,0.5) circle (1.1);
  \draw[->, thick, orange!85!black] (0.55,0.5) arc (0:-250:0.5);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (0.9,0.15) {$m{=}1,\ \sigma{=}{+}1$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-0.75) {$\tau_z\propto -(m{+}\sigma)P_{\mathrm{rad}}/\omega$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: helical phase on a radiating spherical shell opening a nonzero axis torque channel at fixed \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\).}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-helical-shell-torque}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-helical-shell-torque} links azimuthal phase breaking to Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-helical-torque}: for \(m=1\), \(\sigma=+1\), the mechanical torque scale follows \((m+\sigma)P_{\mathrm{rad}}/\omega\) while Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-rad-power} stays unchanged.
Retain the radiating shell of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-rad-power} but impose
\(\E=E_0 e^{-\jj k_0 r}e^{\jj m\phi}/r\) with integer \(m\neq 0\), helicity
\(\sigma=+1\) for right-circular polarization on the shell, and
\(\H=\eta_0^{-1}\hat{\mathbf{r}}\times\E\). On \(S_R\), the traction from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} produces axis torque density
\([(\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{T})\cdot\hat{\mathbf{z}}]\propto (m+\sigma)/\omega\) times local
power flux. Integrating over \(S_R\) and using
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral} gives
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.worked-helical-torque}
\tau_z^{\mathrm{mech}}
\approx -\frac{m+\sigma}{\omega}\,P_{\mathrm{rad}}(R),
\end{equation}
where \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}(R)\) is unchanged from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-rad-power}. For \(m=1\), \(\sigma=+1\),
\(\omega=2\pi\times 10\,\mathrm{GHz}\), and \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}=1\,\mathrm{W}\),
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-helical-torque} predicts
\(|\tau_z^{\mathrm{mech}}|\approx 3.18\times 10^{-11}\,\mathrm{N\,m}\),
a small but nonzero load torque entirely fixed by exported power and helical index.
The linearly polarized shell (\(m=0\)) and helical shell (\(m=1\)) share the same
\(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) yet differ in \(\tau_z\), illustrating why power alone cannot
certify angular transport \cite{poynting1884,belinfante1940}.

Beth's 1936 experiment \cite{beth1936} demonstrated that circularly polarized
microwave beams exert measurable torque on suspended wave plates, supplying early
evidence that electromagnetic fields carry angular momentum capable of mechanical
exchange. Belinfante \cite{belinfante1940} showed theoretically that spin and orbital
contributions are linked by continuity equations that are not unique in their local
densities but share integrated conservation. Allen \emph{et al.} \cite{allen1992}
later associated Laguerre--Gaussian modes with a countable azimuthal index, extending
the experimental vocabulary to spatial phase gradients. Chapter~\ref{ch:01} does not
choose among these historical languages; it requires that whichever label is used,
closure must follow Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}.

The angular-flux tensor \(\mathbf{M}=\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{T}\) inherits origin
dependence explicitly. Translating the origin by \(\mathbf{d}\) shifts the orbital
part \(\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{em}}\) while leaving total field
momentum unchanged if no external force acts. For torque observables, define the
origin sensitivity
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.torque-origin-shift}
\tau_{\hat{\mathbf{u}}}(\mathbf{r}_0+\mathbf{d})
-\tau_{\hat{\mathbf{u}}}(\mathbf{r}_0)
=\hat{\mathbf{u}}\cdot\left(\mathbf{d}\times\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{em}}\right),
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{em}}=\int_V \mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV\) is total
electromagnetic linear momentum in \(V\). Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.torque-origin-shift}
states that torque about an axis changes under origin translation unless
\(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{em}}\) is parallel to \(\mathbf{d}\times\hat{\mathbf{u}}\).
Reproducible torque comparisons therefore require a declared \(\mathbf{r}_0\) and
documented \(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{em}}\) \cite{belinfante1940}.

Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-density-split} separates orbital and spin contributions
at the density level. The orbital term \(\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{em}}\)
depends on the chosen origin; the spin term \(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{em}}\) captures
polarization-linked density that Beth's experiment accessed through circular
polarization. Neither term alone satisfies a gauge-independent local conservation
law; only the sum \(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\) enters
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-momentum-balance} with the flux divergence
\(\divg\mathbf{M}\) \cite{belinfante1940}.

The transport-qualified criterion Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.torque-transport-qualified-criterion}
combines three physically distinct tests: nonzero angular-flux integral on
\(\partial V\), nonzero mechanical torque channel, and small admissibility residual
\(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{adm}}\). The third condition prevents torque artifacts from
charge-inconsistent sources. The first two prevent reactive circulation or gauge
artifacts from being promoted to transport \cite{poynting1884,belinfante1940}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: origin shift on a helical beam.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,-0.2) -- (0,1.8) node[ch1oncanvas, above] {$\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \fill (0,0.6) circle (1.5pt) node[left, ch1lblout] {$O$};
  \fill (0.5,0.6) circle (1.5pt) node[right, ch1lblout] {$O'$};
  \draw[->, dashed, gray] (0,0.6) -- (0.5,0.6) node[midway, above, ch1lblout] {$\mathbf{d}$};
  \draw[ch1shell] (0,0.6) circle (0.9);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: axial versus transverse origin shifts for torque readout on a helical beam with unchanged radiated power.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-helical-origin-shift}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-helical-origin-shift} distinguishes symmetric axial shifts from transverse lever-arm effects in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.torque-origin-shift}: \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) and \(m\) can remain fixed while the reported torque changes under \(\mathbf{d}\times\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{em}}\).
Take the helical shell of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-helical-torque} with
\(m=1\), \(\sigma=+1\), and \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}=1\,\mathrm{W}\) at
\(f=10\,\mathrm{GHz}\). With origin on the beam axis,
\(\tau_z^{\mathrm{mech}}\approx -3.18\times 10^{-11}\,\mathrm{N\,m}\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-helical-torque}. Shift the origin to
\(\mathbf{r}_0=(0,0,z_0)\) with \(z_0=5\,\mathrm{mm}\) along the propagation axis.
The orbital density \(\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{em}}\) changes because
\(\mathbf{r}\) is re-measured, but \(\int_{S_R}\mathbf{M}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS\)
remains unchanged if \(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{em}}\) is parallel to \(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\).
Torque measured by a wave plate centered on the beam axis is therefore origin
invariant in this symmetric case. Offset the detector transversely by
\(\mathbf{d}=2\,\mathrm{mm}\,\hat{\mathbf{x}}\): Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.torque-origin-shift}
predicts an additional contribution \(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\cdot(\mathbf{d}\times\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{em}})\)
that can alter the reported torque even when \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) and \(m\) are
unchanged \cite{beth1936,allen1992}.

\paragraph{Validity of the angular balance laws.}
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-momentum-balance} and~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}
assume the symmetric Maxwell stress of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress}, classical
material response, and slow variation of constitutive parameters on the scale of
\(\lambda\). Magnetic materials with strong spin-orbit coupling, moving media, and
quantized spin Hall systems require extended torque budgets \cite{stratton1941}.
Within the vacuum harmonic framework adopted here, the balance laws are the
rotational closure imported by every transport criterion in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}
\cite{maxwell1873,belinfante1940,stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Section 1.1 summary and transition.}
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1} closes the Maxwell conservation chain---field laws,
admissible sources and boundaries, uniqueness, and the energy--momentum--angular
balance laws that fix measurable transfer channels
\cite{stratton1941}. Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} now asks
which directional and phase-polarization descriptors inherit transport meaning from
those balances rather than from visual pattern alone
\cite{stratton1941}.


\section{Angular Structure of Electromagnetic Fields}
\label{sec:ch01.2}

\emph{Angular structure of electromagnetic fields} names the directional, phase, polarization,
and transport organization of qualified Maxwell solutions---not beam nicknames alone.
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} develops that meaning from field quantities and transport criteria.
Appearance versus transport is enforced repeatedly: phase winding and modal index are
representation-facing descriptors until flux integrals and measurement maps certify export
\cite{belinfante1940,poynting1884,allen1992}. The degrees-of-freedom question seeded in
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.0} reappears here as directional resolution and channel count on
observation spheres \cite{hansen1988}.

Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} develops the meaning of angular structure from
field quantities and transport criteria rather than from phase-pattern naming.
Its scope is to separate directional geometry, polarization-phase organization,
and genuine angular-momentum carriage under explicit radiative and reactive
regimes, so that each later modal interpretation remains tied to an operational
electromagnetic criterion.


\subsection{Directional dependence and angular momentum}
\label{sec:ch01.2.1}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Directional dependence is the first operational screening in angular analysis. A field
distribution can satisfy global energy conservation and still fail to produce
directional rotational transfer in the channels used by a detector or scatterer.
Therefore, this subsection defines angular interpretation through direction-resolved
transport observables rather than through visual phase morphology
\cite{stratton1941}.

The modern emphasis on orbital angular momentum of paraxial beams
\cite{allen1992} makes directional decomposition indispensable. Allen \emph{et al.}
showed that Laguerre--Gaussian modes carry a well-defined azimuthal index and that
mode converters can transfer that index reversibly between Hermite and Laguerre
families. Those results belong to the physics of structured light, yet they do not
replace the Maxwellian flux pair \((\Pi,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J)\): a mode label is a
compact name for a field pattern, whereas Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-power-density}
and~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-am-flux} are the measurement-facing quantities that
decide whether a named pattern exports power and angular action on a chosen sphere
\cite{stratton1941,belinfante1940}. Historical continuity matters here:
directional radiation patterns were analyzed in antenna theory long before OAM
became a standalone keyword \cite{stratton1941}.

The transition from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1} is governed by the strict qualification
\(\mathcal{Q}_{J}=1\) in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.torque-transport-qualified-criterion}. Any directional angular
claim made below is physically accepted only when that criterion remains satisfied; if
the criterion fails, the same directional pattern is treated as kinematic appearance
without verified rotational transfer meaning.

Let \(\hat{\mathbf{r}}=\hat{\mathbf{r}}(\theta,\phi)\) be radial unit vector on a
radius-\(r\) sphere and \(d\Omega=\sin\theta\,d\theta\,d\phi\) the solid-angle
measure. Using \(\mathbf{S}\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-complex}, define
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.directional-power-density}
\Pi(\theta,\phi;r)=
r^2\,\Re\!\left\{\mathbf{S}(r,\theta,\phi)\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}}\right\}.
\end{equation}
The product \(\mathbf{S}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}}\) selects outward energetic transport
in that channel, while \(r^2\) rescales local flux density to per-solid-angle
channel power.

The integrated total is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.directional-power-integral}
P_{\mathrm{rad}}(r)=\int_{4\pi}\Pi(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-power-integral} is a projection of channelwise
structure. Equal projected totals do not imply equal directional physics.

For rotational transport, define from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-momentum-balance}
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.directional-am-flux}
\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi;r)=
r^2\,\mathbf{M}(r,\theta,\phi)\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}}.
\end{equation}
Here \(\mathbf{M}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}}\) is direction-resolved angular-flux
transfer. The pair \((\Pi,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J)\) is the minimal dataset required to
separate energetic transport from rotational transport channel by channel.

The corresponding total outward angular flux is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.directional-am-total}
\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}(r)=
\int_{4\pi}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega.
\end{equation}
This vector integral cannot replace channelwise structure because cancellation among
opposing angular channels can leave a small total despite large directional flux
magnitudes.

A normalized directional efficiency observable is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.directional-transport-ratio}
\boldsymbol{\eta}_J(\theta,\phi;r)=
\frac{\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi;r)}
{\Pi(\theta,\phi;r)+\epsilon_\Pi},
\qquad \epsilon_\Pi>0.
\end{equation}
\(\epsilon_\Pi\) regularizes channels near energetic nulls. Large
\(\|\boldsymbol{\eta}_J\|\) identifies directions where modest power supports strong
rotational transfer, which is common in highly structured emission fields.

In antenna practice, directional diagrams display \(\Pi(\theta,\phi)\) long before
angular-flux maps are computed; the present subsection makes the second map explicit
so that a main-lobe power peak is never automatically read as a torque-capable
channel. Stratton's systematic field-theory treatment \cite{stratton1941} already
 emphasized that multipole expansions organize angular dependence of radiation,
while Belinfante's decomposition \cite{belinfante1940} warns that local spin and
orbital labels are not unique even when totals are fixed. Both lessons are absorbed
here by keeping \(\Pi\) and \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) distinct until their ratio
passes the qualification criteria below \cite{stratton1941}.

Channel qualification is imposed explicitly by
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.directional-qualified-gate}
\mathcal{Q}_{J,\Omega}(\theta,\phi;r)=1
\iff
\Big(
\Pi(\theta,\phi;r)>0,\
\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi;r)\|>0,\
\mathcal{Q}_{J}=1
\Big).
\end{equation}
Thus only channels with \(\mathcal{Q}_{J,\Omega}=1\) contribute to transport-
qualified angular interpretation in later subsections.

Directional centroids are defined by
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.directional-power-centroid}
\mathbf{c}_P(r)=
\frac{\int_{4\pi}\hat{\mathbf{r}}\,\Pi(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega}
{\int_{4\pi}\Pi(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega},
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.directional-am-centroid}
\mathbf{c}_J(r)=
\frac{\int_{4\pi}\hat{\mathbf{r}}\,
\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi;r)\|\,d\Omega}
{\int_{4\pi}\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi;r)\|\,d\Omega}.
\end{equation}
