Mismatch \(\mathbf{c}_P\neq\mathbf{c}_J\) means dominant energetic direction and
dominant rotational direction are geometrically displaced, a measurable effect in
torque-sensitive coupling experiments.

\begin{proposition}[Directional non-equivalence criterion]
\label{prop:ch1.directional-nonequivalence}
Let two radiative states \(\mathcal{F}_1,\mathcal{F}_2\) at radius \(r\) satisfy
\(P_{\mathrm{rad}}^{(1)}(r)=P_{\mathrm{rad}}^{(2)}(r)\). If there exists a set
\(\mathcal{U}\subset4\pi\) of nonzero measure where
\(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J^{(1)}\neq\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J^{(2)}\) and
\(\mathcal{Q}_{J,\Omega}=1\), then the states are rotationally non-equivalent for
direction-selective interactions.
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
The equality of \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) constrains only
\(\int_{4\pi}\Pi\,d\Omega\). It does not constrain channelwise rotational flux.
Define admissible interaction kernel \(K(\theta,\phi)\) supported on \(\mathcal{U}\):
\[
\mathbf{T}_{\mathrm{obs}}=
\int_{4\pi}K(\theta,\phi)\,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega.
\]
Since qualified channels differ, observed torque differs.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1shell] (0,0) circle (1.5);
  \foreach \a in {30,150,270} {
    \draw[->, thick, teal!60] (0,0) -- (\a:1.2);
  }
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above=10mm] at (0,1.5) {$\Pi,\ \boldsymbol{\Psi}_J$};
  \node[ch1box, below=22mm of {(0,0)}] (q) {$\mathcal{Q}_{J,\Omega}=1$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Direction-resolved energetic and rotational transport under the strict transport-qualified interpretation criterion inherited from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1}.}
\label{fig:ch1.directional-transport-decomposition}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.directional-transport-decomposition} clarifies that integrated
totals are terminal reductions of richer channel fields. Different channel topology
can produce nearly identical totals while yielding different torque outcomes.

Origin declaration remains essential because angular transport is moment-based.
Changing origin re-weights channel moments even when global balances remain
unchanged. For reproducible comparison, all directional flux and torque statements
must use the same origin convention fixed in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1.5}.

Directional observables represent physical transport
only when outward channels are well-defined and \(\mathcal{Q}_{J}=1\) remains valid.
In strongly reactive near zones, strongly nonlocal media, or rapidly modulated
boundaries, extra decomposition terms may be necessary before \(\Pi\) and
\(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) are treated as direct rotational-transfer observables
\cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: equal power, unequal directional torque.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \node[ch1box] (a) {State A\\$\Delta\Omega_+$ only};
  \node[ch1box, right=22mm of a] (b) {State B\\$+\!$ and $\Delta\Omega_-$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below=11mm of a] {$\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}=\mathbf{J}_+\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below=11mm of b] {$\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}=\mathbf{0}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above=6mm of a] {$P_0$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above=6mm of b] {$P_0$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: matched radiated power with unidirectional versus bidirectional angular-flux sector allocation.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-equal-power-torque}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-equal-power-torque} makes Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-directional-cancellation} visible: State~B can exceed State~A in integrated \(\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\|\) yet export zero net torque when opposite sectors cancel.
Two far-zone states \(\mathcal{F}_A,\mathcal{F}_B\) on radius \(r\) share
\(P_{\mathrm{rad}}^{(A)}=P_{\mathrm{rad}}^{(B)}=P_0\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-power-integral}. State~A carries angular flux only
in sector \(\Delta\Omega_+\) with \(\int_{\Delta\Omega_+}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J^{(A)}\,d\Omega
=\mathbf{J}_+\hat{\mathbf{z}}\), while state~B splits equal magnitude flux between
\(\Delta\Omega_+\) and opposite sector \(\Delta\Omega_-\) with
\(\int_{\Delta\Omega_-}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J^{(B)}\,d\Omega=-\mathbf{J}_+\hat{\mathbf{z}}\).
Then
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.worked-directional-cancellation}
\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}^{(A)}=\mathbf{J}_+\hat{\mathbf{z}},\qquad
\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}^{(B)}=\mathbf{0},
\end{equation}
even though \(\int_{4\pi}\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J^{(B)}\|\,d\Omega\) may exceed
\(\int_{4\pi}\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J^{(A)}\|\,d\Omega\). The cancellation factor
\(\mathcal{K}_J\) of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-cancellation-factor} is near
unity for~A and near zero for~B. A direction-selective interaction kernel
\(K(\theta,\phi)\) supported on \(\Delta\Omega_+\) therefore produces different
observed torques despite matched radiated power, confirming
Proposition~\ref{prop:ch1.directional-nonequivalence} in operational form
\cite{stratton1941}.

Antenna engineers have long plotted \(\Pi(\theta,\phi)\) as radiation patterns
\cite{stratton1941}; the present subsection adds the companion map
\(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi)\) so that pattern symmetry in power does not
automatically imply symmetry in torque response. Stratton's multipole organization
\cite{stratton1941} shows that angular dependence factorizes into spherical
harmonics at large distance, while Belinfante's decomposition \cite{belinfante1940}
warns that different angular-momentum densities can yield identical far-zone totals.
The pair \((\Pi,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J)\) is the minimal directional dataset that
respects both lessons.

The directional transport ratio Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-transport-ratio} has
dimensions of inverse frequency when \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) carries units of angular
flux density and \(\Pi\) carries watts per steradian. Large \(\|\boldsymbol{\eta}_J\|\)
identifies directions where a small power channel nonetheless exports disproportionate
angular action---typical of vortex beams with hollow intensity cores where
\(\Pi(\theta,\phi)\) is small but \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) remains nonzero on the
ring \cite{allen1992}. Interpreting the ratio requires simultaneous
report of \(\Pi\): dividing by a near-zero denominator inflates \(\boldsymbol{\eta}_J\)
artificially.

Lobe asymmetry can be quantified without introducing new transport postulates:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.directional-lobe-asymmetry}
\mathcal{A}_J=
\frac{
\max_{\Delta\Omega}\|\int_{\Delta\Omega}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\,d\Omega\|
}{
\int_{4\pi}\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\|\,d\Omega+\epsilon_A
},
\qquad \epsilon_A>0,
\end{equation}
where the maximum runs over a declared family of solid-angle sectors, for example
main lobe plus sidelobes. When \(\mathcal{A}_J\approx 1\), angular flux is
concentrated in one sector; when \(\mathcal{A}_J\ll 1\), flux is distributed and
prone to cancellation in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-am-total}. This index
complements \(\mathcal{K}_J\) of Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.3}
\cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: cardiod power with asymmetric angular flux.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[thick, domain=0:180, samples=50, smooth]
    plot ({0.35+0.25*cos(\x)},{0.35+0.25*sin(\x)});
  \fill[red!15] (45:0.5) arc (45:90:0.5) -- (0,0) -- cycle;
  \node[ch1oncanvas] at (0.35,0.35) {C};
  \node[ch1oncanvas] at (0.55,0.15) {D};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0.5,-0.1) {same $\Pi$, different $\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: identical directional power density with distinct angular-flux maps and sector-selective torque sensitivity.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-cardiod-asymmetric-flux}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-cardiod-asymmetric-flux} confirms Proposition~\ref{prop:ch1.directional-nonequivalence}: equal intercepted power \(0.31\,\mathrm{W}\) on the same sector can yield \(40\%\) torque difference because \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) differs on the absorbed patch.
Two states radiate \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}=1\,\mathrm{W}\) at \(kr=10\). State~C has
\(\Pi(\theta,\phi)\propto(1+\cos\theta)^2\) and
\(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\propto\sin\theta\cos\theta\,\hat{\boldsymbol{\phi}}\), giving
\(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}=0.05\,\hat{\mathbf{z}}\,\mathrm{N\,m}\) and
\(\mathcal{A}_J=0.82\). State~D has the same \(\Pi\) but
\(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\propto\sin(2\theta)\,\hat{\mathbf{z}}\), yielding
\(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}=0.12\,\hat{\mathbf{z}}\,\mathrm{N\,m}\) and
\(\mathcal{A}_J=0.55\). A direction-selective absorber covering
\(\theta\in[\pi/4,\pi/2]\) intercepts \(0.31\,\mathrm{W}\) for both states yet
produces torques differing by \(40\%\) because \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) differs on
the intercepted sector. Equal \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) therefore does not imply equal
torque sensitivity, confirming Proposition~\ref{prop:ch1.directional-nonequivalence}
\cite{belinfante1940}.

\paragraph{Validity of directional observables.}
The maps \(\Pi\) and \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) assume harmonic steady state, outgoing
support on the observation sphere, and a declared origin for
\(\mathbf{M}=\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{T}\). In the reactive near zone, \(\Pi\) may
be small while \(\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\|\) is dominated by storage circulation;
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.4} supplies \(\chi(r)\) to flag that regime
\cite{chu1948}. Within the radiative shell where
\(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=1\), directional observables are the primary
inputs to the three-part transport test of Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.3} \cite{stratton1941}.


\subsection{Amplitude, phase, polarization, and Poynting topology}
\label{sec:ch01.2.2}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Amplitude, phase, polarization, and Poynting topology are treated here as a single
diagnostic chain rather than independent descriptors. Amplitude determines local
channel weight, phase controls interference geometry, polarization encodes vector
state of oscillation, and Poynting topology encodes flow organization. None of the
four has direct rotational-transport meaning unless the transport-qualification criterion
from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.torque-transport-qualified-criterion} remains satisfied
\cite{stratton1941}.

In local transverse basis
\((\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}},\hat{\boldsymbol{\phi}})\), write
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.transverse-components}
\E_t=E_\theta\,\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}+E_\phi\,\hat{\boldsymbol{\phi}},\qquad
E_\theta=A_\theta e^{\jj\varphi_\theta},\quad
E_\phi=A_\phi e^{\jj\varphi_\phi},
\end{equation}
with amplitudes \(A_\theta,A_\phi\ge 0\) and phases
\(\varphi_\theta,\varphi_\phi\). The relative phase
\(\Delta\varphi=\varphi_\phi-\varphi_\theta\) controls polarization coupling,
whereas common phase shift leaves transport observables unchanged.

Long before Laguerre--Gaussian modes were linked to orbital index
\cite{allen1992}, helical phase fronts and rotating Poynting vectors appeared in
laser cavity physics and antenna aperture theory \cite{stratton1941}. The
present diagnostic chain separates what those historical pictures share---local
vector geometry---from what they cannot guarantee: signed export of angular
momentum through a control surface. Circular polarization carries spin angular
momentum in the sense measured by Beth \cite{beth1936}; azimuthal phase ramps
suggest orbital organization; only the triad tests of
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.3} decide whether either signature is export-capable in
a given regime \cite{belinfante1940}.

Polarization coherence is encoded through
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.coherency-matrix}
\mathbf{J}_p=
\begin{bmatrix}
\langle E_\theta E_\theta^* \rangle & \langle E_\theta E_\phi^*\rangle\\
\langle E_\phi E_\theta^*\rangle & \langle E_\phi E_\phi^*\rangle
\end{bmatrix},
\end{equation}
where \(\langle\cdot\rangle\) is local temporal/ensemble average. Diagonal entries
measure component intensity and off-diagonal entries measure phase-coherent coupling
between channels.

Stokes invariants are
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.stokes-local}
\begin{aligned}
S_0&=|E_\theta|^2+|E_\phi|^2,\\
S_1&=|E_\theta|^2-|E_\phi|^2,\\
S_2&=2\Re\!\left\{E_\theta E_\phi^*\right\},\\
S_3&=-2\Im\!\left\{E_\theta E_\phi^*\right\}.
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}
\(S_2\) and \(S_3\) vanish without coherence between \(E_\theta\) and \(E_\phi\),
so elliptical/handed structure requires phase-coupled component channels.

Ellipse parameters follow by
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.polarization-ellipse-angles}
\psi=\frac{1}{2}\operatorname{atan2}(S_2,S_1),\qquad
\chi=\frac{1}{2}\arcsin\!\left(\frac{S_3}{S_0+\epsilon_S}\right),
\end{equation}
where \(\psi\) is orientation, \(\chi\) ellipticity, and \(\epsilon_S>0\) is
regularizer near low-intensity states. These are representation coordinates, not
transport guarantees.

\paragraph{Concrete polarization walk-through.}
Take \(E_\theta=1\) and \(E_\phi=\jj\). Then \(S_0=2\), \(S_1=0\), \(S_2=0\),
\(S_3=-2\), giving circular polarization with \(\chi=-\pi/4\). The Poynting vector
rotates locally, yet equal outward channel flux can yield vanishing net angular
export. For \(E_\theta=1\), \(E_\phi=0.9e^{\jj\pi/3}\), one obtains
\(S_0\approx 3.61\), \(S_1\approx 0.19\), \(S_2\approx 0.78\), \(S_3\approx -1.56\):
elliptical coupling with nonzero \(\curl\mathbf{P}\) but possibly small
\(\int_{4\pi}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\,d\Omega\). Polarization fixes local geometry;
transport meaning requires the closure tests of Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2.3}
\cite{stratton1941}. For the linear-polarization case
\(E_\theta=1\), \(E_\phi=0\), Stokes parameters give \(S_3=0\) and vanishing
helicity density, so no preferred handedness is present even if \(\curl\mathbf{P}\)
is nonzero locally. For circular polarization \(E_\phi=\jj E_\theta\), \(S_3=\mp 2\)
and helicity density has definite sign, yet net torque still requires channel
imbalance as in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.ex62-net-am-transport}. Students should therefore
treat polarization and phase winding as orthogonal diagnostics: the first labels
local vector state, the second labels azimuthal organization, and neither replaces
\(\mathcal{D}_J=1\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.transport-qualified-decision}.

Phase-geometry gradients are
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.local-phase-gradient}
\mathbf{k}_{\theta}=\nabla\varphi_\theta,\qquad
\mathbf{k}_{\phi}=\nabla\varphi_\phi,\qquad
\Delta\mathbf{k}=\nabla\Delta\varphi.
\end{equation}
\(\Delta\mathbf{k}\) measures local shear in polarization-coupling phase; large
\(\|\Delta\mathbf{k}\|\) indicates rapid local vector-state rotation.

Poynting-flow topology is read from
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.poynting-average}
\mathbf{P}=\Re\!\left\{\mathbf{S}\right\},
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.poynting-vorticity}
\boldsymbol{\Omega}_P=\curl \mathbf{P}.
\end{equation}
\(\mathbf{P}\) is active transport density; \(\boldsymbol{\Omega}_P\) marks local
circulation of that transport. Local circulation alone does not certify exported
rotational transport.

Phase-singularity support satisfies
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.phase-singularity-condition}
A_\theta=A_\phi=0.
\end{equation}
For contour \(\mathcal{C}\) excluding singular points,
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.phase-winding-number}
q=\frac{1}{2\pi}\oint_{\mathcal{C}}\nabla\arg(E_\theta+\jj E_\phi)\cdot d\boldsymbol{\ell},
\end{equation}
with \(q\in\mathbb{Z}\) classifying local winding class.

To enforce transport-qualified interpretation at local level, define
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.topology-qualified-gate}
\mathcal{Q}_{\mathrm{top}}(\mathbf{r})=1
\iff
\Big(
\mathcal{Q}_{J}=1,\
\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi;r)\|>0,\
\|\mathbf{P}(\mathbf{r})\|>0
\Big).
\end{equation}
Hence local topology markers are interpreted as rotationally relevant only where
global admissibility and directional angular-flux support are simultaneously
verified.

\begin{proposition}[Topology does not imply exported angular transport]
\label{prop:ch1.topology-transport-separation}
There exist fields with nontrivial local phase topology
\((q\neq 0,\ \boldsymbol{\Omega}_P\neq\mathbf{0})\) and nontrivial polarization
texture \((S_3\neq 0)\) for which exported angular flux across a closed sphere
vanishes after channel integration.
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
Construct counter-rotating channel pairs with matched energetic content and opposite
directional angular flux. Local winding and handedness remain nontrivial, yet
angular-flux integral cancels. Therefore local topology is not sufficient for
torque-capable export.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=9mm and 11mm]
  \node[ch1box] (ap) {$(A_\theta,A_\phi,\Delta\varphi)$};
  \node[ch1box, right=14mm of ap] (st) {Stokes $(\psi,\chi)$};
  \node[ch1box, right=14mm of st] (fl) {$(\mathbf{P},\boldsymbol{\Omega}_P)$};
  \node[ch1box, below=10mm of st] (qt) {$\mathcal{Q}_{\mathrm{top}}=1$};
  \draw[ch1flow] (ap) -- (st) -- (fl);
  \draw[ch1flow] (st) -- (qt);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Dependency chain from local amplitude-phase structure to transport-qualified topology diagnostics in Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.2}.}
\label{fig:ch1.phase-polarization-topology-map}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.phase-polarization-topology-map} emphasizes ordering: first
local representation, then flow geometry, then transport qualification. This order
prevents misclassification of texture-rich but transport-inactive states.

Two states can therefore share similar \(S_0\) maps while differing in
\(\Delta\varphi\), \(S_3\), and \(\boldsymbol{\Omega}_P\), yielding different torque
coupling when \(\mathcal{Q}_{\mathrm{top}}=1\). Conversely, similar topological
markers can yield weak exported torque when directional-flux cancellation dominates.

The Stokes--Poynting decomposition assumes local smoothness for
component extraction, differentiability for \(\curl\mathbf{P}\), and measurement
bandwidth sufficient for coherency inference. In strongly lossy, highly
inhomogeneous, or truncated-aperture settings, windowing/regularization are needed
before cross-section topology comparison becomes transport-credible
\cite{stratton1941}.

Poynting \cite{poynting1884} first visualized energy flow lines; Beth
\cite{beth1936} later connected polarization handedness to mechanical torque. The
Stokes parameters Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.stokes-local} encode polarization on the
Poincar\'e sphere: \(S_0\) is total intensity, \((S_1,S_2,S_3)\) are Cartesian
coordinates on the unit sphere of polarization states. The map
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.poincare-sphere-map}
S_0=\|\mathbf{s}_p\|,\qquad
\frac{S_3}{S_0+\epsilon_S}=\sin 2\chi,\qquad
\frac{\sqrt{S_1^2+S_2^2}}{S_0+\epsilon_S}=\cos 2\chi,
\end{equation}
with \(\mathbf{s}_p=(S_1,S_2,S_3)\), connects measurable Stokes components to
ellipse angles Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.polarization-ellipse-angles}. Points at the
poles represent circular polarization; the equator represents linear polarization.
Transport interpretation, however, requires leaving the Poincar\'e sphere and
evaluating \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) on a control surface, because local
polarization labels do not integrate to exported angular flux without channel
imbalance \cite{belinfante1940}.

The phase-winding number Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.phase-winding-number} classifies
topological charge of the transverse field line. Allen \emph{et al.}
\cite{allen1992} identified this index with Laguerre--Gaussian modes, but
Proposition~\ref{prop:ch1.topology-transport-separation} shows that \(q\neq 0\)
does not force \(\int_{4\pi}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\,d\Omega\neq\mathbf{0}\). The
Poynting vorticity Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-vorticity} marks local circulation
of power flow; combined with Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-phase-gradient}, it
distinguishes shear in polarization phase from azimuthal phase ramps associated
with orbital indices \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: orthogonal linear polarizations with controlled phase ramp.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (-1.8,0) -- (1.8,0) node[right] {$y$};
  \draw[->, thick, blue!70!black] plot[domain=-1.5:1.5,samples=30] (\x,{0.35*sin(deg(50*\x))});
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above] at (0,0.45) {$\mathbf{P}$ transverse};
  \draw[ch1shell, dashed] (0,0) circle (1.1);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (1.1,0) {$\mathcal{D}_J=0$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: rotating linear polarization producing local Poynting circulation without export-capable angular transport.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-phase-ramp-linear}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-phase-ramp-linear} separates rotating linear polarization from helical export: opposing sectors cancel \(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}\) despite peaked \(\|\boldsymbol{\Omega}_P\|\) near \(y=\pi/(4\beta)\).
On a plane transverse to \(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\), take
\(E_\theta=A e^{\jj\beta y}\) and \(E_\phi=A e^{\jj(\beta y+\Delta\varphi)}\) with
\(A=1\,\mathrm{V/m}\), \(\beta=50\,\mathrm{rad/m}\), and \(\Delta\varphi=\pi/2\).
Then \(S_0=2A^2\), \(S_3=-2A^2\sin\Delta\varphi=0\), and the field is linearly
polarized with orientation rotating along \(y\). The Poynting vector
\(\mathbf{P}=\Re\{\mathbf{S}\}\) acquires a transverse component from the phase
gradient, and \(\|\boldsymbol{\Omega}_P\|\) peaks near \(y=\pi/(4\beta)\approx
1.6\,\mathrm{cm}\). Evaluating \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) on a sphere of radius
\(r=20\,\mathrm{cm}\) at \(f=10\,\mathrm{GHz}\) yields
\(\|\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}\|\ll \|\int_{4\pi}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\|\,d\Omega\)
because opposing sectors cancel: \(\mathcal{Q}_{\mathrm{top}}=1\) locally but
\(\mathcal{D}_J=0\) globally. The example separates rotating linear polarization
from export-capable helical transport \cite{beth1936,stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Jones calculus on the Poincar\'e sphere.}
For fully polarized harmonic fields, the Jones vector
\(\mathbf{j}_p=(E_\theta,E_\phi)^\mathsf{T}\) evolves under ideal polarizers and
wave plates by unitary \(2\times 2\) matrices. A rotation of the polarization
state through angle \(2\psi\) about axis \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}_p\) on the Poincar\'e
sphere corresponds to
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.jones-rotation}
\mathbf{j}_p'
=
\exp\!\left(-\jj\,\psi\,\hat{\mathbf{n}}_p\cdot\boldsymbol{\sigma}\right)\mathbf{j}_p,
\end{equation}
where \(\boldsymbol{\sigma}=(\sigma_x,\sigma_y,\sigma_z)\) are Pauli matrices and
\(\hat{\mathbf{n}}_p\cdot\boldsymbol{\sigma}\) is the generator of polarization
rotations \cite{stratton1941}. Mathematically,
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.jones-rotation} is a unitary map on \(\mathbb{C}^2\); physically,
it describes how a lossless optical element reorients the oscillation ellipse without
changing \(S_0\) for a single coherent mode. Beth's quartz-plate measurement
\cite{beth1936} is the mechanical readout of such a rotation when circularly
polarized light transfers angular momentum to the plate.

Partial polarization requires the coherency matrix
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.coherency-matrix}. The degree of polarization
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.polarization-degree}
p=\frac{\sqrt{S_1^2+S_2^2+S_3^2}}{S_0+\epsilon_p},
\qquad 0\le p\le 1,
\end{equation}
with \(\epsilon_p>0\), measures how much of the field energy participates in a
polarized component versus an unpolarized background. When \(p<1\), Stokes parameters
describe an ensemble average and \(\boldsymbol{\Omega}_P=\curl\mathbf{P}\) can remain
large locally while exported torque averages to zero because incoherent components
do not carry a definite handedness. Narrowband laboratory sources often have
\(p>0.95\); thermal or multiply-scattered fields may have \(p\ll 1\), which must be
reported before interpreting \(S_3\) as spin angular momentum
\cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Helical-mode topology without plane-wave templates.}
Allen \emph{et al.} \cite{allen1992} identified paraxial modes
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.lg-vortex-field}
E_\phi(\rho,\phi,z)
=
E_0
\left(\frac{\rho}{w_0}\right)^{|m|}
L_p^{|m|}\!\left(\frac{2\rho^2}{w_0^2}\right)
e^{-\rho^2/w_0^2}\,
e^{\jj(m\phi+k_0 z)},
\end{equation}
where \(L_p^{|m|}\) is an associated Laguerre polynomial, \(w_0\) is beam waist,
and integer \(m\) is azimuthal index. For \(p=0\), \(|m|=1\), the intensity vanishes
on the axis (\(\rho=0\)) while the phase winds by \(2\pi\) on a circuit about the
origin: Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.phase-winding-number} gives \(q=m\). The Poynting vector
circulates azimuthally on the intensity ring, so \(\|\boldsymbol{\Omega}_P\|\) is
large there even when the beam is collimated. Export of orbital angular momentum
requires a single signed channel: superposing Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.lg-vortex-field}
with its conjugate winding produces the same standing pattern as
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.ex62-counter-rotating-superposition} in
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.6.2}. Topology and polarization therefore remain
diagnostic layers; transport is fixed only after channel imbalance is measured on a
control surface \cite{allen1992}.

\paragraph{Poynting-flow organization: circulation versus saddle structure.}
On a transverse plane, critical points of \(\mathbf{P}=\Re\{\mathbf{S}\}\) satisfy
\(\partial_x P_x+\partial_y P_y=0\) with Jacobian
\(\mathcal{J}_P=\partial(P_x,P_y)/\partial(x,y)\). Vortex-type regions have
\(\operatorname{tr}\mathcal{J}_P\approx 0\) and \(\det\mathcal{J}_P>0\); saddle-type
regions have \(\det\mathcal{J}_P<0\). Define the flow-structure index
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.poynting-flow-index}
\mathcal{F}_P=
\frac{\int_A \|\boldsymbol{\Omega}_P\|\,dA}
{\left|\int_A \divg\mathbf{P}\,dA\right|+\epsilon_F},
\end{equation}
where \(A\) is a declared patch and \(\epsilon_F>0\). Large \(\mathcal{F}_P\) with
small net \(\int_A\divg\mathbf{P}\,dA\) signals predominantly circulating storage;
large net divergence with moderate \(\mathcal{F}_P\) signals export-dominated
patches. Poynting's original flow-line pictures \cite{poynting1884} emphasized this
distinction long before Stokes vectors were standard: a beautiful vortex pattern in
\(\mathbf{P}\) is not, by itself, a radiative torque channel.

\paragraph{Worked problem: annular aperture on an LG\(_0^1\)-type ring beam.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1shell, thick] (0,0) circle (0.55);
  \draw[ch1shell, thick] (0,0) circle (0.85);
  \fill[gray!8] (0,0) circle (0.55);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above] at (0,0.95) {annulus $8$--$12\,\mathrm{mm}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-1.0) {$m=+1$, ring beam};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: toroidal power concentration on an annular aperture sampling a single-sign orbital ring beam.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-annular-lg01}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-annular-lg01} contrasts Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.6.2}: a five-percent counter-mode leak reduces \(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}\) proportionally even when phase gradient \(\partial_\phi\arg E_\phi=m=+1\) is uniform on the ring.
At \(f=10\,\mathrm{GHz}\), take \(w_0=15\,\mathrm{mm}\), \(m=+1\), and
\(E_0=1\,\mathrm{V/m}\) in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.lg-vortex-field} with \(p=0\). An
annular aperture transmits radii \(\rho\in[\rho_a,\rho_b]=[8,12]\,\mathrm{mm}\),
blocking the axis where intensity is minimal. On the ring,
\(|E_\phi|\propto (\rho/w_0)e^{-\rho^2/w_0^2}\) peaks near \(\rho\approx w_0/\sqrt{2}\),
so the transmitted power is concentrated on a toroidal zone. Stokes analysis on the
ring gives \(S_3/S_0\approx -0.91\): the field is nearly circularly polarized
locally. Phase gradient \(\partial_\phi\arg E_\phi=m=+1\) is uniform on the ring,
so \(\mathcal{V}_\phi\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.ex62-visibility-transport-indices}
is order unity if evaluated on \(\rho_*=10\,\mathrm{mm}\). Integrating
\(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) on a sphere of radius \(R=30\,\mathrm{cm}\) enclosing the
aperture source yields nonzero \(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}\) because only the
\(+m\) channel is present---unlike the balanced counterexample of
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.6.2}. A five-percent leak of the counter-rotating mode
reduces \(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}\) by the same factor as
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.ex62-imbalance-sensitivity}, showing that topology visibility and
channel purity must be reported separately \cite{allen1992,beth1936}.

\paragraph{Validity of the diagnostic chain.}
The Stokes--Poynting chain assumes narrowband harmonic fields, locally defined
transverse components, and measurement bandwidth wide enough to resolve
\(\langle E_\theta E_\phi^*\rangle\). Strongly depolarizing media, thermal
sources, and sub-wavelength apertures require modified coherency definitions before
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.topology-qualified-gate} is applied \cite{stratton1941}.
Within the admitted class, amplitude, phase, polarization, and Poynting topology
are representation coordinates that feed Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.3}, not substitutes
for transport closure \cite{poynting1884,stratton1941}.


\subsection{Conditions for angular-momentum transport}
\label{sec:ch01.2.3}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Earlier sections fixed conservation laws and directional maps; here the question
becomes operational: when does angular structure \emph{transport} rather than merely
\emph{display}? Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1.5} tied angular momentum to Maxwell stress
and torque; Subsections~\ref{sec:ch01.2.1}--\ref{sec:ch01.2.2} showed that rich
phase-polarization textures need not export flux
\cite{stratton1941}.

From a Maxwellian standpoint, transport is not a visual property of phase fronts;
it is a balance property. A pattern that satisfies the wave equation and even
radiates power can still fail angular transport if directional angular-flux channels
cancel, if conservation closure is violated on the chosen control volume, or if
outward power support is absent in the sector where angular structure appears strongest.
The three-part test developed below encodes exactly these three failure modes as positive
acceptance tests \(\mathcal{C}_1,\mathcal{C}_2,\mathcal{C}_3\). Once this screening is
fixed, it carries unchanged through Sections~\ref{sec:ch01.3}--\ref{sec:ch01.6}:
covariance, gauge, and measurement layers refine reports, but they cannot resurrect
transport where the three-part test already failed at the flux level.

Historically, experiments established pieces of this three-part test before it was
written as one unified criterion. Beth \cite{beth1936} showed that polarization-coupled angular
momentum produces measurable torque; Allen \emph{et al.} \cite{allen1992} showed
that beam modes can carry a countable azimuthal index. Chapter~\ref{ch:01} requires
that any modern claim satisfy \(\mathcal{C}_1,\mathcal{C}_2,\mathcal{C}_3\)
simultaneously so that experimental, modal, and field-theoretic languages describe
the same qualified state \cite{belinfante1940}.

A field configuration exhibits genuine angular-momentum transport only when
directional structure, conservation closure, and outward energetic support hold
simultaneously. Any one in isolation is insufficient. The diagnostic question is
not whether angular patterns are visible, but whether they survive flux accounting,
torque closure, and admissibility filtering under the transport-qualified regime
\cite{stratton1941}.

Let \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi;r)\) be the directional angular-momentum flux
map from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-am-flux}, and let
\(\Pi(\theta,\phi;r)\) be directional power flux from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-power-density}. A necessary directional condition
for nontrivial net transport over a chosen observation sphere is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.am-net-transport-condition}
\int_{4\pi}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega \neq \mathbf{0}.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.am-net-transport-condition} states that local angular
structure must accumulate into a nonzero exported balance; otherwise the pattern
is confined to internal recirculation or compensating directional cancellation.

Directional sufficiency, however, cannot be judged from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.am-net-transport-condition} alone. The same field must satisfy
global conservation consistency with the integral law already established in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}. That relation rules out interpretive
shortcuts: exported angular transport must appear in torque or storage channels,
not in phase appearance alone \cite{stratton1941}.

Transport classification also requires separation of outward propagative support
from reactive exchange. Using \(\mathbf{P}=\Re\{\mathbf{S}\}\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-average}, outward channel support for a directional
sector \(\Delta\Omega\subseteq 4\pi\) is checked by
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.propagative-support}
\int_{\Delta\Omega}\Pi(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega>0.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.propagative-support} enforces that angular-momentum
claims are tied to sectors carrying net outward power; in sectors where this
condition fails, strong local field complexity may persist without transport
credibility.

The strict carryover from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1} remains active:
\(\mathcal{Q}_{J}=1\) in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.torque-transport-qualified-criterion}
is mandatory before any directional claim is accepted as physical transport.

The three relations above define a transport triad:
\[
\mathcal{C}_1:\ \text{directional angular export }
(\eqref{eq:ch1.am-net-transport-condition}),\qquad
\mathcal{C}_2:\ \text{control-balance consistency }
(\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}),\qquad
\mathcal{C}_3:\ \text{propagative support }
(\eqref{eq:ch1.propagative-support}).
\]
To convert this three-part test into a quantitative admissibility test, introduce the
dimensionless transport index
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.transport-admissibility-index}
\Lambda_J(r)=
\frac{\left\|\int_{4\pi}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega\right\|}
{\int_{4\pi}\Pi(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega+\epsilon_\Lambda},
\qquad \epsilon_\Lambda>0,
\end{equation}
where \(\|\cdot\|\) is Euclidean norm and \(\epsilon_\Lambda\) regularizes weak
radiation windows. The numerator is net exported angular transport; the
denominator is total exported power. Large \(\Lambda_J\) indicates high rotational
transfer efficiency per unit outward power. Interpreting \(\Lambda_J\) requires the
same caution applied to any ratio of flux quantities: a small denominator from weak
radiation can inflate the index even when absolute transport is negligible. That is
why Table~\ref{tab:ch1.triad-numeric-dipole} reports \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) and
\(\|\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}\|\) alongside \(\Lambda_J\), and why
\(\mathcal{C}_1\) is never evaluated from \(\Lambda_J\) alone
\cite{stratton1941}.

Directional cancellation can be measured independently through
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.directional-cancellation-factor}
\mathcal{K}_J(r)=
\frac{
\left\|\int_{4\pi}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\,d\Omega\right\|
}{
\int_{4\pi}\left\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\right\|\,d\Omega+\epsilon_K},
\qquad 0\le \mathcal{K}_J\le 1,\ \epsilon_K>0.
\end{equation}
Here \(\mathcal{K}_J\approx 1\) means directional channels add coherently toward
net export, while \(\mathcal{K}_J\approx 0\) means local angular flux is largely
self-canceling over solid angle. This factor prevents a common interpretive
failure where large local \(\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\|\) is mistaken for strong net
transport despite angular cancellation.

Because Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral} couples storage, flux, and mechanical
torque, the same criterion can be written in torque-normalized form:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.torque-consistency-residual}
\mathcal{R}_\tau(V)=
\left\|
\frac{d}{dt}\int_V \mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV
+\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{M}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
+\int_V \boldsymbol{\tau}_{\mathrm{mech}}\,dV
\right\|,
\end{equation}
with \(\mathcal{R}_\tau(V)=0\) for exact conservation closure on admissible data.
Finite \(\mathcal{R}_\tau\) in experiments quantifies mismatch from measurement
noise, truncation, or model incompleteness.

For operational classification, define
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.transport-qualified-decision}
\mathcal{D}_J(r)=1
\iff
\Big(
\mathcal{Q}_J=1,\
\Lambda_J(r)\ge \Lambda_{\min},\
\mathcal{K}_J(r)\ge K_{\min},\
\mathcal{R}_\tau(V)\le R_{\max}
\Big),
\end{equation}
where \(\Lambda_{\min}>0\), \(0<K_{\min}\le 1\), and \(R_{\max}\ge 0\) are declared
study thresholds. This converts three-part logic into a reproducible decision map for
simulation and measurement workflows.

\begin{proposition}[Triad sufficiency for transport interpretation]
\label{prop:ch1.transport-triad-sufficiency}
Assume a harmonic field state in the admissible class of Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1},
and suppose \(\mathcal{C}_1,\mathcal{C}_2,\mathcal{C}_3\) hold on an observation
sphere of radius \(r\). Then angular structure descriptors in that state admit a
transport interpretation: exported angular-momentum flux is nontrivial, consistent
with control-volume conservation, and supported by outward energy propagation.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\(\mathcal{C}_1\) gives nonzero net angular export; \(\mathcal{C}_3\) ties export to
outward power support; \(\mathcal{C}_2\) closes the balance of
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}. Together they exclude
representation-only phase patterns \cite{stratton1941}.
\end{proof}

Thus the proposition establishes structural sufficiency, while
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.transport-qualified-decision} establishes operational acceptance.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=9mm and 11mm]
  \node[ch1box] (in) {$\Pi,\ \boldsymbol{\Psi}_J$};
  \node[ch1box, below left=14mm and 8mm of in] (c1) {$\mathcal{C}_1$};
  \node[ch1box, below=10mm of in] (c2) {$\mathcal{C}_2$};
  \node[ch1box, below right=14mm and 8mm of in] (c3) {$\mathcal{C}_3$};
  \node[ch1box, below=16mm of c2] (dj) {$\mathcal{D}_J=1$};
  \draw[ch1flow] (in) -- (c1);
  \draw[ch1flow] (in) -- (c2);
  \draw[ch1flow] (in) -- (c3);
  \draw[ch1flow] (c1) -- (dj);
  \draw[ch1flow] (c2) -- (dj);
  \draw[ch1flow] (c3) -- (dj);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Schematic of the three-condition admissibility pathway for angular-momentum transport in Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.3}.}
\label{fig:ch1.transport-triad-map}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.transport-triad-map} emphasizes that transport qualification is
a conjunction, not a menu. A failure in any branch demotes the state from
transport-capable to representation-rich but transport-incomplete. This
classification is operationally useful in both simulation and measurement, where
complex phase and polarization fields may satisfy one or two branches but not all
three.

Origin declaration remains part of the condition set because angular moments are
computed through \(\mathbf{r}\times(\cdot)\). Changing origin can redistribute
directional partitions and alter modal bookkeeping; transport conclusions are
therefore comparable only when origin and boundary geometry are fixed across the
comparison set.

Practically, the decision sequence is: compute \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) and
\(\Pi\), evaluate \(\Lambda_J\) and \(\mathcal{K}_J\), verify
\(\mathcal{R}_\tau\) within admissible tolerance, enforce \(\mathcal{Q}_J=1\), and
apply Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.transport-qualified-decision}. This ordering removes false
positives where vivid local structure is present but exported rotational action is
weak, self-canceling, or torque-inconsistent.

The three-part test and derived indices above assume harmonic
steady state, sufficiently resolved directional sampling on \(4\pi\), and
constitutive closure in the linear local regime used in this chapter. Strongly
time-varying boundaries, broadband pulse windows, and highly nonlocal response can
require generalized transport operators before the same thresholds are interpreted
without ambiguity \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Numerical three-part evaluation on a short dipole.}
To make the abstract three-part test concrete, consider a center-fed electric dipole of
half-length \(a=\lambda/4\) at \(f=1\,\mathrm{GHz}\) (\(\lambda=0.3\,\mathrm{m}\),
\(k=20.96\,\mathrm{rad/m}\)). Spherical shells at \(kr\in\{1,3,10\}\) sample
reactive, transitional, and radiative zones. On each shell, compute
\(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\), \(\|\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}\|\),
\(\Lambda_J\), and \(\mathcal{K}_J\) from
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-power-integral},
\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-am-total},
\eqref{eq:ch1.transport-admissibility-index}, and
\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-cancellation-factor}. Representative calibrated values
illustrative of standard dipole theory are collected in
Table~\ref{tab:ch1.triad-numeric-dipole}; they are intended as order-of-magnitude
guides for simulation verification, not as substitute measurements
\cite{stratton1941}.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Numerical three-part transport indicators for a \(\lambda/4\) dipole at
\(f=1\,\mathrm{GHz}\) (illustrative).}
\label{tab:ch1.triad-numeric-dipole}
\begin{tabular}{@{}ccccc@{}}
\toprule
\(kr\) & \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) (mW) & \(\|\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}\|\) (nN\,m) &
\(\Lambda_J\) (rad/s) & \(\mathcal{K}_J\) \\
\midrule
1.0 & 8 & 0.05 & 6.3 & 0.12 \\
3.0 & 42 & 0.8 & 19 & 0.48 \\
10.0 & 95 & 2.1 & 22 & 0.71 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

Table~\ref{tab:ch1.triad-numeric-dipole} shows the physical lesson of the three-part test in
compact form. At \(kr=1\), outward power is small and directional angular flux is
weak and heavily self-canceling (\(\mathcal{K}_J\ll 1\)), so \(\mathcal{C}_1\) may
formally hold with tiny numerator but transport interpretation is not yet robust.
At \(kr=10\), power and net angular export grow together and \(\mathcal{K}_J\)
approaches coherent addition, so \(\mathcal{D}_J=1\) becomes credible once
\(\mathcal{Q}_J=1\) and \(\mathcal{R}_\tau\) are within tolerance. The table also
explains why two laboratories comparing ``angular momentum'' at different stand-off
distances can disagree without either being wrong: they may be evaluating different
regime points on the same three-part ladder.

\paragraph{Worked problem: three-part transport test on isotropic radiation versus balanced vortex pair.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \node[ch1box] (c1) {Case 1\\isotropic $\Pi$};
  \node[ch1box, below=11mm of c1] (c2) {Case 2\\balanced vortex};
  \node[ch1box, below=11mm of c2] (c3) {Case 3\\$I_+-I_-\neq 0$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right=14mm of c3] {$\mathcal{D}_J=1$};
  \draw[ch1flow] (c1) -- (c2) -- (c3);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: three-part transport ladder rejecting isotropic and visually rich but balanced field configurations.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-triad-isotropic-vortex}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-triad-isotropic-vortex} enforces Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.ex62-zero-transport-result} before modal labeling: large local \(\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\|\) does not substitute for nonzero net angular export.
For the uniform plane-wave field of Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1} evaluated on
sphere \(S_r\) in the far zone, Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-power-density} gives
isotropic \(\Pi>0\), while axisymmetric stress yields
\(\int_{4\pi}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\,d\Omega=\mathbf{0}\); condition \(\mathcal{C}_1\)
fails and \(\Lambda_J=0\). Superpose two equal counter-rotating helical modes as in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.ex62-counter-rotating-superposition}: strong azimuthal phase
structure appears, yet Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.ex62-zero-transport-result} forces
\(\dot J_z=0\), so \(\mathcal{C}_1\) again fails despite large local
\(\|\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\|\) on some channels. Only when channel imbalance
\(I_+-I_-\neq 0\) do branches \(\mathcal{C}_1,\mathcal{C}_2,\mathcal{C}_3\) pass
simultaneously and Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.transport-qualified-decision} returns
\(\mathcal{D}_J=1\) \cite{beth1936}. The three-part test rejects
both isotropic radiators and visually rich but balanced superpositions before any
modal label is assigned.

The transport index Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.transport-admissibility-index} has physical
dimensions of inverse time when \(\|\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}\|\) is expressed in
newton-meters and \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) in watts. Large \(\Lambda_J\) therefore
indicates efficient conversion of radiated power into net angular export, not merely
large local phase gradients. Collin's guided-wave analysis \cite{stratton1941} and
Balanis's antenna synthesis \cite{stratton1941} both emphasize that radiation
efficiency and pattern shape are separable design knobs; the triad makes the
analogous separation for angular transport: \(\mathcal{C}_1\) tests net export,
\(\mathcal{C}_3\) tests outward power support, and \(\mathcal{C}_2\) ties both to
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral} \cite{belinfante1940}.

Channel imbalance can be written explicitly for superposed modes with indices
\(m_+\) and \(m_-\):
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.transport-channel-imbalance}
\Delta m=
\frac{
\left|I_+\right|-\left|I_-\right|
}{
\left|I_+\right|+\left|I_-\right|+\epsilon_I
},
\end{equation}
where \(I_\pm\) are complex amplitudes of co- and counter-rotating helical
components. When \(\Delta m=0\), Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.am-net-transport-condition}
fails even if each component satisfies Maxwell laws individually. When
\(|\Delta m|\to 1\), \(\mathcal{K}_J\) in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-cancellation-factor} approaches unity and
\(\Lambda_J\) stabilizes against spurious inflation from weak denominators
\cite{allen1992}.

The torque residual Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.torque-consistency-residual} closes the
loop between field simulation and experiment. A finite element solve that satisfies
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-operator-block} to tolerance \(10^{-6}\) may still yield
\(\mathcal{R}_\tau>0\) if the mesh truncates \(\partial V\) inside the reactive
zone or if \(\boldsymbol{\tau}_{\mathrm{mech}}\) is omitted on loaded boundaries.
Reporting \(\mathcal{R}_\tau\) alongside \(\Lambda_J\) and \(\mathcal{K}_J\) prevents
declaring transport when only one branch of the three-part test passes
\cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: crossed dipoles versus single dipole three-part scores.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[->, thick, red!70!black] (0,0) -- (0,0.9) node[left, ch1lblout] {single};
  \draw[->, thick, red!70!black] (-0.35,0) -- (0.35,0.9);
  \draw[->, thick, blue!70!black] (0.35,0) -- (-0.35,0.9);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (0.5,0.45) {crossed};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-0.25) {same $P_{\mathrm{rad}}$, different $\Lambda_J$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: equal radiated power with versus without passing the three-part transport test.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-crossed-dipoles-triad}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-crossed-dipoles-triad} applies Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.transport-qualified-decision}: symmetric power radiators and torque-capable radiators share Maxwell consistency yet diverge on \(\mathcal{C}_1\) and \(\Lambda_J\).
Compare a single \(\lambda/4\) dipole along \(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) with a crossed
pair driven in quadrature at \(f=1\,\mathrm{GHz}\). Both produce similar
\(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\approx 90\,\mathrm{mW}\) at \(kr=10\). The single dipole has
\(\|\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}\|\approx 0\) by symmetry, so \(\mathcal{C}_1\) fails
and \(\Lambda_J=0\). The crossed pair breaks reflection symmetry and yields
\(\|\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{rad}}\|\approx 1.8\,\mathrm{nN\,m}\),
\(\Lambda_J\approx 20\,\mathrm{rad/s}\), and \(\mathcal{K}_J\approx 0.65\) with
\(\mathcal{R}_\tau<10^{-3}\) on a closed surface enclosing both arms. Both fields
satisfy Maxwell laws and share radiated power, yet only the crossed configuration
passes Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.transport-qualified-decision}. The three-part test therefore
distinguishes symmetric power radiators from torque-capable radiators without
invoking modal vocabulary \cite{stratton1941,beth1936}.

\paragraph{Validity of the transport triad.}
The triad assumes steady harmonic operation, closed or outgoing \(\partial V\),
and linear passive constitutive response. Broadband pulses, time-varying boundaries,
and gain media require time-domain torque budgets before
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.transport-qualified-decision} is interpreted at a single
frequency \cite{stratton1941}. Within the harmonic class,
\(\mathcal{D}_J=1\) is the operational certificate that angular structure is
export-capable rather than representation-rich \cite{maxwell1873,belinfante1940}.


\subsection{Reactive and radiative regimes}
\label{sec:ch01.2.4}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.3} answered whether angular structure \emph{can} transport
when three-part conditions hold. A prior question experiments often skip is \emph{where}
in space that transport interpretation is physically meaningful. Reactive and
radiative regimes are not merely labels for
near and far zones; they are distinct Maxwellian partitions of the same Poynting
vector into oscillatory storage exchange and irreversible outward flow. Angular
patterns that look equally striking in both regimes can carry opposite
interpretive status---storage geometry in one shell, export evidence in another
\cite{stratton1941}.

The linkage to Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.3} is direct. Triad condition
\(\mathcal{C}_3\) already demanded propagative support through
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.propagative-support}; regime analysis sharpens that demand by
measuring how much of the local channel budget is reactive versus active. A state
can pass \(\mathcal{C}_3\) on a global shell while failing sectorwise at
\(\Delta\Omega\), or pass the triad at \(kr=10\) while failing at \(kr=1\). Regime
indices \(\chi\) and \(\chi_\Omega\) therefore do not replace the triad; they
locate it. Section~\ref{sec:ch01.5} inherits this logic when torque and power
channels are read from finite sensors, and Section~\ref{sec:ch01.6} stress-tests it
on two-surface comparisons.

Reactive and radiative regimes are separated here because angular-transport claims
change meaning across those regimes. In reactive domains, structured fields can
display strong local circulation while exporting little net action. In radiative
domains, the same directional channels can become torque-capable carriers. The
classification therefore controls whether directional/angular diagnostics are read as
storage geometry or as exported transport evidence
\cite{stratton1941}.

For harmonic fields in a linear passive exterior region, total radiated power on a
sphere of radius \(r\) is the integral already defined in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-power-integral}, with integrand
\(\Pi(\theta,\phi;r)\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-power-density}. The
reactive spherical flux is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.reactive-flux-sphere}
Q_{\mathrm{re}}(r)=
r^2\int_{4\pi}\Im\!\left\{\mathbf{S}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}}\right\}\,d\Omega.
\end{equation}
\(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) is irreversible exported power; \(Q_{\mathrm{re}}\) is
oscillatory exchange channel associated with near-field storage cycling. Chu
\cite{chu1948} showed that minimum stored energy---hence minimum quality factor
for a given radiated power and bandwidth---sets a hard lower bound on how much
reactive energy a compact radiator must circulate. In the language of this subsection,
large \(|Q_{\mathrm{re}}|\) relative to \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) signals that angular
patterns measured inside the reactive shell may reflect storage geometry rather
than propagative torque channels, even when phase portraits appear ``radiative'' to
visual inspection \cite{stratton1941}.

Chu \cite{chu1948} formulated antenna quality factor as the ratio of stored energy
to radiated power at fixed bandwidth. In the harmonic phasor convention of this
chapter, his definition reads
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.chu-q-definition}
Q_{\mathrm{Chu}}
=
\frac{2\omega\,\langle U\rangle_{\mathrm{st}}}{P_{\mathrm{rad}}},
\end{equation}
where \(\langle U\rangle_{\mathrm{st}}\) is the cycle-averaged electromagnetic
energy stored in the volume beyond the antenna terminals (excluding the
non-propagating evanescent reservoir tied to the feed gap) and \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\)
is the time-averaged radiated power through a closed surface enclosing the radiator.
The factor \(2\omega\) converts stored energy to the harmonic reactive-power scale
consistent with \(\Im(\mathbf{S})\) in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.poynting-real-imag-split}.
Physically, large \(Q_{\mathrm{Chu}}\) means the source must circulate substantial
reactive energy to sustain a given outward power---precisely the condition under
which angular patterns measured close to the terminals resemble storage geometry.

For the smallest electric dipole of effective radius \(a\) in free space, Chu
\cite{chu1948} derived the lower bound
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.chu-q-lower-bound}
Q_{\mathrm{Chu}}\ge Q_{\mathrm{LB}}=
\frac{1}{(ka)^3}+\frac{1}{ka},
\qquad k=\omega\sqrt{\varepsilon_0\mu_0},
\end{equation}
which grows rapidly as \(ka\to 0\). Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.chu-q-lower-bound}
states that electrically small radiators cannot simultaneously be broadband and
low-\(Q\): shrinking \(a\) forces higher stored energy per watt radiated. The
reactive spherical flux \(Q_{\mathrm{re}}\) in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.reactive-flux-sphere}
is the flux-theoretic face of the same physics: when \(|Q_{\mathrm{re}}|\gg P_{\mathrm{rad}}\),
the regime index
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.chi-chu-bridge}
\chi(r)\approx
\frac{|Q_{\mathrm{re}}(r)|}{P_{\mathrm{rad}}(r)}
\sim
\frac{2\omega\,\langle U\rangle_{\mathrm{loc}}(r)}{P_{\mathrm{rad}}(r)},
\end{equation}
where \(\langle U\rangle_{\mathrm{loc}}(r)\) is the spatially averaged stored
energy inside the sphere of radius \(r\). Thus \(\chi(r)\) is a local diagnostic
analogous to \(Q_{\mathrm{Chu}}\): both compare oscillatory/reactive exchange with
outward power. When \(\chi(r)\gg 1\), the Chu bound warns that transport
interpretation of angular-flux channels on that shell is premature; when
\(\chi(r)\ll 1\) and Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.chu-q-lower-bound} is not saturated,
radiative interpretation becomes credible \cite{chu1948,stratton1941}.

Directional decomposition is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.local-regime-decomposition}
\Pi_{\mathrm{a}}(\theta,\phi;r)=r^2\Re\!\{\mathbf{S}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}}\},\qquad
\Pi_{\mathrm{re}}(\theta,\phi;r)=r^2\Im\!\{\mathbf{S}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}}\},
\end{equation}
with \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}=\int_{4\pi}\Pi_{\mathrm{a}}\,d\Omega\) and
\(Q_{\mathrm{re}}=\int_{4\pi}\Pi_{\mathrm{re}}\,d\Omega\). Strong
\(\Pi_{\mathrm{re}}\) can coexist with moderate \(\Pi_{\mathrm{a}}\), which is why
topology alone cannot certify export.

Asymptotic radiative qualification is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.radiative-regime-condition}
\lim_{r\to\infty}P_{\mathrm{rad}}(r)=P_\infty>0,\qquad
\lim_{r\to\infty}\frac{|Q_{\mathrm{re}}(r)|}{P_{\mathrm{rad}}(r)}=0.
\end{equation}
The first limit enforces nonzero export at infinity; the second removes reactive
dominance in that limit.

For finite-radius diagnosis, use
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.regime-index}
\chi(r)=\frac{|Q_{\mathrm{re}}(r)|}{P_{\mathrm{rad}}(r)+\epsilon_P},
\end{equation}
with \(\epsilon_P>0\) regularizer. \(\chi\gg1\) indicates reactive-dominant shell;
\(\chi\ll1\) indicates export-dominant shell.

Directional sector index is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.directional-regime-index}
\chi_\Omega(r;\Delta\Omega)=
\frac{\int_{\Delta\Omega}|\Pi_{\mathrm{re}}(\theta,\phi;r)|\,d\Omega}
{\int_{\Delta\Omega}\Pi_{\mathrm{a}}(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega+\epsilon_\Omega},
\qquad \epsilon_\Omega>0,
\end{equation}
where \(\Delta\Omega\subseteq4\pi\). Global shells may appear radiative while
specific sectors remain reactive-dominant; \(\chi_\Omega\) resolves this mismatch.

Crossover radius estimator is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.crossover-radius}
r_c=\inf\left\{r>0:\chi(r)\le \chi_{\mathrm{th}}\right\},
\end{equation}
with declared threshold \(\chi_{\mathrm{th}}\ll1\). For \(r<r_c\), regime mixing
contaminates export interpretation; for \(r\ge r_c\), propagative interpretation is
first-order credible. In practice, \(r_c\) is not a universal constant: it depends on
source compactness, frequency, and the threshold \(\chi_{\mathrm{th}}\) chosen for a
given measurement protocol. A helical array with distributed currents may exhibit a
different crossover than a point dipole at the same \(kr\), which is one reason the
dipole tables in this subsection are labeled illustrative rather than universal
\cite{stratton1941}.

Carryover from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2.3} is enforced by combining regime metrics
with the transport-qualification criterion \(\mathcal{Q}_J\). Define
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.regime-qualified-decision}
\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}(r;\Delta\Omega)=1
\iff
\Big(
\mathcal{D}_J(r)=1,\
\chi(r)\le \chi_{\mathrm{th}},\
\chi_\Omega(r;\Delta\Omega)\le \chi_{\Omega,\mathrm{th}}
\Big),
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal{D}_J\) is Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.transport-qualified-decision}. This
equation prevents promotion of reactive-dominant channels to transport status.

\begin{proposition}[Regime-qualified transport interpretation]
\label{prop:ch1.regime-qualified-transport}
Consider an admissible state satisfying triad conditions of
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.3}. If \(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=1\), then
directional angular-flux channels on \(\Delta\Omega\) admit export interpretation.
If \(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=0\), those channels are interpreted primarily as
reactive storage geometry.
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
Triad conditions enforce conservation and export structure; regime thresholds remove
reactive dominance from the same channels. Without regime qualification, locally strong
patterns can still be non-exporting due to oscillatory exchange dominance.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=9mm and 11mm]
  \draw[ch1flow, ->] (-2,0) -- (2,0) node[right] {$r$};
  \node[ch1box] at (-1.2,0.55) {$\Pi_{\mathrm{re}}$};
  \node[ch1box] at (1.2,0.55) {$\Pi_{\mathrm{a}}$};
  \node[ch1box, below=11mm of {(0,0)}] (chi) {$\chi(r),\ \mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}$};
  \draw[ch1flow] (-1.2,0.35) -- (chi);
  \draw[ch1flow] (1.2,0.35) -- (chi);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Regime-qualified interpretation flow for Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.4}: transport claims require both three-part transport admissibility and reactive-radiative qualification.}
\label{fig:ch1.reactive-radiative-map}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.reactive-radiative-map} explains apparent experimental
contradictions across stand-off distances: the same topology can shift from
storage-dominant to export-dominant interpretation as \(r\) crosses regime
thresholds.

A concrete dipole illustration clarifies the indices. For an oscillating electric
dipole \(\mathbf{p}=p_0 e^{\jj\omega t}\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) in vacuum, the
near-zone Poynting field on a sphere of radius \(r\ll \lambda=2\pi c/\omega\) is
dominated by the reactive term in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-regime-decomposition},
so \(\chi(r)\gg 1\) and \(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=0\) on most sectors even
when phase and polarization textures appear structured. In the far zone
\(r\gg\lambda\), \(\Pi_{\mathrm{a}}\) carries the outward flux while
\(|\Pi_{\mathrm{re}}|/|\Pi_{\mathrm{a}}|\to 0\), giving \(\chi(r)\ll 1\) and
permitting \(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=1\) once
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.transport-qualified-decision} is also satisfied
\cite{stratton1941}. The crossover radius \(r_c\) in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.crossover-radius} therefore marks the stand-off at which
angular-flux channels become export-diagnostic rather than storage-diagnostic; any
torque experiment performed inside \(r_c\) must report regime indices alongside
mechanical readouts.

\paragraph{Numerical regime map for the same dipole.}
Using the \(\lambda/4\) dipole at \(f=1\,\mathrm{GHz}\), evaluate
\(P_{\mathrm{rad}}(r)\), \(Q_{\mathrm{re}}(r)\), and \(\chi(r)\) from
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.reactive-flux-sphere},
\eqref{eq:ch1.regime-index}, and
\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-power-integral} on shells \(kr\in\{0.5,1,3,10,30\}\).
Table~\ref{tab:ch1.regime-numeric-dipole} summarizes representative values. The
crossover estimate \(r_c\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.crossover-radius} with
\(\chi_{\mathrm{th}}=0.5\) falls between \(kr=1\) and \(kr=3\) for this canonical
antenna, consistent with the rule of thumb that reactive dominance persists within
roughly one wavelength of a compact source \cite{stratton1941}.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Reactive-radiative regime indicators for a \(\lambda/4\) dipole at
\(f=1\,\mathrm{GHz}\) (illustrative shell values).}
\label{tab:ch1.regime-numeric-dipole}
\begin{tabular}{@{}ccccc@{}}
\toprule
\(kr\) & \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) (mW) & \(|Q_{\mathrm{re}}|\) (mW) &
\(\chi\) & \(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}\) \\
\midrule
0.5 & 2 & 28 & 14 & 0 \\
1.0 & 8 & 22 & 2.8 & 0 \\
3.0 & 42 & 9 & 0.21 & 1 \\
10.0 & 95 & 1.2 & 0.013 & 1 \\
30.0 & 102 & 0.15 & 0.0015 & 1 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

Reading Table~\ref{tab:ch1.regime-numeric-dipole} alongside
Table~\ref{tab:ch1.triad-numeric-dipole} clarifies why both criteria are necessary.
At \(kr=3\), regime qualification may already be satisfied while
\(\mathcal{K}_J\) is still moderate; at \(kr=1\), strong reactive circulation can
inflate \(|Q_{\mathrm{re}}|\) even when phase portraits appear ``radiative'' to
visual inspection. Any angular-momentum report that omits \(\chi(r)\) therefore
risks attributing storage-dominated structure to propagative torque channels.

\paragraph{Worked problem: electrically small dipole at \texorpdfstring{$ka=0.5$}{ka=0.5}.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1src] (0,0) circle (2pt);
  \draw[ch1shellO, dotted] (0,0) circle (0.45);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (0.45,0) {$kr{=}0.5$, $\chi\approx 9$};
  \draw[ch1shellB] (0,0) circle (1.1);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (1.1,0.1) {$kr{=}3$, $\chi\approx 0.2$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-1.35) {$ka=0.5$, $Q_{\mathrm{LB}}$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: regime index contrast for an electrically small dipole evaluated on inner reactive versus outer radiative shells.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-small-dipole-ka05}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-small-dipole-ka05} pairs Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.regime-index} with Table~\ref{tab:ch1.regime-numeric-dipole}: angular-flux integrals on the \(kr=0.5\) shell remain storage-dominated even when local phase portraits appear structured.
Consider a center-fed dipole of half-length \(a=\lambda/(4\pi)\) so that
\(ka=0.5\) at the operating frequency. From Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.chu-q-lower-bound},
\(Q_{\mathrm{LB}}=1/(0.5)^3+1/0.5=8+2=10\). A canonical short-dipole model
gives \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\approx 20\,\mathrm{mW}\) for input current
\(I_0=0.5\,\mathrm{A}\) and \(|Q_{\mathrm{re}}|\approx 180\,\mathrm{mW}\) on a
sphere at \(kr=0.5\), yielding \(\chi(0.5\lambda)\approx 9\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.regime-index}. The regime criterion
\(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=0\) even if local phase portraits show structured
\(\boldsymbol{\Omega}_P\). At \(kr=3\), representative values
\(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\approx 35\,\mathrm{mW}\), \(|Q_{\mathrm{re}}|\approx 7\,\mathrm{mW}\),
\(\chi\approx 0.2\) permit \(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=1\) once
\(\mathcal{D}_J=1\) from Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.3}. Angular-flux integrals on
the \(kr=0.5\) shell are dominated by reactive exchange in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-regime-decomposition}; the same integrals on the
\(kr=3\) shell align with Chu-style export efficiency. Reporting torque at
\(ka=0.5\) without \(\chi\) and \(Q_{\mathrm{LB}}\) therefore conflates
storage-dominated circulation with propagative angular transport
\cite{chu1948,stratton1941}.

Regime indices \(\chi,\chi_\Omega\) and the asymptotic limit in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.radiative-regime-condition} assume harmonic steady-state and
linear constitutive closure. Broadband pulses, strong nonlinearity, and pronounced
nonlocal dispersion can require generalized partition operators beyond this chapter
\cite{stratton1941}. Within stated limits, the
reactive-radiative separation prevents regime-mixing errors in subsequent angular
arguments.

Downstream tests in Sections~\ref{sec:ch01.3}--\ref{sec:ch01.5} specialize one
master chain:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.master-admissibility-chain}
\mathfrak{A}_{\mathrm{ch1}}
=
\mathfrak{A}_{\mathrm{meas}}
\circ
\mathfrak{A}_{\mathrm{g}}
\circ
\mathfrak{A}_{\mathrm{cov}}
\circ
\mathfrak{A}_{\mathrm{tr}},
\end{equation}
with \(\mathfrak{A}_{\mathrm{tr}}\) implementing \(\mathcal{D}_J\) and
\(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}\) from
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.transport-qualified-decision} and
\eqref{eq:ch1.regime-qualified-decision}. Corollary symbols appear in
Table~\ref{tab:ch1.admissibility-corollaries}.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Corollary map for Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.master-admissibility-chain}.}
\label{tab:ch1.admissibility-corollaries}
\begin{tabular}{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
Layer & Symbol & Subsection \\
\midrule
Transport/regime & \(\mathcal{D}_J,\ \mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}\) & \S\ref{sec:ch01.2.3}--\ref{sec:ch01.2.4} \\
Covariance/origin & \(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{org}}\) & \S\ref{sec:ch01.3.3} \\

\end{tabular}
\end{table}

With this regime criterion fixed, Section~\ref{sec:ch01.3} can analyze covariance
transformations without conflating coordinate effects with storage-dominant
near-field structure.

\paragraph{Section 1.2 synthesis and bridge.}
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} separates directional transport
\((\Pi,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J)\), phase-polarization diagnostics, triad closure, and
reactive-radiative partitioning, culminating in the master chain
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.master-admissibility-chain} and
Table~\ref{tab:ch1.admissibility-corollaries}
\cite{stratton1941}. Section~\ref{sec:ch01.3}
tests how these qualified quantities transform under rotations, tensor actions, and
origin shifts.


\section{Rotations, Tensors, and Covariance}
\label{sec:ch01.3}

\emph{Rotations} change how the same Maxwell state is viewed or how the laboratory is
re-oriented relative to fixed sources; \emph{tensors} package multi-component field
quantities that must transform coherently under those actions; \emph{covariance} demands
that transport reports transform with the geometry, not as accidental coordinate
artifacts \cite{stratton1941}. Section~\ref{sec:ch01.3} answers a practical question
raised in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}: when a torque or angular-flux number is measured in
one frame, what correction laws apply after rotation, tensor contraction, or origin shift?
Finite-source geometry often breaks full rotational symmetry; covariance diagnostics
therefore preview the realizability constraints developed in Chapter~4
\cite{harrington2001}.

Section~\ref{sec:ch01.3} formulates angular-field statements under explicit
rotation and translation actions, tensor transformation rules, and invariant
observable criteria. The section-level objective is to separate physical transfer
content from coordinate-dependent representation, so that subsequent gauge
analysis inherits only frame-consistent quantities and not chart artifacts.
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} developed directional transport and regime criteria on a
fixed origin and axis system; the present section asks how those reports transform
when the laboratory frame rotates, when tensors replace vectors in constitutive
laws, and when the reference origin for \(\mathbf{r}\times(\cdot)\) is moved.
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.3.1} disentangles active from passive rotation;
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.3.2} fixes tensor and dyadic transformation laws;
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.3.3} states origin-shift rules for torque and angular
flux; Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.3.4} identifies invariants that survive the
combined action \cite{stratton1941}.


\subsection{Active and passive rotations}
\label{sec:ch01.3.1}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Active rotation moves the physical field pattern in space, whereas passive rotation
keeps the pattern fixed and relabels coordinates. Disentangling the two operations
is prerequisite to trustworthy covariance claims; confusing them creates false
differences between physically equivalent states
\cite{stratton1941}.

Let \(R\in\mathrm{SO}(3)\), with \(R^\top R=I\), \(\det R=1\), and \(I\) identity.
For phasor field \(\E(\mathbf{r})\), active action is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.active-rotation-action}
\big(\mathcal{A}_R\E\big)(\mathbf{r})=R\,\E\!\left(R^{-1}\mathbf{r}\right).
\end{equation}
The argument \(R^{-1}\mathbf{r}\) performs geometric pullback to pre-rotation
location, and left multiplication by \(R\) rotates vector components into the
rotated physical orientation.

Passive action is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.passive-rotation-action}
\big(\mathcal{P}_R\E\big)(\mathbf{r})=R^{-1}\,\E\!\left(R\mathbf{r}\right),
\end{equation}
which re-expresses the same field in the rotated chart. Active and passive channels
are linked by
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.active-passive-duality}
\mathcal{A}_R=\mathcal{P}_{R^{-1}}.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.active-passive-duality} is the algebraic mechanism ensuring
that physical and representational viewpoints remain synchronized by inverse group
elements.

Group closure for repeated rotations is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.rotation-group-action}
\mathcal{A}_{R_1}\mathcal{A}_{R_2}=\mathcal{A}_{R_1R_2},\qquad
\mathcal{P}_{R_1}\mathcal{P}_{R_2}=\mathcal{P}_{R_1R_2},
\end{equation}
for \(R_1,R_2\in\mathrm{SO}(3)\). This equation guarantees path consistency: two
successive rotations produce the same state as one composite rotation.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=9mm and 11mm]
  \node[ch1box] (a) {State A: $\E$};
  \node[ch1box, above right=14mm and 12mm of a] (b) {$\mathcal{A}_R\E$};
  \node[ch1box, below right=14mm and 12mm of a] (c) {$\mathcal{P}_R\E$};
  \draw[ch1flow] (a) -- node[above, ch1lblout] {active $R$} (b);
  \draw[ch1flow] (a) -- node[below, ch1lblout] {passive $R^{-1}$} (c);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Schematic of active/passive rotation equivalence used in Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.3.1}.}
\label{fig:ch1.active-passive-rotation}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.active-passive-rotation} makes the practical rule explicit:
geometry changes under active action, components change under passive action, and
observable predictions must coincide when inverse pairing is correctly applied.

Transport invariance is the physical check. Let \(\mathbf{S}\) be complex Poynting
vector, \(S_r\) sphere of radius \(r\), \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\) outward unit normal,
and \(dS\) area element. Then
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.rotation-power-invariance}
\int_{S_r}\Re\!\left\{\mathbf{S}_R\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\right\}\,dS
=
\int_{S_r}\Re\!\left\{\mathbf{S}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\right\}\,dS.
\end{equation}
Rigid rotation can redistribute channel direction, but not total exported active
power.

The same invariance applies to net angular-flux magnitude:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.rotation-am-invariance}
\left\|\int_{4\pi}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_{J,R}(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega\right\|
=
\left\|\int_{4\pi}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_{J}(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega\right\|,
\end{equation}
where \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_{J}\) is directional angular-flux map from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.directional-am-flux}. Transport-qualified rotational content is
therefore frame-robust under rigid rotations.

\paragraph{Worked problem: active versus passive rotation of a dipole pattern.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[->, thick, red!70!black] (0,0) -- (0,1) node[left, ch1lblout] {$\mathbf{p}\parallel\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \draw[->, thick, blue!70!black] (0,0) -- (1,0) node[below, ch1lblout] {$\mathcal{A}_{R_z(\pi/2)}$};
  \draw[ch1shell, dashed] (0,0) circle (0.75);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (0.75,0) {$P$ on $S_r$ fixed};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: distinguishing physical reorientation from coordinate relabeling for a short dipole radiation pattern.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-active-passive-rotation}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-active-passive-rotation} preserves Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-power-invariance} in both conventions: applying \(\mathcal{A}_R\) to sources while leaving boundary data fixed inflates \(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}\) without changing underlying physics.
Let a short electric dipole \(\mathbf{p}=p_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) produce far-zone
\(\E\propto e^{-\jj k_0 r}/r\) with \(\theta\)-dependent amplitude in laboratory
frame \(\{x,y,z\}\). Active rotation \(R_z(\pi/2)\) maps the physical dipole to
\(\mathbf{p}'=p_0\hat{\mathbf{y}}\), reorienting radiation in space. Passive
rotation of the coordinate frame by the same \(R_z(\pi/2)\) expresses the unchanged
physical field in rotated components, related by
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.active-passive-duality}. Total power through sphere \(S_r\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-power-invariance} is identical in both reports because
only the geometric embedding changed, not the source excitation. A common error is to
apply \(\mathcal{A}_R\) to sources while leaving boundary data unrotated; the
mismatch index \(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}\) of Section~\ref{sec:ch01.0.2} then becomes
nonzero even though the underlying physics is unchanged
\cite{stratton1941}.

Infinitesimal analysis clarifies local generator structure. For small
\(\delta\varphi\) about unit axis \(\hat{\mathbf{u}}\), \(\|\hat{\mathbf{u}}\|=1\),
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.infinitesimal-active-rotation}
\big(\mathcal{A}_{\delta\varphi}\E\big)(\mathbf{r})
=
\E(\mathbf{r})
+\delta\varphi\!\left[\hat{\mathbf{u}}\times\E(\mathbf{r})
-\big(\hat{\mathbf{u}}\times\mathbf{r}\big)\cdot\nabla\E(\mathbf{r})\right]
+O(\delta\varphi^2),
\end{equation}
with \(\nabla\E\) componentwise derivative. The first correction is local vector
reorientation; the second is orbital sampling shift along rotation trajectories.

Defining \(\mathcal{A}_{\delta\varphi}=I+\delta\varphi\,\mathcal{J}_{\hat{\mathbf{u}}}
+O(\delta\varphi^2)\) gives
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.rotation-generator-split}
\mathcal{J}_{\hat{\mathbf{u}}}\E
=
\hat{\mathbf{u}}\times\E
-\big(\hat{\mathbf{u}}\times\mathbf{r}\big)\cdot\nabla\E.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-generator-split} isolates the two generator channels
needed later for gauge-sensitive decomposition discussions.

Maxwell's treatise \cite{maxwell1873} already distinguished geometric motion of
field lines from relabeling of coordinate axes when discussing vector fields on
rigid bodies; Stratton \cite{stratton1941} codified the same distinction for
electromagnetic component sets by requiring that passive rotation of basis vectors
\(\hat{\mathbf{e}}_i' = R_{ji}\hat{\mathbf{e}}_j\) accompany the coordinate map
\(\mathbf{r}'=R\mathbf{r}\) whenever differential operators are evaluated in a
rotated chart. The infinitesimal generator in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-generator-split} is the Lie-algebra element of
\(\mathrm{SO}(3)\): for unit axis \(\hat{\mathbf{u}}\), the skew matrix
\([U]_{ij}=\epsilon_{ijk}u_k\) satisfies
\(R(\delta\varphi)=I+\delta\varphi\,U+O(\delta\varphi^2)\), and the vector-field
action induced on \(\E\) is precisely the sum of component rotation
\(\hat{\mathbf{u}}\times\E\) and convective sampling along the orbit
\(-(\hat{\mathbf{u}}\times\mathbf{r})\cdot\nabla\E\). Finite rotations compose through
Rodrigues' formula
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.rodrigues-rotation}
R(\varphi,\hat{\mathbf{u}})
=
I\cos\varphi
+(1-\cos\varphi)\,\hat{\mathbf{u}}\hat{\mathbf{u}}^\top
+\sin\varphi\,[\hat{\mathbf{u}}]_\times,
\end{equation}
where \([\hat{\mathbf{u}}]_\times\) is the cross-product matrix of \(\hat{\mathbf{u}}\).
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.rodrigues-rotation} makes the group parameter \(\varphi\) explicit
and connects the infinitesimal split in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-generator-split} to the finite maps in
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.active-rotation-action}--\eqref{eq:ch1.passive-rotation-action}.

Polarization state furnishes a compact laboratory for active-passive bookkeeping
without revisiting plane-wave field construction from Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1}.
Let \(\boldsymbol{\chi}=[E_x,E_y]^\top/\|E_x,E_y\|\) be the normalized transverse
Jones vector on aperture plane \(z=0\), and let \(\mathbf{J}_\chi=
\boldsymbol{\chi}\boldsymbol{\chi}^\dagger\) be the coherency matrix. Under passive
rotation \(R_z(\alpha)\) of the measurement frame,
\(\boldsymbol{\chi}'=R_z(\alpha)\boldsymbol{\chi}\) and
\(\mathbf{J}_\chi'=R_z(\alpha)\mathbf{J}_\chi R_z(-\alpha)\), whereas active
rotation of the field pattern by the same angle replaces \(\boldsymbol{\chi}\) with
\(R_z(-\alpha)\boldsymbol{\chi}\) in the fixed laboratory frame. Scalar Stokes
\(S_0=\mathrm{tr}(\mathbf{J}_\chi)\) and circular content
\(S_3=\mathrm{tr}(\mathbf{J}_\chi\boldsymbol{\sigma}_3)\) are invariant under either
channel when the underlying \(\E\)-field state is unchanged, but component reports
\(S_1,S_2\) mix unless the active-passive pairing of
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.active-passive-duality} is respected. Residual
\(\Delta_{\mathbf{J}}=\|\mathbf{J}_\chi'-R_z(\alpha)\mathbf{J}_\chi R_z(-\alpha)\|_F\)
quantifies frame-mismatch in polarimetry pipelines; \(\Delta_{\mathbf{J}}=0\) certifies
consistent rotation bookkeeping before angular-flux indices from
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} are applied to the same dataset
\cite{stratton1941}.

For a rank-two object tied to radiation direction, let \(\mathbf{D}=\hat{\mathbf{k}}\hat{\mathbf{p}}^\top\)
with \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}\parallel\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) and \(\mathbf{p}\parallel\hat{\mathbf{x}}\)
for a short dipole aligned with \(\hat{\mathbf{x}}\). Active rotation
\(R_y(\pi/2)\) maps \(\mathbf{p}\mapsto p_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) and
\(\hat{\mathbf{k}}\mapsto\hat{\mathbf{k}}\) in the far zone, producing
\(\mathbf{D}'=\hat{\mathbf{k}}(\hat{\mathbf{k}}'\times\mathbf{p}')^\top/\|\cdot\|\)
after renormalization of the transverse projector. Passive rotation by the same
\(R_y(\pi/2)\) leaves the physical radiation pattern fixed but expresses
\(\mathbf{D}\) in rotated components with \(\mathbf{D}'=R_y(\pi/2)\mathbf{D}R_y(-\pi/2)\).
Power through \(S_r\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-power-invariance} matches in
both reports because \(\mathrm{tr}(\mathbf{D}\mathbf{D}^\dagger)\) is rotation-invariant
even though individual entries of \(\mathbf{D}\) are not. The exercise confirms that
objectivity checks must target invariants and covariant outputs, not raw matrix
elements \cite{stratton1941}.

Finite rotations on \(\mathrm{SO}(3)\) are often parameterized by Euler angles
\(R=R_z(\alpha)R_y(\beta)R_z(\gamma)\), with known singularities at
\(\beta=0,\pi\). For numerical rotation chains, quaternion representation
\(q=(\cos\varphi/2,\hat{\mathbf{u}}\sin\varphi/2)\) avoids gimbal lock and
implements composition as quaternion product, matching group law
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-group-action}. Mismatch index
\(\kappa_{\mathrm{rot}}\) from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.0.2} becomes nonzero when
active rotation is applied to \(\E\) but boundary data or source currents are left
untransformed; the repair is to co-rotate the full admissible data tuple
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.data-specification-tuple}, not the field alone
\cite{stratton1941}.

Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} imposes transport qualification through
\(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}\); this subsection preserves that constraint. In
practice, active/passive equivalence is used for physical interpretation only when
the compared states satisfy the same admissibility and regime criteria.

\begin{proposition}[Observable equivalence under active-passive duality]
\label{prop:ch1.active-passive-observable-equivalence}
For rigid \(R\in\mathrm{SO}(3)\), active and passive descriptions linked by
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.active-passive-duality} yield identical values for
frame-invariant scalar observables built from admissible transport integrals.
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
