Duality maps field descriptions by inverse-group pairing. Invariant scalar
observables use invariant contractions and measure-preserving integration on rotated
manifolds. Therefore values are unchanged under representation channel choice.

Rigid-body rotation laws require Euclidean geometry,
orthonormal frames, and constitutive response for which transport observables
remain finite under the closure of Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}. Non-rigid
deformations, manifold curvature requiring connection terms, and orientation-
dependent constitutive operators require extended covariance machinery.


\subsection{Transformation of vectors, tensors, and dyads}
\label{sec:ch01.3.2}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

\emph{Vectors} carry one index and rotate by \(R\); \emph{tensors} carry multiple
indices and transform by congruence laws; \emph{dyads} are rank-two objects whose
contractions with vectors must remain objective when frames rotate
\cite{stratton1941}. Maxwell stress is the canonical dyad in angular transport:
mis-stating its rotation law produces fictitious torque even when \(\E\) and \(\H\)
are correct.

Rigid rotations act on vectors, tensors, and dyads through distinct but linked
transformation laws. Covariance failures at any one layer propagate into false
conclusions about stress transfer, torque prediction, and modal coupling. The
objective is operational: assign each object its rotation law, then verify that
physically meaningful contractions remain objective under rigid frame action
\cite{stratton1941}.

For proper rotation \(R\in\mathrm{SO}(3)\), with \(R^\top R=I\), \(\det R=1\), let
\(\mathbf{v}=v_j\hat{\mathbf{e}}_j\) be vector in orthonormal basis
\(\hat{\mathbf{e}}_j\). Component transformation is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.vector-rotation-components}
v_i' = R_{ij}v_j.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.vector-rotation-components} is the component-level
realization of active/passive vector rotation from Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.3.1}.

For second-rank tensor \(\mathbf{T}\) with components \(T_{pq}\),
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.tensor-rotation-rule}
\mathbf{T}' = R\,\mathbf{T}\,R^\top,
\qquad
T'_{ij}=R_{ip}R_{jq}T_{pq}.
\end{equation}
The two factors \(R_{ip}\), \(R_{jq}\) transform each tensor slot. This is the
objectivity law ensuring that stress tensor
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} rotates geometrically rather than numerically.

Tensor invariants satisfy
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.tensor-invariant-trace}
\operatorname{tr}(\mathbf{T}')=\operatorname{tr}(\mathbf{T}),\qquad
\|\mathbf{T}'\|_F=\|\mathbf{T}\|_F,
\end{equation}
because \(\operatorname{tr}(R\mathbf{T}R^\top)=\operatorname{tr}(\mathbf{T})\) and
orthogonality of \(R\) preserves Frobenius inner product.

If \(\mathbf{T}\mathbf{q}_k=\lambda_k\mathbf{q}_k\), then
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.stress-eigen-covariance}
\mathbf{T}'(R\mathbf{q}_k)=\lambda_k(R\mathbf{q}_k),
\end{equation}
so eigenvalues are objective and principal directions rotate covariantly.

For dyadic map \(\mathbf{D}\) defined by \(\mathbf{w}=\mathbf{D}\cdot\mathbf{u}\):
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.dyadic-covariance}
\mathbf{D}' = R\,\mathbf{D}\,R^\top,
\qquad
\mathbf{w}'=\mathbf{D}'\cdot\mathbf{u}'=R\mathbf{w},
\end{equation}
with \(\mathbf{u}'=R\mathbf{u}\). This equation establishes slotwise equivariance:
rotating input and operator yields rotated output.

Rank-one dyad \(\mathbf{D}=\mathbf{a}\mathbf{b}^\top\) transforms as
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.rank1-dyad-rotation}
\mathbf{D}'=R\mathbf{a}\,(R\mathbf{b})^\top.
\end{equation}
Both generating directions rotate, preserving dyadic rank and directional coupling
interpretation used in mode-projection constructions.

Composition compatibility follows:
\[
\mathbf{D}'' = R_2(R_1\mathbf{D}R_1^\top)R_2^\top
=(R_2R_1)\mathbf{D}(R_2R_1)^\top.
\]
Hence staged rotations and single composite rotation are algebraically identical.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=9mm and 11mm]
  \node[ch1box] (v) {$\mathbf{v}\mapsto R\mathbf{v}$};
  \node[ch1box, below=11mm of v] (t) {$\mathbf{T}\mapsto R\mathbf{T}R^\top$};
  \node[ch1box, below=11mm of t] (d) {$\mathbf{D}\mapsto R\mathbf{D}R^\top$};
  \node[ch1box, below=11mm of d] (tr) {$\mathbf{t}=\mathbf{T}\hat{\mathbf{n}}$};
  \draw[ch1flow] (v) -- (t) -- (d) -- (tr);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Covariance ladder linking Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.vector-rotation-components}, \eqref{eq:ch1.tensor-rotation-rule}, and \eqref{eq:ch1.dyadic-covariance}; invariant and traction branches identify objective versus reoriented quantities.}
\label{fig:ch1.tensor-dyadic-covariance}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.tensor-dyadic-covariance} organizes transformation rules into
one operational flow from algebraic object to measurable traction output.

With \(\mathbf{t}=\mathbf{T}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\) and
\(\hat{\mathbf{n}}'=R\hat{\mathbf{n}}\):
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.traction-rotation-covariance}
\mathbf{t}'=\mathbf{T}'\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}' = R\mathbf{t}.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.traction-rotation-covariance} is the direct bridge from
covariance algebra to measurable mechanical response.

The Maxwell stress tensor of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} is symmetric in
vacuum regions, \(\mathbf{T}=\mathbf{T}^\top\), and Maxwell \cite{maxwell1873}
introduced the associated mechanical force on conductors precisely through traction
\(\mathbf{t}=\mathbf{T}\hat{\mathbf{n}}\). Under \(R\in\mathrm{SO}(3)\), symmetry is
preserved because
\(\mathbf{T}'=(R\mathbf{T}R^\top)^\top=R\mathbf{T}^\top R^\top=\mathbf{T}'\).
The eigenvalue problem therefore commutes with rotation:
if \(\mathbf{T}\mathbf{q}_k=\lambda_k\mathbf{q}_k\), then
\(\mathbf{T}'(R\mathbf{q}_k)=\lambda_k(R\mathbf{q}_k)\), extending
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.stress-eigen-covariance} to the full spectral decomposition.
Principal stresses \(\lambda_k\) are scalars reportable without frame tag; principal
directions \(R\mathbf{q}_k\) rotate covariantly and must be compared only after
declaring active or passive convention from Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.3.1}.

Stratton \cite{stratton1941} emphasized that dyadic Green functions
\(\boldsymbol{\mathcal{G}}(\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}')\) for the vector Helmholtz
operator transform as
\(\boldsymbol{\mathcal{G}}'(\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}')=
R\,\boldsymbol{\mathcal{G}}(R^{-1}\mathbf{r},R^{-1}\mathbf{r}')\,R^\top\)
under simultaneous rotation of observation and source coordinates. Slotwise
equivariance in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.dyadic-covariance} is the local differential
realization of this global operator law. For a rank-one modal coupling
\(\mathbf{D}_{mn}=\mathbf{f}_m\mathbf{g}_n^\top\) between modes \(m\) and \(n\),
rotation re-expresses both factor vectors, but the scalar coupling strength
\(\mathrm{tr}(\mathbf{D}_{mn}^\top\mathbf{D}_{mn})\) remains invariant when
\(\mathbf{f}_m,\mathbf{g}_n\) are co-rotated.

\paragraph{Worked problem: Maxwell stress under \(R_x(\pi/2)\) for a dipole field.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,0) -- (1.5,0);
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,0) -- (0,1.5);
  \fill (0.9,0) circle (1.5pt);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above] at (0.9,0.05) {$\theta=\pi/2$};
  \draw[->, thick, gray] (0.9,0) -- (1.25,0) node[above, ch1lblout] {$\hat{\mathbf{r}}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0.45,-0.2) {$\mathbf{T}'=R_x(\pi/2)\mathbf{T}R_x^\top$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: Maxwell stress on an equatorial observation point and its transformation under \(R_x(\pi/2)\).}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-stress-rx-dipole}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-stress-rx-dipole} validates Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.traction-rotation-covariance} on the dipole equator: normal pressure \(t_r\) is scalar-invariant while tangential traction rotates with the field triad.
In the far zone of \(\mathbf{p}=p_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}\), take
\(\E=E_\theta\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}\), \(\H=H_\phi\hat{\boldsymbol{\phi}}\) with
\(|E_\theta|=\eta_0|H_\phi|\). Construct \(\mathbf{T}\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} at fixed \(\mathbf{r}\) on the equatorial plane
\(\theta=\pi/2\). Under \(R=R_x(\pi/2)\), the field pair transforms as
\(\E'=R\E\), \(\H'=R\H\), and direct substitution yields
\(\mathbf{T}'=R\mathbf{T}R^\top\). Traction on outward normal
\(\hat{\mathbf{n}}=\hat{\mathbf{r}}\) satisfies \(\mathbf{t}'=R\mathbf{t}\) per
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.traction-rotation-covariance}. The normal pressure component
\(t_r=\hat{\mathbf{n}}\cdot\mathbf{t}\) is invariant because
\(\hat{\mathbf{n}}'\cdot\mathbf{t}'=\hat{\mathbf{n}}\cdot\mathbf{t}\), whereas
tangential components rotate. A numerical implementation that forms \(\mathbf{T}'\) by
rotating Cartesian components of \(\E,\H\) without applying
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.tensor-rotation-rule} produces
\(\mathcal{R}_{\mathbf{T}}>0\) in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.covariance-residual} even for
analytic fields---a frequent source of false torque discrepancies in post-processed
near-field scans \cite{maxwell1873,stratton1941}.

For operator \(\mathcal{F}\) mapping admissible sources to fields, define covariance residual
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.covariance-residual}
\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{F}}(R;X)=
\left\|\mathcal{F}(R\cdot X)-R\cdot\mathcal{F}(X)\right\|,
\end{equation}
with admissible input \(X\). Exact covariance corresponds to
\(\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{F}}=0\); finite values diagnose discretization-induced frame
artifacts.

Transport and regime indices fixed in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} constrain
covariance comparisons: frame maps are physically meaningful only when compared
states satisfy common transport closure and outward-power support.

\begin{proposition}[Covariant map criterion]
\label{prop:ch1.covariant-map-criterion}
If a tensor/dyadic map obeys \(X\mapsto RXR^\top\) and acts linearly on vector
slots transformed by \(v\mapsto Rv\), then derived vector outputs satisfy
\(y\mapsto Ry\), and scalar contractions built from invariant inner products are
frame-independent.
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
Linearity plus conjugation provides slotwise equivariance by substitution.
Orthogonality of \(R\) cancels paired \(R,R^\top\) in scalar contractions, leaving
objective values.

\paragraph{Worked problem: rank-one dyad under \(R_z(\pi/2)\).}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,0) -- (1.6,0) node[below] {$x$};
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,0) -- (0,1.6) node[left] {$y$};
  \draw[->, thick, red!70!black] (0.2,0) -- (0.2,0.9) node[right, ch1lblout] {$\mathbf{a}$};
  \draw[->, thick, blue!70!black] (0,0.2) -- (0.9,0.2) node[above, ch1lblout] {$\mathbf{b}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas] at (0.75,0.55) {$\mathbf{D}=\mathbf{a}\mathbf{b}^\top$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: rank-one modal coupling dyad and its equivariance under a quarter-turn about \(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\).}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-rank-one-dyad}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-rank-one-dyad} confirms Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.dyadic-covariance} on a single off-diagonal entry: co-rotated factor vectors reproduce the same mapped output in the rotated frame.
Let \(\mathbf{a}=a_0\hat{\mathbf{x}}\), \(\mathbf{b}=b_0\hat{\mathbf{y}}\), so
\(\mathbf{D}=\mathbf{a}\mathbf{b}^\top\) has only \(D_{xy}=a_0b_0\). Under
\(R=R_z(\pi/2)\), \(\mathbf{a}'=a_0\hat{\mathbf{y}}\) and
\(\mathbf{b}'=-b_0\hat{\mathbf{x}}\), giving \(D'_{yx}=-a_0b_0\) in the rotated frame.
For input \(\mathbf{u}=\hat{\mathbf{x}}\), \(\mathbf{w}=\mathbf{D}\mathbf{u}=a_0b_0\hat{\mathbf{y}}\)
and \(\mathbf{w}'=\mathbf{D}'\mathbf{u}'=a_0b_0\hat{\mathbf{y}}'\), confirming
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.dyadic-covariance}. Traction on normal \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}=\hat{\mathbf{z}}\)
uses \(\mathbf{T}\) rather than \(\mathbf{D}\), but the same slotwise rule applies when
stress dyads are decomposed into rank-one modal couplings in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.5.3}
\cite{stratton1941}.

Scope is restricted to proper rigid Euclidean rotations and orthonormal frames.
Improper transformations, curved manifolds with connection terms, and constitutive
operators tied to anisotropic geometric structure require extended machinery.


\subsection{Origin dependence and translational covariance}
\label{sec:ch01.3.3}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Origin dependence enters angular reporting through the moment arm, not through a
change of physical field state. If the same state is reported from two translated
origins, the reported angular quantity changes by an affine correction term. The
central objective is therefore to separate translation-induced bookkeeping from
transport-bearing content \cite{stratton1941}.

Let \(O\) and \(O'=O+\mathbf{a}\) be two origins, with
\(\mathbf{a}\in\mathbb{R}^3\). Position vectors are related by
\(\mathbf{r}'=\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{a}\). Using linear-momentum density
\(\mathbf{p}_{\mathrm{em}}\) (in this chapter,
\(\mathbf{p}_{\mathrm{em}}=\mathbf{P}/c^2\)), define
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.origin-angular-definition}
\mathbf{J}_O=\int_V \mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{p}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV,\qquad
\mathbf{J}_{O'}=\int_V \mathbf{r}'\times\mathbf{p}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV.
\end{equation}
Replacing \(\mathbf{r}'\) gives
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.origin-shift-law}
\mathbf{J}_{O'}=\mathbf{J}_O-\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}},\qquad
\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}=\int_V \mathbf{p}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-law} is the fundamental affine shift rule:
translation contributes an additive extrinsic term controlled by total linear
momentum.

An equivalent local statement appears for angular-flux tensor:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.origin-shift-flux-law}
\mathbf{M}_{O'}=\mathbf{M}_{O}-\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{T},
\end{equation}
with \(\mathbf{T}\) Maxwell stress tensor from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress}. Hence origin dependence is present at both
integral and local-flux levels.

The shift law Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-law} follows from elementary
kinematics once linear-momentum density is accepted as the mechanical conjugate of
translational motion. With \(\mathbf{r}'=\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{a}\),
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.origin-shift-derivation}
\mathbf{J}_{O'}
=
\int_V (\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{a})\times\mathbf{p}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV
=
\mathbf{J}_O-\mathbf{a}\times\int_V\mathbf{p}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV,
\end{equation}
because \(\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{p}_{\mathrm{em}}\) integrates to
\(\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}\) when \(\mathbf{a}\) is constant on
\(V\). Maxwell \cite{maxwell1873} treated the same lever-arm structure for
mechanical couples on rigid conductors: translating the reference point for torque
report adds \(-\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{F}_{\mathrm{tot}}\) when
\(\mathbf{F}_{\mathrm{tot}}\) is total force. Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-law}
is the field-theoretic analogue with \(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}\) replacing
\(\mathbf{F}_{\mathrm{tot}}\) in the harmonic momentum picture of
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1.5}.

The angular-flux tensor shift Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-flux-law} is not
independent of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-law}. Using
\(\mathbf{M}=\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{T}\) in the standard Maxwell stress
construction \cite{maxwell1873,stratton1941}, replacement \(\mathbf{r}\mapsto\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{a}\)
gives \(\mathbf{M}_{O'}=\mathbf{M}_O-\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{T}\) because
\(\mathbf{T}\) is independent of origin choice in a homogeneous coordinate translation.
Thus torque density reports and angular-flux reports shift coherently: a dataset that
satisfies Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-law} but violates
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-flux-law} signals inconsistent stress reconstruction
rather than new angular physics.

\paragraph{Worked problem: dual-origin report on a two-dipole array.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[->, thick, red!70!black] (-0.6,0) -- (-0.6,0.7);
  \draw[->, thick, red!70!black] (0.6,0) -- (0.6,-0.7);
  \fill (0,0) circle (1.5pt) node[below, ch1lblout] {$O$};
  \fill (0,0.5) circle (1.5pt) node[right, ch1lblout] {$O'$};
  \draw[dashed, gray] (0,0) -- (0,0.5);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-0.35) {$\mathbf{p}_\pm=\pm p_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: common affine correction for dual-origin angular-momentum reports on a symmetric two-dipole array.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-two-dipole-dual-origin}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-two-dipole-dual-origin} shows that the origin shift in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-law} depends only on \(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}\), not on inter-dipole phase, before intrinsic comparison via Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.intrinsic-angular-observable}.
Consider two equal short dipoles \(\mathbf{p}_\pm=\pm p_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) at
\(\mathbf{r}_\pm=\pm d\hat{\mathbf{x}}\). Total linear momentum flux through
\(S_r\) in the radiative zone is \(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}=P_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}\)
from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1.5} superposition. About origin \(O\) at the array
center, \(\mathbf{J}_O\) receives contributions from both arms. About
\(O'=O+\mathbf{a}\) with \(\mathbf{a}=a\hat{\mathbf{y}}\),
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-law} gives
\(\mathbf{J}_{O'}=\mathbf{J}_O-aP_0\hat{\mathbf{x}}\). The shift is entirely
determined by \(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}\) and does not depend on inter-dipole
phase; two arrays with identical exported power but different near-field circulation
can share the same affine correction. Removing the shift via
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.intrinsic-angular-observable} with \(\mathbf{r}_c=\mathbf{0}\)
yields a common intrinsic report before comparing radiation patterns. Residual
\(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{tr}}\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.translation-consistency-residual}
exceeds tolerance only if \(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}\) was estimated from a
truncated aperture omitting reactive shell contributions, illustrating why transport
closure from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} precedes origin comparison
\cite{stratton1941}.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=9mm and 11mm]
  \node[ch1box] (a) {Report A: $(\mathbf{J}_O,\mathbf{M}_O)$};
  \node[ch1box, below=10mm of a] (b) {Report B: $(\mathbf{J}_{O'},\mathbf{M}_{O'})$};
  \node[ch1box, right=22mm of a] (aff) {$-\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}$};
  \draw[ch1flow] (a) -- (aff);
  \draw[ch1flow] (b) -- (aff);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Affine translation structure for angular-momentum reports: translation modifies lever-arm representation while preserving underlying field transport dynamics.}
\label{fig:ch1.origin-shift-map}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.origin-shift-map} emphasizes a practical rule: reported angular
differences are physically meaningful only after removing origin-shift contributions.

A translation-covariant intrinsic observable is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.intrinsic-angular-observable}
\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{int}}
=
\mathbf{J}_O-\mathbf{r}_c\times\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}},
\end{equation}
with declared reference point \(\mathbf{r}_c\). If \((O,\mathbf{r}_c)\) is
translated jointly, \(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{int}}\) is unchanged.

For finite-resolution data, translation residual is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.translation-consistency-residual}
\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{tr}}=
\left\|
\mathbf{J}_{O'}-\mathbf{J}_{O}+\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}
\right\|.
\end{equation}
Ideal affine closure gives \(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{tr}}=0\); nonzero values diagnose
truncation, sampling mismatch, or incomplete momentum closure.

Origin comparison applies only when both reports use the same transport closure
from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}; otherwise bookkeeping shifts are indistinguishable
from genuine transport differences. Comparisons are accepted only when those
closure conditions remain valid and affine residual is controlled:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.origin-qualified-decision}
\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{org}}=1
\iff
\Big(
\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=1,\
\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{tr}}\le R_{\mathrm{tr,max}}
\Big),
\end{equation}
where \(R_{\mathrm{tr,max}}\ge0\) is declared reporting tolerance.

\begin{proposition}[Origin-comparable angular reporting criterion]
\label{prop:ch1.origin-comparable-criterion}
Two datasets referenced to origins \(O_1,O_2\) are directly comparable in angular
transport if and only if either a common origin protocol is used, or both are
mapped to a common intrinsic form \(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{int}}\), and
\(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{org}}=1\).
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-law} contributes additive
\(\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}\). Without common origin or intrinsic
conversion, this additive term contaminates physical differences. Residual control
in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-qualified-decision} enforces reliable affine closure.

Origin-shift bookkeeping requires finite momentum integrals,
regular control boundaries, and rigid Euclidean translations. Open systems with
unresolved far-field flux, strongly nonlocal constitutive response, or actively
time-varying boundary injection require extended closure operators before
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-law}--\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-qualified-decision}
can be used without qualification.

\paragraph{Worked problem: origin shift on a finite dipole.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[->, thick, red!70!black] (0,0) -- (0,0.95) node[left, ch1lblout] {$\mathbf{p}\parallel\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \fill (0,0) circle (1.5pt) node[below left, ch1lblout] {$O$};
  \fill (0.55,0) circle (1.5pt) node[below, ch1lblout] {$O'$};
  \draw[dashed, gray, ->] (0,0) -- (0.55,0) node[midway, below, ch1lblout] {$\mathbf{a}$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: affine origin shift on angular-momentum reports for a finite dipole with fixed exported linear momentum.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-finite-dipole-origin}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-finite-dipole-origin} isolates the lever-arm term in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-law}: excess residual beyond \(R_{\mathrm{tr,max}}\) signals aperture truncation or inconsistent \(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}\) estimation, not new intrinsic angular structure.
Let a short dipole \(\mathbf{p}=p_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) produce total linear momentum
\(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}=P_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) in the harmonic radiative zone.
Report angular momentum about origin \(O\) at the dipole center and about
\(O'=O+\mathbf{a}\) with \(\mathbf{a}=a\hat{\mathbf{x}}\). Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-shift-law}
gives \(\mathbf{J}_{O'}=\mathbf{J}_O-\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}
=\mathbf{J}_O-aP_0\hat{\mathbf{y}}\). The shift term is pure bookkeeping: it
contains no new field physics, only lever-arm change. Removing it via
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.intrinsic-angular-observable} with \(\mathbf{r}_c=\mathbf{0}\)
restores a common intrinsic report before comparing two sources. If measured
\(\|\mathbf{J}_{O'}-\mathbf{J}_O+\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}\|\)
exceeds tolerance \(R_{\mathrm{tr,max}}\) in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.origin-qualified-decision}, the discrepancy is attributed to
finite aperture truncation or inconsistent \(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{tot}}\) estimation,
not to intrinsic angular structure \cite{stratton1941}.


\subsection{Invariants and frame-independent observables}
\label{sec:ch01.3.4}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

\emph{Invariants} are scalars unchanged by allowed frame actions; \emph{frame-independent
observables} are vector or tensor reports that transform with the geometry rather than
as coordinate accidents \cite{stratton1941}. A laboratory rotation of the apparatus
must not alter exported power or net angular-flux magnitude; only directional
\emph{components} of vector reports may change when the reference axes rotate.

Field diagnostics qualify as observables only when their numerical report survives
allowed frame transformations with the correct geometric law. This subsection
constructs a complete observable filter at three levels: scalar invariance, vector
covariance, and tensor-invariant reduction. The resulting filter is then coupled to
the directional transport quantities of Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}.

Let \(\mathcal{F}\) be the electromagnetic state and \(\mathcal{A}_R\mathcal{F}\)
its rotated image for \(R\in\mathrm{SO}(3)\). A scalar functional
\(\mathcal{I}[\mathcal{F}]\in\mathbb{R}\) is frame-invariant when
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.scalar-observable-invariance}
\mathcal{I}[\mathcal{A}_R\mathcal{F}] = \mathcal{I}[\mathcal{F}]
\quad \forall R\in\mathrm{SO}(3).
\end{equation}
A vector observable \(\mathbf{Q}[\mathcal{F}]\in\mathbb{R}^3\) is frame-objective
when
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.vector-observable-covariance}
\mathbf{Q}[\mathcal{A}_R\mathcal{F}] = R\,\mathbf{Q}[\mathcal{F}].
\end{equation}
These two relations separate true observables from coordinate artifacts: violation
of both indicates representation dependence.

For second-rank tensors transformed by \( \mathbf{T}'=R\mathbf{T}R^\top \), define
principal invariants
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.tensor-principal-invariants}
I_1(\mathbf{T})=\mathrm{tr}(\mathbf{T}),\qquad
I_2(\mathbf{T})=\frac{1}{2}\!\left[(\mathrm{tr}\,\mathbf{T})^2-\mathrm{tr}(\mathbf{T}^2)\right].
\end{equation}
These are invariant because trace is unchanged under similarity action.
The third invariant
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.tensor-third-invariant}
I_3(\mathbf{T})=\det(\mathbf{T})
\end{equation}
is also frame-invariant for proper rotations. Hence
\((I_1,I_2,I_3)\) provides a coordinate-independent scalar signature of tensorial
content in three-dimensional Euclidean space.

For symmetric \(\mathbf{T}\), the characteristic polynomial
\(\det(\mathbf{T}-\lambda I)=0\) expands to
\(-\lambda^3+I_1\lambda^2-I_2\lambda+I_3=0\), so the invariant triplet
\((I_1,I_2,I_3)\) from
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.tensor-principal-invariants}--\eqref{eq:ch1.tensor-third-invariant}
uniquely determines eigenvalues in \(\mathbb{R}^3\) even though eigenvectors rotate
under \(R\). Maxwell \cite{maxwell1873} exploited trace combinations of stress
components when reducing force resultants on closed surfaces; the modern form is
exactly \(I_1=\mathrm{tr}(\mathbf{T})\), the isotropic pressure signature of the
stress state. For Maxwell stress in vacuum, \(I_1\) equals twice the local electric
energy density minus twice the magnetic energy density in the time-harmonic
real-quadratic map, a scalar that can be probed without resolving principal axes.

The Poynting vector magnitude \(|\mathbf{S}|\) with
\(\mathbf{S}=\tfrac12\E\times\H^*\) is rotation-invariant when \(\E,\H\) are
co-rotated, because \(\|\Re\{\E\times\H^*\}\|\) is preserved under
\(\E\mapsto R\E\), \(\H\mapsto R\H\). Combined with total power
\(P=\int_{S_r}\Re\{\mathbf{S}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\}\,dS\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.rotation-power-invariance}, scalar observables built from
\(|\mathbf{S}|\) and \(P\) survive rigid-frame changes and serve as minimal
calibration checks before angular-flux functionals are evaluated.

\paragraph{Worked problem: invariants for the uniform plane-wave sector.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,0) -- (0,1.8) node[ch1oncanvas, above] {$\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \draw[->, thick, red!70!black] (-0.3,0.8) -- (0.3,0.8);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (0.35,0.8) {$\mathbf{T}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-0.15) {$I_1,I_2,I_3$ invariant};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: rotation-invariant stress scalars for the uniform plane-wave sector used as calibration before angular-flux functionals.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-planewave-invariants}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-planewave-invariants} certifies Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.objective-sector-index} on the plane-wave baseline: individual stress components mix under \(R_y(\pi/4)\) while \(I_1,I_2,I_3\) and co-rotated \(\Gamma_J\) remain fixed.
Using the field template of Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1} with propagation along
\(\hat{\mathbf{k}}=\hat{\mathbf{z}}\), take \(\E,\H\) transverse to \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}\)
without re-deriving the Maxwell solution. The Maxwell stress tensor at fixed phase
has \(I_1=0\) in source-free vacuum because electric and magnetic quadratic terms
cancel in \(\mathrm{tr}(\mathbf{T})\) for equiamplitude transverse waves. Under
\(R=R_y(\pi/4)\), direct computation confirms \(\mathbf{T}'=R\mathbf{T}R^\top\) and
\(I_1,I_2,I_3\) unchanged. Residuals
\(\Delta_{\mathbf{T}}\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.tensor-objectivity-residual} and
\(\Delta_{\mathcal{I}}\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.observable-frame-residual} vanish
to machine precision, while individual stress components mix. Sector index
\(\Gamma_J\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.objective-sector-index} evaluated on a
hemispherical window co-rotated with the field remains invariant, certifying that
angular-flux reporting can proceed once scalar stress invariants and power qualification
\(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=1\) are confirmed \cite{maxwell1873,stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: coherency-matrix invariants under frame rotation.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[thick] (0,0) circle (0.75);
  \node[ch1oncanvas] at (0,0) {$S_0$};
  \draw[->, thick, blue!70!black] (0,0) -- (0.55,0.35);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right] at (0.6,0.35) {$(S_1,S_2,S_3)$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0,-0.95) {$\mathrm{tr}\boldsymbol{\Gamma}$, $\det\boldsymbol{\Gamma}$ fixed};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: rotation-covariant Stokes components versus rotation-invariant coherency scalars.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-coherency-invariants}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-coherency-invariants} applies Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.scalar-observable-invariance} to polarization reporting: only scalar invariants survive passive axis rotation without being mistaken for physical polarization change.
For partially polarized light on a transverse plane, let
\(\boldsymbol{\Gamma}=\langle\mathbf{e}\mathbf{e}^\dagger\rangle\) be the
\(2\times2\) coherency matrix in laboratory axes. Rotation of the measurement frame
by \(R_z(\alpha)\) yields \(\boldsymbol{\Gamma}'=R_z(\alpha)\boldsymbol{\Gamma}R_z(-\alpha)\).
Invariants \(\mathrm{tr}(\boldsymbol{\Gamma})\), \(\det(\boldsymbol{\Gamma})\), and
\(\mathrm{tr}(\boldsymbol{\Gamma}^2)\) are unchanged, whereas Stokes parameters
\(S_1,S_2\) mix. Observable filter
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.scalar-observable-invariance} therefore accepts \(S_0\) and
\(\sqrt{S_1^2+S_2^2+S_3^2}\) as scalars but treats \((S_1,S_2,S_3)\) as a
covariant vector under passive rotations. Mapping this structure to directional
diagnostics of Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} prevents false claims of polarization
rotation when only the reference axes were rotated \cite{stratton1941}.

Observable checks in computation or measurement require quantitative residuals:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.observable-frame-residual}
\Delta_{\mathcal{I}}(R;\mathcal{F})=
\left|\mathcal{I}[\mathcal{A}_R\mathcal{F}]-\mathcal{I}[\mathcal{F}]\right|,
\qquad
\Delta_{\mathbf{Q}}(R;\mathcal{F})=
\left\|\mathbf{Q}[\mathcal{A}_R\mathcal{F}]-R\mathbf{Q}[\mathcal{F}]\right\|.
\end{equation}
Exact objectivity implies zero residual; nonzero residual quantifies numerical,
experimental, or modeling non-objectivity.

Tensor-level residual can be introduced analogously:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.tensor-objectivity-residual}
\Delta_{\mathbf{T}}(R;\mathcal{F})=
\left\|\mathbf{T}[\mathcal{A}_R\mathcal{F}]-R\,\mathbf{T}[\mathcal{F}]\,R^\top\right\|_F,
\end{equation}
with \(\|\cdot\|_F\) Frobenius norm. This residual is useful when stress or
coherency tensors are reconstructed from discretized fields.

Using directional angular-flux \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) and active power density
\(\Pi\) from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}, their objectivity-coupled sector index is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.objective-sector-index}
\Gamma_J(\Delta\Omega;r)=
\frac{\left\|\int_{\Delta\Omega}\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega\right\|}
{\int_{\Delta\Omega}\Pi(\theta,\phi;r)\,d\Omega+\epsilon_\Pi},
\end{equation}
where \(\Delta\Omega\subseteq4\pi\) is an interaction sector and
\(\epsilon_\Pi>0\) regularizes weak-power windows. Numerator captures net rotational
export over the sector; denominator captures energetic support. The ratio remains
scalar-objective when \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) and \(\Pi\) satisfy the covariance
and invariance conditions above.

For inter-frame comparability across multiple sectors, define
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.multisector-objective-vector}
\boldsymbol{\Gamma}(r)=
\big[\Gamma_J(\Delta\Omega_1;r),\ldots,\Gamma_J(\Delta\Omega_N;r)\big]^\top,
\end{equation}
with partition \(\{\Delta\Omega_n\}_{n=1}^N\) of \(4\pi\). This vector is not a
spatial vector; it is a sector-index report. Its objectivity requires fixed
physical sector definition under frame rotation and consistent pullback of sector
boundaries.

Define observability admissibility set
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.observability-admissibility-map}
\mathcal{O}_{\mathrm{adm}}[\mathcal{F}]
=
\Big\{\mathcal{X}:\Delta_{\mathcal{X}}(R;\mathcal{F})\le\tau_{\mathcal{X}},\
\forall R\in\mathrm{SO}(3)\Big\},
\end{equation}
where \(\tau_{\mathcal{X}}\) is declared tolerance for report class
\(\mathcal{X}\). This definition incorporates exact theory (\(\tau_{\mathcal{X}}=0\))
and practical finite-resolution settings (\(\tau_{\mathcal{X}}>0\)).

\begin{proposition}[Frame-objective transport index criterion]
\label{prop:ch1.frame-objective-index}
If \(\Pi\) is scalar-invariant and \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) is vector-covariant
under \(R\in\mathrm{SO}(3)\), then
\(\Gamma_J(\Delta\Omega;r)\) in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.objective-sector-index} is
frame-objective for fixed physical sector definition.
\end{proposition}

Proof sketch: sector-integrated \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\) transforms covariantly, so
its norm is invariant; sector-integrated \(\Pi\) is invariant by scalar property.
Their ratio is therefore invariant.

\begin{proposition}[Invariant-tensor observable equivalence]
\label{prop:ch1.tensor-invariant-equivalence}
For two tensor reports \(\mathbf{T}_a,\mathbf{T}_b\) related by proper rotation
\(\mathbf{T}_b=R\mathbf{T}_aR^\top\), the invariant triplets are identical:
\((I_1,I_2,I_3)_a=(I_1,I_2,I_3)_b\). Conversely, if two symmetric tensors share the
same triplet and have non-degenerate eigenspaces, they are related by orthogonal
similarity.
\end{proposition}

The first statement is direct from invariance properties. The converse uses
spectral decomposition: shared characteristic polynomial implies shared eigenvalue
multiset; non-degenerate eigenspaces allow one orthogonal basis to rotate into the
other.

\paragraph{Worked problem: stress-tensor objectivity for a short dipole.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,0) -- (1.4,0);
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,0) -- (0,1.4);
  \fill (1.0,0.35) circle (1.5pt);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, above right] at (1.0,0.35) {far zone};
  \draw[->, thick, gray] (0,0) arc (0:90:0.55) node[midway, right, ch1lblout] {$R_y(\pi/2)$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, below] at (0.5,-0.15) {$\Delta_{\mathbf{T}}=0$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}

\caption{Worked-problem schematic: passive rotation of a dipole far-zone stress tensor and invariant principal traces.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-stress-objectivity-dipole}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-stress-objectivity-dipole} verifies Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.tensor-objectivity-residual} on an analytic dipole field: nonzero \(\Delta_{\mathbf{T}}\) signals implementation error, not physical non-objectivity.
In the far zone of an electric dipole \(\mathbf{p}=p_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}\), take
\(\E=E_\theta\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}\) and \(\H=H_\phi\hat{\boldsymbol{\phi}}\)
with amplitudes scaling as \(1/r\). Construct \(\mathbf{T}\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress} and rotate the field pair by
\(R=R_y(\pi/2)\). Direct evaluation confirms \(\mathbf{T}'=R\mathbf{T}R^\top\) with
residual \(\Delta_{\mathbf{T}}(R;\mathcal{F})=0\) in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.tensor-objectivity-residual}. Scalar invariants
\(I_1,I_2,I_3\) from
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.tensor-principal-invariants}--\eqref{eq:ch1.tensor-third-invariant}
match before and after rotation. Traction on \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}=\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}\)
transforms as \(\mathbf{t}'=R\mathbf{t}\), consistent with
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.traction-rotation-covariance}. A code that reports
\(\Delta_{\mathbf{T}}>10^{-12}\) for this analytic case signals passive/active
rotation mix-up or incorrect spherical-component ordering rather than physical
non-objectivity \cite{maxwell1873}.

Objectivity tests presuppose proper rotations in Euclidean
space, gauge-robust field constructions, and sufficient regularity for the
integrals and residual norms used above. Non-Euclidean manifolds, improper
transformations, and gauge-dependent local decompositions require modified
objectivity operators handled in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.4}.

\paragraph{Section 1.3 closure and forward link.}
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.3} fixes active/passive rotation duality, tensor and dyadic
covariance, origin translation, and frame-invariant observables. Quantities carried
forward must survive rigid-frame action and declared origin protocol before entering
transport interpretation. Section~\ref{sec:ch01.4} treats gauge freedom within the
same screening discipline.


\section{Gauge Structure and Physical Observables}
\label{sec:ch01.4}

\emph{Gauge structure} names the equivalence class of potentials that produce the same
\((\E,\H)\); \emph{physical observables} are the gauge-invariant and frame-consistent
quantities that survive that equivalence and connect to measurement
\cite{stratton1941,belinfante1940}. The laboratory risk is familiar: local
angular-momentum or helicity densities can look substantial in one gauge yet change
under a legal potential redefinition while exported flux integrals remain fixed.
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.4} separates bookkeeping from physics before Chapter~3 introduces
richer modal decompositions that could otherwise be mistaken for new invariants.

Section~\ref{sec:ch01.4} develops gauge structure as an equivalence-class framework
for electromagnetic representation: potentials are introduced as computational
coordinates on field space, gauge transformations as symmetry operations within that
representation class, and helicity-linked constructions as meaningful only after
explicit observability filtering. The section objective is therefore twofold: first,
to show which potential-level decompositions are gauge-dependent bookkeeping; second,
to isolate the combinations that remain invariant and can be connected to physical
transport channels established earlier. Every statement in this section is evaluated
against that invariant-content requirement before it is used in subsequent chapters.


\subsection{Potentials and gauge transformations}
\label{sec:ch01.4.1}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

\emph{Potentials} \((\mathbf{A},\phi)\) are a convenient representation of the same
\((\E,\H)\); \emph{gauge transformations} are the legal reparameterizations that leave
measurable fields unchanged while moving potential-dependent local formulas
\cite{stratton1941,belinfante1940}. Angular discussions that begin from \(\mathbf{A}\)
therefore inherit gauge orbit motion before any transport claim is made.

Potential theory in Maxwell electrodynamics is a representation framework, not an
extra layer of physics. Observable fields are invariant under a continuous family
of potential reparameterizations, so potential-level statements become meaningful
only after explicit gauge-invariance checks
\cite{stratton1941}.

Gauge freedom is not a Maxwellian afterthought: it is the statement that infinitely
many potential pairs generate the same physically measurable \((\E,\H)\). Classical
monographs from Stratton \cite{stratton1941} through Jackson
\cite{maxwell1873,stratton1941} treat Lorenz and Coulomb gauges as complementary tools---the
first for radiation problems with retarded potentials, the second for static and
quasi-static limits. Angular-momentum discussions that build local densities from
\(\mathbf{A}\) and \(\phi\) therefore inherit gauge orbit motion by construction;
Subsections~\ref{sec:ch01.4.2} and~\ref{sec:ch01.4.5} quantify when such maps may
still be reported and when they must remain interpretive \cite{belinfante1940}.

Let \(\mathbf{A}(\mathbf{r})\) be vector potential, \(\phi(\mathbf{r})\) scalar
potential, \(\E(\mathbf{r})\) electric phasor, and \(\mathbf{B}(\mathbf{r})\)
magnetic flux density, with \(\omega>0\) and time factor \(\exp(-\jj\omega t)\).
Potential-to-field map is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.potential-field-relations}
\mathbf{B}=\nabla\times\mathbf{A},\qquad
\E=-\nabla\phi+\jj\omega\mathbf{A}.
\end{equation}
The first identity enforces magnetic solenoidality in representation form; the
second decomposes electric field into irrotational and inductive channels.

Gauge freedom is generated by smooth scalar function \(\chi(\mathbf{r})\):
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.gauge-transform}
\mathbf{A}'=\mathbf{A}+\nabla\chi,\qquad
\phi'=\phi-\jj\omega\chi.
\end{equation}
Substituting Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-transform} into
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.potential-field-relations} gives explicit invariance:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.gauge-field-invariance-check}
\nabla\times\mathbf{A}'=\nabla\times\mathbf{A},\qquad
-\nabla\phi'+\jj\omega\mathbf{A}'=-\nabla\phi+\jj\omega\mathbf{A}.
\end{equation}
Thus gauge transformation moves representation coordinates on one physical state
rather than generating new measurable fields.

Maxwell \cite{maxwell1873} introduced scalar and vector potentials as auxiliary
functions whose curls and gradients reconstruct \(\mathbf{B}\) and \(\E\); the
modern phasor map Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.potential-field-relations} is the
frequency-domain specialization of that construction. Stratton \cite{stratton1941}
systematized gauge fixing for radiation problems by showing that the Lorenz condition
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.lorenz-gauge-phasor} decouples \(\mathbf{A}\) and \(\phi\) into
retarded Helmholtz equations with shared wavenumber \(k\), whereas Coulomb gauge
\(\nabla\cdot\mathbf{A}=0\) enforces transverse \(\mathbf{A}\) at the expense of
instantaneous \(\phi\). Neither gauge is privileged at the level of
\((\E,\H)\); both are coordinates on the same physical orbit.

The gauge-orbit structure can be written as an equivalence relation:
\((\mathbf{A},\phi)\sim(\mathbf{A}',\phi')\) iff
\(\mathbf{A}'=\mathbf{A}+\nabla\chi\) and \(\phi'=\phi-\jj\omega\chi\) for some
\(\chi\). Harmonic time dependence makes the scalar update proportional to
\(-\jj\omega\chi\) rather than \(-\partial_t\chi\), so static gauge shifts
\(\chi=\) constant leave \(\phi\) unchanged but add irrotational content to
\(\mathbf{A}\). Purely solenoidal gauge updates with \(\nabla^2\chi=0\) alter
\(\mathbf{A}\) without changing its curl, illustrating that gauge freedom is
orthogonal to the physical content carried by \(\nabla\times\mathbf{A}\).

In homogeneous regions, the Lorenz-gauge potentials obey
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.potential-wave-equations}. The retarded solution for impressed
current \(\Jimp\) at angular frequency \(\omega\) is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.retarded-potential-harmonic}
\mathbf{A}(\mathbf{r})
=
\frac{\mu}{4\pi}\int
\frac{\Jimp(\mathbf{r}')\,e^{\jj k|\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{r}'|}}
{|\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{r}'|}\,dV',
\end{equation}
with \(k=\omega\sqrt{\mu\varepsilon}\). Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.retarded-potential-harmonic}
is Stratton's \cite{stratton1941} standard radiation kernel; gauge transformations
add gradient terms that integrate to boundary contributions on finite domains without
altering \(\nabla\times\mathbf{A}\) in the interior.

\paragraph{Worked problem: Coulomb versus Lorenz gauge for a static charge pair.}
For time-independent fields with \(\omega\to 0\) replaced by electrostatic limit,
Coulomb gauge gives \(\phi_C=\sum_i q_i/(4\pi\varepsilon_0|\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{r}_i|)\)
and \(\mathbf{A}_C=\mathbf{0}\). Lorenz gauge with the same \(\E=-\nabla\phi\) admits
\(\mathbf{A}_L=\nabla\psi\) for harmonic \(\psi\) satisfying
\(\nabla^2\psi=0\) on source-free regions. Both reproduce identical \(\E\), yet
\(\mathbf{A}_C=\mathbf{0}\) while \(\mathbf{A}_L\neq\mathbf{0}\). A candidate local
density linear in \(\mathbf{A}\) therefore differs between gauges even though
\(\E\) is fixed. Gauge residual
\(\Delta_{\mathrm{g}}\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-observable-residual} is nonzero
for such densities, while \(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{L}}=0\) for the Lorenz pair and
divergence residual \(\|\nabla\cdot\mathbf{A}_C\|=0\) for Coulomb. The example
shows that gauge-qualified reporting must name the gauge orbit before interpreting
potential-weighted maps \cite{maxwell1873,stratton1941}.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=7mm]
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=52mm] (L1) {Layer 1:\\$(\mathbf{A},\phi)\!\to\!(\mathbf{A}',\phi')\!\to\!\cdots$ via $\chi$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=52mm, below=of L1] (L2) {Layer 2:\\invariant $(\E,\mathbf{B})$ from };
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=52mm, below=of L2] (L3) {Layer 3:\\accepted report iff orbit-invariant};
  \draw[ch1flow] (L1) -- (L2);
  \draw[ch1flow] (L2) -- (L3);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, left=16mm of L1, align=center] {gauge\\orbit};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right=16mm of L2, align=center] {physical\\state};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Gauge-orbit structure: potential-level multiplicity maps to a single physical field state; observables are admitted only after orbit invariance checks.}
\label{fig:ch1.gauge-orbit}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.gauge-orbit} mirrors the frame-objectivity logic of
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.3}: representation freedom is admitted, but reporting freedom
is constrained by invariance criteria.

Gauge-admissible scalar reporting functional \(\mathcal{I}\) must satisfy
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.gauge-observable-invariance}
\mathcal{I}[\mathbf{A}',\phi']=\mathcal{I}[\mathbf{A},\phi]
\quad\text{for all }\chi\text{ in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-transform}}.
\end{equation}
Quantitative residual version is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.gauge-observable-residual}
\Delta_{\mathrm{g}}(\chi;\mathcal{I})=
\left|\mathcal{I}[\mathbf{A}+\nabla\chi,\phi-\jj\omega\chi]-\mathcal{I}[\mathbf{A},\phi]\right|.
\end{equation}
Exact gauge objectivity requires \(\Delta_{\mathrm{g}}=0\); finite values quantify
discretization or modeling contamination of gauge invariance.

Potential equations close after gauge condition choice. In Lorenz gauge:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.lorenz-gauge-phasor}
\nabla\cdot\mathbf{A}-\jj\omega\mu\varepsilon\,\phi=0,
\end{equation}
with \(\mu\) permeability and \(\varepsilon\) permittivity. In homogeneous regions
this yields Helmholtz-type equations
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.potential-wave-equations}
\nabla^2\mathbf{A}+k^2\mathbf{A}=-\mu\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{imp}},\qquad
\nabla^2\phi+k^2\phi=-\frac{\rho}{\varepsilon},
\end{equation}
where \(k^2=\omega^2\mu\varepsilon\). This form is computationally convenient and
keeps gauge-fixed propagation and source coupling explicit.

\paragraph{Worked problem: Lorenz-gauge potential for a point dipole.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,-0.15) -- (0,3.1) node[ch1oncanvas, above] {$\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \fill[ch1src] (0,0.75) circle (1.5pt);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, left=11mm] at (0,0.75) {$\mathbf{p}=p_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \draw[thick, blue!60!black] (0,0.45) -- (0,1.05);
  \draw[ch1shell] (0,0.75) circle (1.75);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, right=1mm] at (1.75,0.75) {$kr\!\gg\!1$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, align=left, anchor=west] at (2.15,2.35)
    {$\mathbf{A}\!\propto\!e^{\jj k_0 r}/r\,\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, align=left, anchor=west] at (2.15,1.65)
    {$\phi\!\propto\!\cos\theta/r^2$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, align=center] at (0,-0.7)
    { $\to\ (\E,\mathbf{B})$;\quad
     $\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{L}}$ from };
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Worked-problem schematic: Lorenz-gauge retarded potentials for a point dipole and consistency check on the Lorenz condition.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-lorenz-dipole-potential}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-lorenz-dipole-potential} ties potential components to radiation fields through Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.potential-field-relations}: a solver with \(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{L}}>0\) is internally inconsistent even if \(\E,\mathbf{B}\) appear correct.
For \(\mathbf{p}=p_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) in vacuum, the Lorenz-gauge potential in the
far zone is \(\mathbf{A}(\mathbf{r})\approx -\jj\omega\mu_0 p_0 e^{\jj k_0 r}/(4\pi r)\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}\)
and \(\phi(\mathbf{r})\approx p_0\cos\theta/(4\pi\varepsilon_0 r^2)\) to leading
order in \(kr\gg 1\). Substituting into Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.potential-field-relations}
recovers \(\E,\mathbf{B}\) consistent with the dipole radiation fields. A gauge update
\(\chi=\alpha r\) shifts \(\mathbf{A}\) and \(\phi\) but leaves \(\E,\mathbf{B}\)
unchanged by Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-field-invariance-check}. Residual
\(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{L}}\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-condition-residual} should
vanish for the Lorenz-gauge solution; a numerical solver that reports
\(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{L}}>0\) is using an inconsistent potential pair even if
\(\E,\mathbf{B}\) appear correct \cite{stratton1941}.

Gauge-choice compatibility can be monitored by
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.gauge-condition-residual}
\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{L}}=
\left\|\nabla\cdot\mathbf{A}-\jj\omega\mu\varepsilon\,\phi\right\|_{L^2(\Omega)}.
\end{equation}
\(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{L}}=0\) indicates exact Lorenz-gauge compliance; nonzero
values identify closure drift in numerical or measured reconstructions.

Transport and regime closure from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} constrain
gauge-level reports: potential statements are accepted only when transport closure
and gauge invariance hold jointly:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.gauge-qualified-decision}
\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{g}}=1
\iff
\Big(
\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=1,\
\Delta_{\mathrm{g}}\le \delta_{\mathrm{g,max}},\
\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{L}}\le R_{\mathrm{L,max}}
\Big),
\end{equation}
with declared tolerances \(\delta_{\mathrm{g,max}},R_{\mathrm{L,max}}\ge0\).

\begin{proposition}[Gauge-orbit observable criterion]
\label{prop:ch1.gauge-orbit-observable-criterion}
A potential-derived report is physically admissible in this chapter only if it is
invariant on the full gauge orbit and satisfies \(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{g}}=1\).
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
Gauge transforms leave \((\E,\mathbf{B})\) invariant by
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.potential-field-relations}--\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-transform}
\cite{maxwell1873,belinfante1940}. Reports must satisfy
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-observable-invariance} and the residual bounds in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-qualified-decision}, with transport closure inherited from
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}.
\end{proof}

Gauge-orbit analysis presumes smooth fields on simply connected regions,
linear constitutive closure, and single-frequency excitation.
Topological gauge patching, strongly nonlocal kernels, and nonlinear conversion
require generalized gauge bundles and nonlinear observability filters beyond
Chapter~\ref{ch:01}.

With Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-transform}--\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-qualified-decision}
fixed, the next subsection can classify which local densities are genuinely
observable and which remain gauge-dependent bookkeeping.


\subsection{Gauge dependence of local densities}
\label{sec:ch01.4.2}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

\emph{Gauge dependence} means a local density written with potentials can change under
legal gauge shifts even when integrated totals and measurable fields remain fixed
\cite{belinfante1940,stratton1941}. The physics question is therefore not whether a
formula exists, but whether its gauge variation cancels on the measurement domain.

Gauge freedom preserves measurable fields but can alter local densities written
explicitly in terms of potentials. Belinfante \cite{belinfante1940} established
that symmetric energy-momentum tensors built from potentials need not be unique
even when integrated totals are conserved---the central caution this subsection
formalizes. The technical objective is to separate three classes: strictly
gauge-invariant local reports, gauge-variant decompositions with boundary-canceling
integrals, and gauge-contaminated reports that cannot be promoted to observables
\cite{stratton1941}.

Belinfante \cite{belinfante1940} addressed a sharper problem than gauge
bookkeeping alone: the canonical energy-momentum tensor
\(\mathbf{T}^{\mathrm{can}}\) built from potentials is generally asymmetric and
yields a canonical angular-momentum density
\(\mathbf{j}^{\mathrm{can}}=\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{g}^{\mathrm{can}}+\mathbf{s}^{\mathrm{can}}\)
whose divergence fails to match the Lorentz torque law unless a Belinfante
correction is added. Define the spin-flux density (third-rank tensor contracted to
vector form) through
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.belinfante-spin-flux}
S_{ij k}
=
\frac{1}{2}\Big(
F_{i\ell}\,F_{k j}^{\;\;\ell}
-
F_{k\ell}\,F_{ij}^{\;\;\ell}
\Big),
\end{equation}
where \(F_{\mu\nu}\) is the field-strength tensor in SI phasor notation and index
contractions follow Stratton's \cite{stratton1941} Cartesian Maxwell notation.
The Belinfante symmetrized tensor is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.belinfante-symmetric-tensor}
\mathbf{T}^{\mathrm{sym}}
=
\mathbf{T}^{\mathrm{can}}
+
\frac{1}{2}\,\divg\mathbf{S},
\end{equation}
with \(\divg\mathbf{S}\) the divergence of the spin-flux tensor on its first slot.
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.belinfante-symmetric-tensor} adds a total divergence to
\(\mathbf{T}^{\mathrm{can}}\) without changing integrated four-momentum, but
redistributes local momentum density so that
\(\mathbf{T}^{\mathrm{sym}}=\mathbf{T}^{\mathrm{sym}\,\top}\) and the associated
angular-momentum flux satisfies the macroscopic balance expected from Maxwell
stress \cite{belinfante1940,maxwell1873}.

In three-vector Maxwell language on vacuum phasors, a representative symmetric
stress is Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress}, while a canonical potential-based
stress differs by terms proportional to \(\E(\nabla\cdot\E)\) and
\(\H(\nabla\cdot\H)\) that vanish in source-free regions but generate gauge
sensitivity near impressed sources. Belinfante's correction is precisely the
divergence term that removes this asymmetry when sources are treated through
\(F_{\mu\nu}\) rather than through symmetric \(\E,\H\) alone. The local-density
gauge shift Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-density-gauge-shift} therefore parallels
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.belinfante-symmetric-tensor}: \(\mathbf{\Xi}_\chi\) plays the
role of a gauge-generated Belinfante-like redistribution, and \(\mathbf{\Psi}_\chi\)
signals non-removable gauge contamination in the interior.

Let \(\mathbf{A}\) and \(\phi\) denote a potential pair and
\((\mathbf{A}',\phi')\) the transformed pair from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-transform}. Let \(\mathbf{E}\) and \(\mathbf{B}\) be the
physical fields reconstructed through Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.potential-field-relations},
and let \(\mathbf{d}_{\mathrm{loc}}(\mathbf{r})\) be a candidate local density
vector at position \(\mathbf{r}\). The dependence structure can be written as
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.local-density-functional}
\mathbf{d}_{\mathrm{loc}}(\mathbf{r})
=
\mathcal{D}\!\left[\mathbf{A},\phi,\mathbf{E},\mathbf{B}\right](\mathbf{r}),
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal{D}\) is a density-construction functional. If
\(\mathcal{D}\) uses potentials explicitly, then the gauge update in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-transform} generally induces
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.local-density-gauge-shift}
\delta_\chi \mathbf{d}_{\mathrm{loc}}
\equiv
\mathbf{d}_{\mathrm{loc}}' - \mathbf{d}_{\mathrm{loc}}
=
\nabla\cdot\mathbf{\Xi}_\chi + \mathbf{\Psi}_\chi,
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{\Xi}_\chi\) is a gauge-generated flux tensor and
\(\mathbf{\Psi}_\chi\) is a residual gauge source term. Equation
\eqref{eq:ch1.local-density-gauge-shift} is the core diagnostic of this
subsection: local density objectivity fails whenever either term is nonzero.

The decomposition has direct physical meaning. The divergence branch can contribute
only through boundary flux after volume integration; the residual branch modifies
interior report values even with closed boundaries. Thus the second term is the
stronger indicator of non-observability.

The roles of the two terms are distinct. The divergence contribution
\(\nabla\cdot\mathbf{\Xi}_\chi\) can integrate to boundary transport only; the
residual term \(\mathbf{\Psi}_\chi\) changes local value even in closed domains.
Thus, a density may be locally gauge-dependent but still yield gauge-robust
integrated totals under admissible boundary conditions. Let \(V\) be a finite
volume with boundary \(\partial V\) and outward unit normal
\(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\). Then
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.integrated-density-gauge-test}
\int_V \delta_\chi \mathbf{d}_{\mathrm{loc}}\,dV
=
\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{\Xi}_\chi\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
\;+\;
\int_V \mathbf{\Psi}_\chi\,dV.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.integrated-density-gauge-test} follows by divergence
theorem and separates boundary leakage from volumetric gauge contamination.
Mathematically, the left side is gauge variation of integrated density; physically,
it tests whether a reported quantity is robust or representation-contaminated.

To connect with the gauge criterion \(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{g}}\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-qualified-decision}, define normalized local-gauge index
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.local-gauge-index}
\mathcal{G}_{\mathrm{loc}}=
\frac{\left\|\int_V \delta_\chi\mathbf{d}_{\mathrm{loc}}\,dV\right\|}
{\int_V\|\mathbf{d}_{\mathrm{loc}}\|\,dV+\epsilon_d},
\qquad \epsilon_d>0.
\end{equation}
Small \(\mathcal{G}_{\mathrm{loc}}\) indicates gauge-stable integrated reporting for
the selected volume protocol.

The operational criterion that aligns this subsection with
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-observable-invariance} is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.local-observable-admissibility}
\delta_\chi \mathbf{d}_{\mathrm{loc}}=\mathbf{0}
\quad\text{or}\quad
\int_V \delta_\chi \mathbf{d}_{\mathrm{loc}}\,dV=\mathbf{0}
\ \text{under stated boundary conditions}.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-observable-admissibility} does not force every
useful local decomposition to be gauge-invariant pointwise; it states the exact
conditions under which such decompositions may still be interpreted without
violating observable objectivity \cite{stratton1941}.

Operational acceptance in this chapter is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.local-density-qualified-decision}
\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{loc}}=1
\iff
\Big(
\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{g}}=1,\
\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=1,\
\mathcal{G}_{\mathrm{loc}}\le G_{\mathrm{loc,max}},\
\text{Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-observable-admissibility} holds}
\Big),
\end{equation}
where \(G_{\mathrm{loc,max}}\ge 0\) is declared tolerance. This criterion prevents
gauge-unstable local densities from entering transport interpretation.

\begin{proposition}[Local-density observability criterion]
\label{prop:ch1.local-density-observable-criterion}
A local density report derived from potentials is admissible as an observable in
this chapter only if \(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{loc}}=1\).
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-density-gauge-shift} gives the full gauge variation.
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-observable-admissibility} enforces local or integrated
invariance. Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-density-qualified-decision} adds the gauge
and transport/regime criteria, ensuring that accepted reports are both representation-
robust and physically qualified.

Within Chapter~\ref{ch:01}, this result is used as a filter on local
spin-orbit-style density narratives: forms that fail
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-observable-admissibility} remain representation tools,
whereas forms that pass can be linked to measurable channels from
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.5}. This distinction prevents gauge artefacts from being
misreported as physical transport structure \cite{stratton1941}.

The derivation assumes smooth fields, linear media, and boundary regularity
sufficient for divergence theorem use. Singular gauge choices, topological branch
cuts, and nonlocal constitutive operators require generalized distributional
treatment outside the present subsection.

\paragraph{Worked problem: Coulomb versus Lorenz gauge on a local density.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=24mm] (C) {Coulomb\\$(\mathbf{A}_C,\phi_C)$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=24mm, right=of C] (L) {Lorenz\\$(\mathbf{A}_L,\phi_L)$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=30mm, below=16mm of $(C)!0.5!(L)$] (EB)
    {same $(\E,\mathbf{B})$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=34mm, below=of EB] (jloc)
    {$\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{loc}}=\mathbf{r}\!\times\!(\varepsilon_0\E+\mathbf{A}\!\times\!\mathbf{B}/\mu_0)$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=34mm, below=of jloc] (psi)
    {$\mathbf{A}'=\mathbf{A}+\nabla\chi$:\ $\int_{V_g}\mathbf{\Psi}_\chi\,dV\neq 0$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=34mm, below=of psi, fill=green!8] (Jint)
    {invariant $\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{int}}=\int_{V_g}\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV$};
  \draw[ch1flow] (C) -- (EB);
  \draw[ch1flow] (L) -- (EB);
  \draw[ch1flow] (EB) -- (jloc);
  \draw[ch1flow] (jloc) -- (psi);
  \draw[ch1flow] (psi) -- (Jint);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Worked-problem schematic: identical physical fields with gauge-dependent local density maps and gauge-invariant integrated reports.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-coulomb-lorenz-local}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-coulomb-lorenz-local} illustrates Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-observable-admissibility}: boundary cancellation of \(\mathbf{\Xi}_\chi\) does not rescue a local map when the volume residual \(\int_{V_g}\mathbf{\Psi}_\chi\,dV\) remains finite.
For \(\mathbf{A}=\mathbf{A}_C\), \(\phi=\phi_C\) in Coulomb gauge
(\(\divg\mathbf{A}_C=0\)) and \((\mathbf{A}_L,\phi_L)\) in Lorenz gauge, both
reproduce the same \(\E,\mathbf{B}\). A candidate local density
\(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{loc}}=\mathbf{r}\times(\varepsilon_0\E+\mathbf{A}\times\mathbf{B}/\mu_0)\)
generally satisfies Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-density-gauge-shift} with
\(\delta_\chi\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{loc}}\neq\mathbf{0}\) under
\(\mathbf{A}'=\mathbf{A}+\nabla\chi\). Integrating over volume \(V_g\) and applying
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.integrated-density-gauge-test}, the boundary term
\(\int_{\partial V_g}\mathbf{\Xi}_\chi\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS\) may cancel while
the residual \(\int_{V_g}\mathbf{\Psi}_\chi\,dV\) remains finite, so
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-observable-admissibility} fails. By contrast, total
\(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{int}}=\int_{V_g}\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV\) built from
gauge-invariant \(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\) of Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1.5} survives
the gauge update. The lesson for measurement design is direct: report surface or
volume integrals of invariant densities; treat potential-weighted local maps as
diagnostics unless Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-density-qualified-decision} is verified
\cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: Belinfante redistribution on a dipole near-field shell.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,-0.55) -- (0,3.35) node[ch1oncanvas, above] {$\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \fill[ch1src] (0,0.75) circle (1.5pt);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, left=12mm] at (0,0.75) {$\mathbf{p}$};
  \draw[thick, blue!60!black] (0,0.48) -- (0,1.02);
  \draw[ch1shell] (0,0.75) circle (1.75);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, red!70!black, right=14mm] at (1.75,0.75) {$S_a$};
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=red!10, red!70!black, align=center, minimum width=28mm]
    at (4.25,0.75) {Shell radius:\\$r=a$};
  \draw[->, thick, teal!70!black] (-1.5,0.75) arc (180:125:1.5);
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=teal!12, teal!70!black, align=center, minimum width=36mm]
    at (-4.30,1.55) {Canonical density:\\$\mathbf{d}^{\mathrm{can}}=\mathbf{r}\!\times\!(\varepsilon_0\E)$};
  \draw[->, thick, orange!85!black] (1.5,0.75) arc (0:55:1.5);
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=orange!12, orange!85!black, align=center, minimum width=36mm]
    at (4.30,1.55) {Stress flux:\\$\mathbf{r}\!\times\!(\mathbf{T}\!\cdot\!\hat{\mathbf{r}})$};
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=gray!8, align=center, minimum width=48mm] at (0,-2.85) {equal shell integrals;\ local difference $\divg\mathbf{U}_B$ (Belinfante spin-flux)};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Worked-problem schematic: matching total angular momentum through \(S_a\) while local canonical and stress reports differ by Belinfante redistribution.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-belinfante-shell}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-belinfante-shell} confirms equal shell integrals when \(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=1\) yet order-unity local gauge index for potential-weighted maps---stress integration remains the gauge-invariant reporting route.
On spherical shell \(S_a\) of radius \(a\) surrounding a short electric dipole
\(\mathbf{p}=p_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}\), compare canonical density
\(\mathbf{d}^{\mathrm{can}}=\mathbf{r}\times(\varepsilon_0\E)\) with symmetric
stress-derived flux \(\mathbf{r}\times(\mathbf{T}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{r}})\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress}. Both integrate to the same total angular momentum
through \(S_a\) when \(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=1\), but their local values on
\(S_a\) differ by a term \(\divg\mathbf{U}_B\) identifiable with Belinfante spin-flux
divergence in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.belinfante-spin-flux}. Under gauge update
\(\mathbf{A}'=\mathbf{A}+\nabla\chi\), \(\mathbf{d}^{\mathrm{can}}\) shifts by
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-density-gauge-shift} with generally nonzero
\(\mathbf{\Psi}_\chi\), whereas the stress flux report shifts only through boundary
terms because \(\mathbf{T}\) is gauge-invariant in vacuum. Index
\(\mathcal{G}_{\mathrm{loc}}\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-gauge-index} is small
for stress-integrated totals but order unity for potential-weighted local maps,
confirming Belinfante's \cite{belinfante1940} central lesson: symmetrization and
gauge screening must precede local interpretive claims \cite{belinfante1940}.

Having separated gauge-dependent local densities from admissible observable
content, the next subsection formalizes gauge-invariant observables alongside
helicity and dual-symmetry structure.


\subsection{Gauge-invariant observables, helicity, and dual symmetry}
\label{sec:ch01.4.3}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

\emph{Gauge-invariant observables} survive potential relabeling; \emph{helicity}
names the signed overlap between electric and magnetic rotational content in the
radiative regime; \emph{dual symmetry} links electric and magnetic field patterns
under the duality rotation that preserves free-space Maxwell structure
\cite{stratton1941,belinfante1940}.

The previous subsection showed that potential-dependent local densities can fail
observable status unless gauge variation is explicitly closed. The present
development advances from that screening step to a constructive objective: identify
field reports that remain invariant under gauge relabeling, remain objective under
rigid-frame transformations, and admit a transport law linked to helicity and dual
symmetry \cite{stratton1941}.

Let the phasor field state be \(\mathcal{F}=(\E,\H)\) in a linear isotropic region
with scalar permittivity \(\varepsilon\), scalar permeability \(\mu\), angular
frequency \(\omega\), and intrinsic impedance
\(\eta=\sqrt{\mu/\varepsilon}\). Let \((\mathbf{A},\phi)\) denote a potential pair
that reproduces \(\E\) and \(\mathbf{B}=\mu\H\) through
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.potential-field-relations}. A scalar report \(\mathcal{O}_s\),
vector report \(\mathbf{O}_v\), and pseudoscalar report \(\mathcal{O}_p\) are
accepted as gauge-invariant observables only if all three components of the
admissibility specification are satisfied:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.observable-admissibility-triad}
\mathcal{O}_\alpha[\mathbf{A}',\phi']=\mathcal{O}_\alpha[\mathbf{A},\phi],\qquad
\mathcal{O}_\alpha[\mathcal{A}_R\mathcal{F}]=\mathcal{R}_\alpha(R)\,\mathcal{O}_\alpha[\mathcal{F}],
\qquad
\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{loc}}=1,
\end{equation}
where \(\alpha\in\{s,v,p\}\), \((\mathbf{A}',\phi')\) is generated by
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-transform}, \(\mathcal{A}_R\) is active spatial rotation
from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.active-rotation-action}, and \(\mathcal{R}_\alpha(R)\) is
representation map: identity for scalars, \(R\) for vectors, and parity-aware sign
rule for pseudoscalars. The final clause imports the local-density criterion of
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-density-qualified-decision}; therefore a report can be
algebraically elegant yet rejected if local gauge contamination survives.

A gauge-invariant observable here means more than formal cancellation in a symbolic
expression: the reported quantity must be insensitive to potential-gauge choice and
also carry a consistent rigid-frame transformation law, so that two observers using
rotated coordinates assign equivalent physical content to the same field
configuration
\cite{stratton1941}. This dual requirement removes ambiguity that appears
when gauge robustness is checked without covariance, or covariance is checked
without gauge screening.

Within the admissible class, helicity is introduced as a field-intrinsic
pseudoscalar density, following the handedness measure standard in classical
field theory \cite{stratton1941}:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.helicity-density}
h(\mathbf{r})=
\frac{1}{2\omega}\Im\!\left\{
\varepsilon\,\E^*\cdot(\curl\E)
\;+\;
\mu\,\H^*\cdot(\curl\H)
\right\}.
\end{equation}
Here \((\cdot)^*\) denotes complex conjugation, \(\Im\{\cdot\}\) extracts imaginary
part, and \(\curl=\nabla\times\). The electric contribution
\(\varepsilon\,\E^*\cdot(\curl\E)\) measures local rotational phase-structure of
\(\E\), while the magnetic contribution
\(\mu\,\H^*\cdot(\curl\H)\) measures the corresponding structure in \(\H\). Division
by \(2\omega\) places helicity on a frequency-normalized scale so that comparisons
between configurations at fixed \(\omega\) are not dominated by trivial frequency
rescaling.

Gauge robustness follows directly because \(h\) is built from \(\E\), \(\H\), and
their curls, each invariant under potential gauge relabeling for fixed physical
fields. To verify this at the operator level, note that under
\(\mathbf{A}'=\mathbf{A}+\nabla\chi\), \(\E'=\E\) because
\(-\nabla\phi'+\jj\omega\mathbf{A}'=
-\nabla\phi+\jj\omega\mathbf{A}+\nabla(\jj\omega\chi-\jj\omega\chi)\).
Similarly \(\curl\E\) is unchanged because \(\curl\nabla\chi=\mathbf{0}\). The
magnetic sector follows from \(\H=\mathbf{B}/\mu\) and \(\mathbf{B}=\curl\mathbf{A}\).
Stratton \cite{stratton1941} listed \(\E\), \(\H\), \(\mathbf{B}\), and quadratic
energy expressions among the primary field observables precisely because they factor
through gauge-invariant curls.

The dual rotation Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.duality-rotation} is not a gauge symmetry: it
mixes \(\E\) and \(\H\) while leaving the source-free Maxwell system invariant in
isotropic media. Infinitesimal dual variation \(\xi=\delta\xi\) gives
\(\delta\E=\delta\xi\,\eta\H\) and \(\delta\H=-\delta\xi\,\E/\eta\) to first order.
Helicity density responds as a pseudoscalar should: for circular polarization states
specified through Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1}, \(h\) changes sign under
\(\xi=\pi/2\) because electric and magnetic roles interchange with a handedness
flip. Dual defect index \(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{dual}}\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.duality-defect-index} quantifies failure of this symmetry when
\(\varepsilon,\mu\) vary spatially or when conduction loss breaks phase coherence
between \(\E\) and \(\H\).

Integrated helicity over volume \(V\) connects to flux through
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.helicity-balance}. In harmonic steady state,
\(\partial_t h=0\) and source-free regions give
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.helicity-flux-integral}
\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{f}_h\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
=
\int_V q_h\,dV,
\end{equation}
so net helicity export is controlled by material conversion \(q_h\). For vacuum
\(V\) enclosing a radiative source, Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.helicity-flux-integral} plays
the same screening role as Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}: only flux
through \(\partial V\) is externally reportable.

The local gauge-variation identity is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.helicity-density-wave-reduction}
\delta_\chi h
\equiv
h[\mathbf{A}',\phi']-h[\mathbf{A},\phi]
=
0.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.helicity-density-wave-reduction} is the local statement that
helicity density is gauge-objective when evaluated from physical field data.

Noether-type continuity structure associated with dual rotations gives the local
helicity-balance law
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.helicity-balance}
\frac{\partial h}{\partial t}
+\divg\mathbf{f}_h
=
-q_h,
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{f}_h\) is helicity flux density and \(q_h\) is material helicity
conversion rate. In source-free homogeneous domains \(q_h=0\), so helicity is
transported but not created or annihilated locally
\cite{stratton1941}. Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.helicity-balance} is the
helicity counterpart of the momentum and angular-momentum conservation forms
developed earlier in Sections~\ref{sec:ch01.1}--\ref{sec:ch01.2}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: right-hand versus left-hand circular polarization.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.88]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,-0.15) -- (0,2.8) node[ch1oncanvas, above] {$+\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \draw[->, thick, blue!60!black] (-0.5,0.3) -- (0.5,0.3);
  \draw[->, thick, blue!60!black] (-0.5,1.3) -- (0.5,1.3);
  \draw[->, thick, blue!60!black] (-0.5,2.3) -- (0.5,2.3);
  \draw[->, thick, red!70!black] (0.55,0.3) arc (0:270:0.22);
  \draw[->, thick, red!70!black] (0.55,1.3) arc (0:-270:0.22);
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=blue!12, blue!70!black, align=center, minimum width=38mm]
    at (-4.30,1.3) {RHCP block:\\$\E\!\propto\!(\hat{\mathbf{x}}+\jj\hat{\mathbf{y}})$\\helicity $h>0$};
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=blue!12, blue!70!black, align=center, minimum width=38mm]
    at (4.30,1.3) {LHCP block:\\$\E\!\propto\!(\hat{\mathbf{x}}-\jj\hat{\mathbf{y}})$\\helicity $h<0$};
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=gray!8, align=center, minimum width=44mm] at (0,-2.75)
    {Gauge note:\\$\mathbf{A}'=\mathbf{A}+\nabla\chi$ leaves $h$ unchanged};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Worked-problem schematic: signed helicity density for opposite circular polarizations in a propagating plane wave.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-rhcp-lhcp}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-rhcp-lhcp} separates helicity sign from azimuthal phase index \(m\): Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.helicity-density} tracks polarization handedness, not orbital winding alone.
Fields are taken from the uniform plane-wave template of
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1} without re-solving Maxwell's equations: for
\(\E=E_0(\hat{\mathbf{x}}\pm\jj\hat{\mathbf{y}})/\sqrt{2}\) and matching
\(\H=\pm(E_0/\eta_0)(\hat{\mathbf{y}}\mp\jj\hat{\mathbf{x}})/\sqrt{2}\) in a
\(+\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) plane wave, Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.helicity-density} gives
\(h=\pm |E_0|^2/(2\omega\eta_0)\): opposite signs for opposite helicities.
Under gauge transform \(\mathbf{A}'=\mathbf{A}+\nabla\chi\), both \(\E\) and \(\H\)
are unchanged, so \(h\) is unchanged as required by
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.helicity-density-wave-reduction}. Helicity therefore tracks
signed polarization handedness, not azimuthal phase index \(m\); a field with
\(m\neq 0\) but linear polarization can have \(h=0\) while still exhibiting strong
phase winding in \(\arg E_t\) \cite{stratton1941}.

For phasor diagnostics used throughout Chapter~\ref{ch:01}, define the time-average
helicity divergence residual
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.helicity-closure-residual}
\mathcal{R}_h
=
\frac{\left\|\divg\!\left\langle \mathbf{f}_h\right\rangle
+\left\langle q_h\right\rangle\right\|}
{\left\|\left\langle \mathbf{f}_h\right\rangle\right\|/\ell_h+\epsilon_{rh}},
\end{equation}
where \(\langle\cdot\rangle\) is time average, \(\ell_h>0\) a characteristic length,
and \(\epsilon_{rh}>0\) a regularization constant. Small \(\mathcal{R}_h\) certifies
that computed helicity channels satisfy the local conservation closure demanded by
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.helicity-balance}.

Dual symmetry is the continuous rotation symmetry in the
\((\E,\eta\H)\)-plane:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.duality-rotation}
\begin{bmatrix}
\E_\xi\\[1mm]
\eta\H_\xi
\end{bmatrix}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
\cos\xi & \sin\xi\\
-\sin\xi & \cos\xi
\end{bmatrix}
\begin{bmatrix}
\E\\[1mm]
\eta\H
\end{bmatrix},
\qquad \xi\in\mathbb{R}.
\end{equation}
The angle \(\xi\) parameterizes continuous redistribution between electric and
magnetic components while preserving source-free Maxwell form in isotropic media.
This action is not a gauge transform; it is a physical symmetry of the field
equations under constitutive conditions that keep electric and magnetic sectors on
equal structural footing \cite{stratton1941}.

Invariance tests under Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.duality-rotation} can be written in
closed form. Local energy density
\(u=\frac{1}{4}(\varepsilon\|\E\|^2+\mu\|\H\|^2)\) satisfies
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.dual-invariant-energy}
u_\xi
=
\frac{1}{4}\!\left(\varepsilon\|\E_\xi\|^2+\mu\|\H_\xi\|^2\right)
=u,
\end{equation}
and time-average Poynting vector
\(\langle\mathbf{S}\rangle=\frac{1}{2}\Re\{\E\times\H^*\}\) obeys
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.dual-invariant-poynting}
\langle\mathbf{S}\rangle_\xi
=
\frac{1}{2}\Re\{\E_\xi\times\H_\xi^*\}
=
\langle\mathbf{S}\rangle.
\end{equation}
Equations~\eqref{eq:ch1.dual-invariant-energy}--\eqref{eq:ch1.dual-invariant-poynting}
show that dual mixing redistributes electric/magnetic partitioning without changing
total transport and energy content in the symmetry-valid regime.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=6mm]
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=50mm] (in) {Input:\\$(\mathcal{O}_s,\mathbf{O}_v,\mathcal{O}_p)$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=50mm, below=of in] (A)
    {Gate A: gauge/frame admissibility\\};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=50mm, below=of A] (B)
    {Gate B: helicity closure\\};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=50mm, below=of B] (C)
    {Gate C: dual-symmetry validity\\Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.dual-invariant-energy}, \eqref{eq:ch1.dual-invariant-poynting}};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=24mm, below left=14mm and 2mm of C, fill=green!8] (acc)
    {Accepted:\\$h$, $\langle\mathbf{S}\rangle$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=24mm, below right=14mm and 2mm of C, fill=red!6] (rej)
    {Rejected:\\modeling only};
  \draw[ch1flow] (in) -- (A);
  \draw[ch1flow] (A) -- (B);
  \draw[ch1flow] (B) -- (C);
  \draw[ch1flow] (C.south west) -- (acc.north);
  \draw[ch1flow] (C.south east) -- (rej.north);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Schematic decision flow linking gauge-objective observables to helicity and dual-symmetry admissibility in source-free isotropic regions.}
\label{fig:ch1.helicity-duality-map}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.helicity-duality-map} gives the operational logic used in
subsequent sections: passing one criterion does not imply passing the others. A quantity
may satisfy gauge/frame objectivity and still fail helicity closure in a numerical
or material setting; similarly, a helicity-stable report may lose dual-symmetry
meaning when constitutive assumptions break.

To quantify departure from the symmetry regime, introduce dual-defect index
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.duality-defect-index}
\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{dual}}
=
\frac{\left\|u_\xi-u\right\|+\ell_s^{-1}\left\|\langle\mathbf{S}\rangle_\xi-\langle\mathbf{S}\rangle\right\|}
     {\left\|u\right\|+\ell_s^{-1}\left\|\langle\mathbf{S}\rangle\right\|+\epsilon_d},
\end{equation}
