where \(\ell_s>0\) is a scaling length and \(\epsilon_d>0\) regularization. In an
ideal dual-symmetric domain, \(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{dual}}=0\). In lossy,
anisotropic, magnetoelectric, or strongly dispersive media,
\(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{dual}}>0\), signaling that dual rotation cannot be used as an
exact invariance principle.

The normalized helicity-content monitor remains
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.normalized-helicity-index}
\mathcal{H}_n(\mathbf{r})=
\frac{h(\mathbf{r})}
{\frac{1}{4}\!\left(\varepsilon\|\E\|^2+\mu\|\H\|^2\right)+\epsilon_h},
\end{equation}
with \(\epsilon_h>0\). Because denominator is local energy scale,
\(\mathcal{H}_n\) compares handedness strength across nonuniform fields without
conflating magnitude variation with chirality content.

Combine all subsection criteria into one decision equation:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.helicity-observable-decision}
\mathcal{D}_{h}=1
\iff
\Big(
\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{loc}}=1,\
\mathcal{R}_h\le R_{h,\max},\
\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{dual}}\le D_{\mathrm{dual,max}}
\Big),
\end{equation}
where \(R_{h,\max}\ge 0\) and \(D_{\mathrm{dual,max}}\ge 0\) are declared
tolerances. Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.helicity-observable-decision} states that
helicity-based interpretation is accepted only when gauge locality, continuity
closure, and dual-symmetry validity are simultaneously controlled.

\begin{proposition}[Admissible helicity observable class]
\label{prop:ch1.helicity-observable-class}
In this chapter, helicity-derived reports are physically admissible observables if
and only if \(\mathcal{D}_{h}=1\).
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.observable-admissibility-triad} enforces gauge/frame
admissibility, Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.helicity-balance} with
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.helicity-closure-residual} enforces conservation closure, and
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.duality-defect-index} enforces structural validity of dual
symmetry in the medium class. Their conjunction is exactly
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.helicity-observable-decision}.

The analysis here assumes linear isotropic constitutive response in the region where
duality is invoked. Conductive loss, anisotropy, bianisotropy, and pronounced
dispersion require replacing the ideal dual-rotation interpretation by a perturbed
or broken-symmetry formulation, while keeping the gauge-objectivity filter intact
\cite{stratton1941}.

With gauge-invariant observable criteria, helicity closure, and dual-symmetry
qualification now fixed, the next subsection can define operational observables in
measurement language without conflating representation algebra with measurable
transport channels.


\subsection{Operational definitions of observables}
\label{sec:ch01.4.4}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Sections~\ref{sec:ch01.4.1}--\ref{sec:ch01.4.3} established what may be written
down without changing under gauge relabeling or rigid-frame motion. A student may
still ask the experimenter's question: ``How do I \emph{measure} it?'' That question
is not secondary; in Maxwellian electrodynamics, a quantity that cannot be reached
through a declared channel map remains physics on paper but not yet physics in the
laboratory. Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.4.4} therefore closes the gap between
symmetry-admissible field functionals and reportable numbers with uncertainty,
calibration trace, and identifiability constraints \cite{stratton1941}.

The operational viewpoint links backward to angular transport
(Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2}) and forward to Section~\ref{sec:ch01.5}. Transport
indices such as \(\Lambda_J\) and regime indices such as \(\chi\) are meaningful
only when built from channel outputs that satisfy
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.operational-measurement-model}. Conversely, a perfectly calibrated
torque sensor that reads a reactive-dominated shell without reporting \(\chi\)
violates the regime specification of Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.4}. Operational
definition is thus the language in which earlier abstract criteria become
experimentally falsifiable statements.

After gauge/frame admissibility is fixed, the experimenter's question becomes:
by what measurement chain does a symmetry-admissible field quantity become a
reported number with declared uncertainty and reproducibility? An operational
definition is a closed map from field state to instrument response, from response
to estimator, and from estimator to a traceable report protocol
\cite{poynting1884,beth1936}.

Maxwell \cite{maxwell1873} insisted that measurable quantities must be defined through
operations on bodies---forces, couples, and energy transfers---rather than through
auxiliary potentials alone. The operational layer in this subsection makes that
Maxwellian criterion explicit for angular diagnostics: a symmetry-admissible field
functional becomes an observable only when a physical transducer implements
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.operational-measurement-model} with bounded disturbance and
declared calibration trace. Stratton \cite{stratton1941} likewise separated
``geometrical'' field definitions from ``experimental'' ones when discussing waveguide
and antenna measurements, a distinction preserved here through the criteria
\(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{g}}\), \(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{loc}}\), and
\(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{op}}\).

For electric-field probes with effective length \(\mathbf{h}_e\), the phasor
response to incident \(\E\) is \(\mathbf{V}_e=\mathbf{h}_e\cdot\E+\mathbf{n}_e\).
Magnetic probes with effective area \(\mathbf{A}_m\) read
\(\mathbf{V}_m=\jj\omega\mathbf{A}_m\cdot\mathbf{B}+\mathbf{n}_m\). Composing
probes into a Poynting channel approximates
\(\widehat{P}=\Re\{\mathbf{V}_e\times\mathbf{V}_m^*/Z_0\}\) after calibration,
where \(Z_0\) is the reference impedance linking voltage and flux estimates. Channel
covariance Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.channel-covariance} requires
\(\mathbf{V}_e'=\mathbf{h}_e'\cdot\E'=\mathbf{h}_e\cdot R\E\) when the probe rotates
with the frame (\(\mathbf{h}_e'=R\mathbf{h}_e\)), not when only the field is actively
rotated while the probe remains fixed---another instance of active-passive duality
from Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.3.1}.

Let \(\mathcal{F}\) be a field state that already passes
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.observable-admissibility-triad}. Let
\(\mathcal{M}_\alpha\) denote the forward measurement operator for channel setting
\(\alpha\), \(y_\alpha\) the recorded datum, and \(n_\alpha\) additive disturbance.
The channel law is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.operational-measurement-model}
y_\alpha
=
\mathcal{M}_\alpha[\mathcal{F}]
+n_\alpha.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.operational-measurement-model} is the first operational
pillar: no observable is read from symbolic fields directly; it is inferred through
channel transduction physics plus disturbance structure
\cite{stratton1941}. The disturbance term includes thermal noise,
digitization residue, drift, and calibration bias.

Collect \(N\) channel outputs in
\(\mathbf{y}=[y_1,\dots,y_N]^\mathsf{T}\in\mathbb{C}^{N}\), and let
\(\widehat{\mathcal{O}}\) denote estimator for target observable
\(\mathcal{O}[\mathcal{F}]\). Reconstruction map \(\mathcal{R}\) is accepted only if
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.operational-consistency}
\widehat{\mathcal{O}}=\mathcal{R}[\mathbf{y}],
\qquad
\lim_{\|n\|\rightarrow 0}\widehat{\mathcal{O}}
=
\mathcal{O}[\mathcal{F}],
\end{equation}
where \(\|n\|\) is norm on disturbance vector. First relation defines estimator
architecture; second relation enforces asymptotic consistency. If either fails, the
quantity is mathematically expressible yet operationally undefined.

Consistency alone is insufficient. The inverse problem must also be identifiable.
Let \(\mathcal{S}_{\mathcal{O}}\) be admissible state set restricted by the
symmetry/gauge criterions from Sections~\ref{sec:ch01.3}--\ref{sec:ch01.4}. Then
observability-identifiability for \(\mathcal{O}\) requires
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.observable-identifiability}
\forall \mathcal{F}_1,\mathcal{F}_2\in\mathcal{S}_{\mathcal{O}}:\ 
\mathbf{M}[\mathcal{F}_1]=\mathbf{M}[\mathcal{F}_2]
\Longrightarrow
\mathcal{O}[\mathcal{F}_1]=\mathcal{O}[\mathcal{F}_2],
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{M}=[\mathcal{M}_1,\ldots,\mathcal{M}_N]^\mathsf{T}\). The logical
content of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.observable-identifiability} is fundamental: if two
admissible states are indistinguishable by the entire channel set but produce
different \(\mathcal{O}\), then no estimator can assign that observable uniquely.

In practice, identification quality is measured by local sensitivity. Let
\(\boldsymbol{\theta}\in\mathbb{R}^p\) parameterize admissible states, with Jacobian
\(\mathbf{J}_{\alpha i}=\partial \mathcal{M}_\alpha/\partial \theta_i\), and let
\(\mathbf{W}\succ 0\) be channel-weight matrix. Define weighted information matrix
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.operational-information-matrix}
\mathbf{G}
=
\mathbf{J}^\mathsf{H}\mathbf{W}\mathbf{J},
\end{equation}
where \((\cdot)^\mathsf{H}\) denotes Hermitian transpose. A poorly conditioned
\(\mathbf{G}\) indicates ill-posed inversion and large estimator variance
amplification; a well-conditioned \(\mathbf{G}\) indicates robust local inversion.

Uncertainty in reported observable is summarized by first-order propagation:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.operational-uncertainty-budget}
\sigma_{\mathcal{O}}^2
\approx
\nabla_{\boldsymbol{\theta}}\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{T}}
\mathbf{\Sigma}_{\theta}
\nabla_{\boldsymbol{\theta}}\mathcal{O}
+\sigma_{\mathrm{cal}}^2
+\sigma_{\mathrm{model}}^2,
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{\Sigma}_{\theta}\) is parameter covariance induced by channel noise,
\(\sigma_{\mathrm{cal}}^2\) is calibration contribution, and
\(\sigma_{\mathrm{model}}^2\) is model-mismatch contribution. This equation makes
explicit that uncertainty is a structural part of the definition of an operational
observable, not an optional appendix \cite{stratton1941}.

Gauge and frame admissibility must hold at the channel level, not only at symbolic
field level. For rotated state \(\mathcal{F}'=\mathcal{A}_R\mathcal{F}\) and
gauge-related potentials \((\mathbf{A}',\phi')\), each channel must satisfy
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.channel-covariance}
\mathcal{M}_\alpha[\mathcal{F}']
=
\mathcal{T}_\alpha(R)\,\mathcal{M}_\alpha[\mathcal{F}],
\qquad
\mathcal{M}_\alpha[\mathbf{A}',\phi']
=
\mathcal{M}_\alpha[\mathbf{A},\phi],
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal{T}_\alpha(R)\) is known channel transformation law. Violation of
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.channel-covariance} means the instrumentation itself injects
symmetry bias, and any reported value cannot be claimed as observer-independent.

Calibration closure provides a direct test. Let
\(\mathcal{M}^{\mathrm{ref}}_\alpha\) be reference response and
\(\widehat{\mathcal{M}}_\alpha\) calibrated model used in inversion. Define
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.channel-calibration-residual}
\rho_{\alpha}
=
\frac{\left\|\widehat{\mathcal{M}}_\alpha-\mathcal{M}^{\mathrm{ref}}_\alpha\right\|}
     {\left\|\mathcal{M}^{\mathrm{ref}}_\alpha\right\|+\epsilon_{\alpha}},
\qquad
\rho_{\max}=\max_{\alpha}\rho_\alpha,
\end{equation}
with \(\epsilon_\alpha>0\) regularization. Small \(\rho_{\max}\) certifies that the
forward model used by \(\mathcal{R}\) is compatible with metrological reference
behavior over the declared operating domain.

An operational report is accepted only when the operational layer in
Table~\ref{tab:ch1.admissibility-corollaries} is satisfied:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.observable-report-contract}
\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{op}}=1
\iff
\Big(
\mathcal{D}_{h}=1,\
\kappa(\mathbf{G})\le \kappa_{\max},\
\rho_{\max}\le \rho_{\max}^{\mathrm{tol}},\
\sigma_{\mathcal{O}}\le \sigma_{\max}
\Big),
\end{equation}
where \(\kappa(\mathbf{G})\) is condition number of
\(\mathbf{G}\), \(\kappa_{\max}\) conditioning tolerance,
\(\rho_{\max}^{\mathrm{tol}}\) calibration tolerance, and \(\sigma_{\max}\)
uncertainty tolerance. This equation closes the operational definition in a form
that is testable, reproducible, and directly reusable in Chapters~2--4.

\begin{proposition}[Operational observable criterion]
\label{prop:ch1.operational-observable-criterion}
A field-derived quantity in this chapter is operationally admissible as an
observable if and only if \(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{op}}=1\).
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
The condition \(\mathcal{D}_{h}=1\) imports symmetry, gauge, and helicity validity
from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.4.3}. Identifiability and conditioning enforce stable
invertibility of data-to-observable mapping. Calibration and uncertainty bounds
ensure metrological reproducibility. Removing any clause breaks at least one of the
requirements of operational reportability.

For torque channels used later, the operational form becomes
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.operational-torque-channel}
\tau_z^{\mathrm{meas}}
=
\int_S\left(\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{t}\right)\cdot\hat{\mathbf{z}}\,dS
+\delta\tau_z,
\end{equation}
where \(S\) is sensing surface, \(\mathbf{r}\) position relative to declared origin,
\(\mathbf{t}\) traction vector from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.traction-rotation-covariance},
\(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) measurement axis, and \(\delta\tau_z\) net channel disturbance.
The deterministic integral and disturbance term must be reported jointly; otherwise
the quantity is incomplete from an operational standpoint.

To compare setups with different torque scales, define normalized channel
reliability index
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.operational-torque-reliability}
\mathcal{Q}_{\tau}
=
\frac{\left|\tau_z^{\mathrm{meas}}-\tau_z^{\mathrm{model}}\right|}
     {\left|\tau_z^{\mathrm{model}}\right|+\epsilon_{\tau}},
\end{equation}
where \(\tau_z^{\mathrm{model}}\) is model prediction under calibrated channel model
and \(\epsilon_\tau>0\) regularization. Small \(\mathcal{Q}_{\tau}\) indicates
agreement between field-based prediction and measured report within declared
instrument protocol.

This subsection assumes channels that are linear or weakly nonlinear around the
operating point, and disturbance statistics that permit stable covariance
estimation. Strong hysteresis, severe nonstationarity, hidden cross-couplings, or
unknown transfer nonlinearities require extended inverse frameworks beyond the
present chapter \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: two-probe Poynting reconstruction on a paraxial beam.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,-0.15) -- (0,2.9) node[ch1oncanvas, above] {$\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \fill[ch1src] (0,0.55) circle (1.5pt) node[ch1oncanvas, left=11mm] {$\mathbf{r}_e$};
  \fill[ch1src] (0,1.85) circle (1.5pt) node[ch1oncanvas, right=11mm] {$\mathbf{r}_m$};
  \draw[thick, blue!60!black] (-0.35,0.55) -- (0.35,0.55);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, blue!60!black] at (-0.85,0.55) {$\mathbf{h}_e$};
  \draw[thick, orange!85!black] (0.55,1.85) circle (0.22);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, orange!85!black] at (1.05,1.85) {$\mathbf{A}_m$};
  \draw[<->, thick, gray!70!black] (0.25,0.55) -- (0.25,1.85)
    node[midway, right, ch1lblout] {$\Delta z\!\ll\!\lambda_0$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, align=center] at (0,-0.65)
    {$\widehat{\Pi}=\Re\{V_e V_m^*/(2Z_{\mathrm{cal}})\}$;\quad
     $\mathbf{G}$, };
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Worked-problem schematic: collinear electric and magnetic probes reconstructing paraxial power density on axis.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-two-probe-poynting}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-two-probe-poynting} maps probe voltages to \(\widehat{\Pi}\) through Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.channel-calibration-residual}: identifiability fails when a second polarization mode is present but only one probe orientation is deployed.
Consider square-law electric probe at \(\mathbf{r}_e\) and loop probe at
\(\mathbf{r}_m\) separated by \(\Delta z\ll\lambda_0\) on axis \(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\).
Calibration yields \(\mathbf{h}_e=h_e\hat{\mathbf{x}}\) and
\(\mathbf{A}_m=A_m\hat{\mathbf{y}}\). For a paraxial mode with transverse
\(\E\approx E_0\hat{\mathbf{x}}\) on axis, \(\mathbf{V}_e=h_e E_0+\mathbf{n}_e\) and
\(\mathbf{V}_m=\jj\omega A_m B_0+\mathbf{n}_m\) with \(B_0=E_0/(\jj\omega)\partial_z E_x\)
in the quasi-TEM limit. Reconstructed power density
\(\widehat{\Pi}=\Re\{V_e V_m^*/(2Z_{\mathrm{cal}})\}\) matches model
\(\Pi\) from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.2} when \(\rho_{\max}<0.05\) in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.channel-calibration-residual}. Jacobian entries
\(\partial\widehat{\Pi}/\partial E_0\) populate \(\mathbf{G}\) in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.operational-information-matrix}; with
\(\sigma_{E_0}/E_0=2\%\), Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.operational-uncertainty-budget} gives
\(\sigma_\Pi/\Pi\approx 3\%\). Identifiability
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.observable-identifiability} fails if a second mode with orthogonal
polarization is present but only one probe orientation is used, because
\(\mathbf{M}[\mathcal{F}_1]=\mathbf{M}[\mathcal{F}_2]\) while \(\Pi\) differs.
Operational admissibility therefore requires sufficient channel diversity, not merely
low noise \cite{maxwell1873,stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Operational walk-through: torque channel with uncertainty budget.}
Suppose a torsion-balance readout reports \(y_\tau=-3.10\times 10^{-11}\,\mathrm{N\,m}\)
with calibration \(K_\tau=1.02\pm0.01\) and model prediction
\(\tau_z^{\mathrm{model}}=-3.18\times 10^{-11}\,\mathrm{N\,m}\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.operational-torque-channel} for \((m,\sigma)=(1,+1)\) and
\(P_{\mathrm{rad}}=1\,\mathrm{W}\) at \(f=10\,\mathrm{GHz}\). Parameterize the
admissible state by single-mode power \(P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) and helical index
\((m,\sigma)\). The Jacobian entry
\(\partial y_\tau/\partial P_{\mathrm{rad}}=(m+\sigma)/(\omega K_\tau)\)
feeds Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.operational-information-matrix}. With
\(\sigma_{P}=0.02\,\mathrm{W}\) on the power channel, first-order propagation in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.operational-uncertainty-budget} gives
\(\sigma_{\tau_z}\approx 6\times 10^{-13}\,\mathrm{N\,m}\), dominated by power
calibration at this flux level. Reliability index \(\mathcal{Q}_\tau\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.operational-torque-reliability} is then \(\approx 0.025\), and
\(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{op}}=1\) is credible if \(\mathcal{D}_h=1\),
\(\kappa(\mathbf{G})<10^2\), and \(\rho_{\max}<0.05\) from
Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.channel-calibration-residual} and
\eqref{eq:ch1.observable-report-contract}. The walk-through shows that operational
admissibility is a joint statement about physics \emph{and} metrology: a correct
conservation prediction can still fail as an observable report if uncertainty or
calibration checks are omitted \cite{stratton1941}.

Operational definition is therefore fixed in rigorous terms: an observable is a
symmetry-admissible field quantity equipped with identifiable channel map, stable
reconstruction, calibrated closure, and explicit uncertainty budget. The following
subsection uses this foundation to show why local spin-orbit decompositions remain
interpretive tools unless they satisfy the same operational criteria.


\subsection{Limits of local spin-orbit decompositions}
\label{sec:ch01.4.5}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Subsections~\ref{sec:ch01.4.1}--\ref{sec:ch01.4.4} established gauge transformations,
gauge-sensitive local maps, gauge-invariant observables including helicity, and the
operational channel specification that turns field functionals into reportable numbers.
The most persistent interpretive temptation in angular electrodynamics is to
partition total electromagnetic angular-momentum density
\(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\) into ``spin'' and ``orbit'' parts at each point in
space. Such partitions are widely used in optics and near-field imaging because
they suggest intuitive pictures---polarization circulation as spin, azimuthal phase
gradient as orbit---yet Maxwell theory does not assign a unique pointwise pair
\((\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}},\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}})\) unless convention,
gauge, boundary data, and measurement kernel are declared simultaneously
\cite{stratton1941}.

Local spin-orbit decomposition remains mathematically valuable but physically
delicate: two admissible formalisms may assign different local ``spin-like'' and
``orbital-like'' maps while preserving identical total angular-momentum transfer.
The limits established here are constructive rather than prohibitive: local splits
serve as interpretation tools, and they are promoted to direct observables only
when gauge, boundary, and channel-level closure are all verified. Decomposition is
not forbidden; the task is to state precisely when decomposition outputs carry the
same epistemic status as stress-flux integrals and when they remain
convention-tagged diagnostics attached to a fixed total \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Canonical split families (equivalence class viewpoint).}
Two split families appear repeatedly in the literature and differ even when
\(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\) is held fixed. \emph{Family~A} assigns orbital content
to a cross-product structure \(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}\propto\mathbf{r}\times(\varepsilon_0\E)\)
built from potentials or fields, and spin content to a complementary bilinear term
in \(\E\) and \(\H\). \emph{Family~B} shifts content using a superpotential
\(\mathbf{C}\) so that \(\divg\mathbf{C}\) redistributes labels without altering
\(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\). Neither family is ``wrong'' in isolation; each defines
an admissible representative of the same decomposition class. Physical disagreement
arises only when two groups report Family~A and Family~B maps as if they were
directly comparable observables without stating convention or passing the closure
criteria developed below \cite{belinfante1940}.

The Belinfante--Rosenfeld construction \cite{belinfante1940} explains the
mathematical origin of Family~B: symmetrizing the canonical energy-momentum tensor
requires adding a divergence term built from spin current, which reappears in local
angular-momentum splits as the superpotential \(\mathbf{C}\) in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-spin-orbit-split}. In Maxwellian electrodynamics the
practical consequence is strict: local spin-orbit images are gauge- and
convention-dependent coordinates on a conserved total
\cite{stratton1941}.

Belinfante \cite{belinfante1940} derived the symmetric energy-momentum tensor by
requiring that the total angular-momentum density
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.belinfante-total-am-density}
\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{tot}}
=
\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{sym}}
+\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}}
\end{equation}
obey \(\partial_t\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{tot}}+\divg\mathbf{M}_{\mathrm{sym}}
=-\boldsymbol{\tau}_{\mathrm{ext}}\) with symmetric \(\mathbf{T}^{\mathrm{sym}}\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.belinfante-symmetric-tensor} and conserved integrated momentum.
Here \(\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{sym}}\) is momentum density from the symmetric tensor,
and \(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}}\) is the spatial Belinfante spin density. Maxwell's
original stress-based torque law \cite{maxwell1873} controls the symmetric branch;
the canonical potential branch differs by the spin-flux divergence in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.belinfante-symmetric-tensor}. Family~B splits are
Belinfante-class representatives in which spin-labeled content is concentrated in
\(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}}\) and orbit-labeled content in
\(\mathbf{r}\times\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{sym}}\), modulo superpotential \(\mathbf{C}\)
in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-spin-orbit-split}.

For monochromatic Maxwell fields in vacuum phasors,
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.belinfante-spin-density}
\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}}
=
\frac{1}{2\jj\omega}\,\E^*\times\E
\;+\;
\frac{\mu_0}{2\jj\omega}\,\H^*\times\H,
\end{equation}
up to total-divergence terms movable into \(\mathbf{C}\). Equation
\eqref{eq:ch1.belinfante-spin-density} is gauge-invariant because it depends only on
\(\E,\H\). Orbital-labeled density
\(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{Bel}}=\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}-\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}}\)
then splits \(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\) into gauge-invariant summands, unlike
potential-weighted maps that shift under
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-gauge-redistribution}. Non-uniqueness persists because
any redistribution satisfying Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-equivalence-class} produces
an equally valid local picture \cite{belinfante1940,stratton1941}.

Let \(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}(\mathbf{r})\) denote electromagnetic
angular-momentum density already fixed in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-momentum-balance}. A generic local decomposition takes
the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.local-spin-orbit-split}
\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}
=
\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}
\;+\;
\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}
\;+\;
\divg\mathbf{C},
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}\) is orbital-labeled local density,
\(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}\) is spin-labeled local density, \(\mathbf{C}\) is a
second-rank superpotential tensor, and \(\divg\mathbf{C}\) is the divergence
correction generated by decomposition convention. Equation
\eqref{eq:ch1.local-spin-orbit-split} makes clear that label assignment is not
unique at point level: superpotential terms can move content between
\(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}\) and \(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}\) while preserving
\(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\). This means local decomposition does not define a
single physical density pair; it defines an admissible equivalence class.

To represent this class explicitly, let
\((\mathbf{l}_a,\mathbf{s}_a,\mathbf{C}_a)\) and
\((\mathbf{l}_b,\mathbf{s}_b,\mathbf{C}_b)\) denote two admissible local splits of
the same \(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\). Their difference can always be written
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-equivalence-class}
\mathbf{l}_b-\mathbf{l}_a
=
\divg\mathbf{Q}+\mathbf{q},
\qquad
\mathbf{s}_b-\mathbf{s}_a
=
-\divg\mathbf{Q}-\mathbf{q},
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{Q}\) is redistribution tensor and \(\mathbf{q}\) is residual
balancing vector. The paired signs guarantee that local relabeling does not alter
the summed quantity \(\mathbf{l}+\mathbf{s}\), but they do alter each local
component separately.

Under a gauge update \((\mathbf{A},\phi)\to(\mathbf{A}',\phi')\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.gauge-transform}, the local pair transforms as
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-gauge-redistribution}
\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}'
=
\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}+\divg\mathbf{U}_\chi+\mathbf{v}_\chi,\qquad
\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}'
=
\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}-\divg\mathbf{U}_\chi-\mathbf{v}_\chi,
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{U}_\chi\) is gauge-generated redistribution tensor and
\(\mathbf{v}_\chi\) is residual redistribution vector. The paired signs enforce
that \(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}'+\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}'
=\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}+\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}\), but individual local
components remain gauge-sensitive. In functional terms,
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-gauge-redistribution} moves between admissible local
representatives; in physical terms, it explains why local spin-orbit maps can
differ without contradicting total transport predictions.

Integrating over a finite volume \(V\) with boundary \(\partial V\) and outward
unit normal \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\) yields
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-volume-test}
\int_V\!\left(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}'+\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}'\right)\!dV
=
\int_V\!\left(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}+\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}\right)\!dV
=
\int_V\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV
\;-\;
\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{C}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS.
\end{equation}
The first equality shows decomposition invariance of the sum; the second shows
explicit boundary dependence through \(\mathbf{C}\). Therefore local partitions
can be physically compared only after boundary prescription is declared, exactly
as required by the control-balance logic in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}.

For two admissible split representatives \(a\) and \(b\), integrated difference
obeys
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-boundary-closure}
\Delta_V^{(ab)}
\equiv
\int_V(\mathbf{s}_b-\mathbf{s}_a)\,dV
=
-\int_V(\mathbf{l}_b-\mathbf{l}_a)\,dV
=
\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{Q}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS+\int_V\mathbf{q}\,dV.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-boundary-closure} resolves disagreement into
boundary and volumetric channels, which must both be controlled before integral
spin-orbit attribution can be interpreted as physically comparable.

To quantify decomposition fragility for a given field realization, define
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-nonuniqueness-index}
\kappa_{\mathrm{so}}(V)=
\frac{\int_V\|\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}'-\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}\|\,dV}
{\int_V\|\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\|\,dV+\epsilon_{\mathrm{so}}},
\end{equation}
where \(\|\cdot\|\) is Euclidean norm and \(\epsilon_{\mathrm{so}}>0\) is a small
regularization constant. Small \(\kappa_{\mathrm{so}}\) indicates decomposition
stability for that setup; large \(\kappa_{\mathrm{so}}\) signals strong
representation sensitivity and therefore reduced direct observability of local
spin/orbit attribution \cite{stratton1941}.

Operationally reconstructed split components must also satisfy internal closure.
Let \(\widehat{\mathbf{s}}_{\mathrm{loc}}\),
\(\widehat{\mathbf{l}}_{\mathrm{loc}}\), and
\(\widehat{\mathbf{j}}_{\mathrm{em}}\) be channel-reconstructed fields from
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.4.4}. Define split-closure residual
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-measurement-residual}
\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{so}}
=
\frac{
\left\|
\widehat{\mathbf{s}}_{\mathrm{loc}}
+\widehat{\mathbf{l}}_{\mathrm{loc}}
-\widehat{\mathbf{j}}_{\mathrm{em}}
\right\|
}{
\left\|\widehat{\mathbf{j}}_{\mathrm{em}}\right\|+\epsilon_r},
\end{equation}
where \(\epsilon_r>0\) is regularization. If \(\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{so}}\) is not
small, then even before gauge comparison the measured local split fails closure.

The operational decision criterion for direct local-spin/local-orbit observability
is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-observable-decision}
\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{so}}=1
\iff
\Big(
\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{op}}=1,\
\kappa_{\mathrm{so}}\le \kappa_{\mathrm{so,max}},\
\mathcal{R}_{\mathrm{so}}\le R_{\mathrm{so,max}},\
\|\Delta_V^{(ab)}\|\le \Delta_{\max}
\Big),
\end{equation}
with declared tolerances
\(\kappa_{\mathrm{so,max}}\), \(R_{\mathrm{so,max}}\), and \(\Delta_{\max}\). This
condition is intentionally strict: it prevents representation-sensitive local
decompositions from being reported as if they were unique observables.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=5mm]
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=44mm, fill=blue!8] (anchor)
    {anchor: $\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}$ (gauge-invariant)};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=22mm, below left=14mm and 0mm of anchor] (A)
    {Rep.~A:\\$(\mathbf{l}_A,\mathbf{s}_A)$\\$+\mathbf{C}_A$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=22mm, below right=14mm and 0mm of anchor] (B)
    {Rep.~B:\\$(\mathbf{l}_B,\mathbf{s}_B)$\\};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=44mm, below=14mm of $(A)!0.5!(B)$] (sum)
    {$\mathbf{l}+\mathbf{s}$ fixed;\ $\int_V$ depends on $\partial V$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=44mm, below=of sum, fill=green!8] (ok)
    {$\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{so}}=1$: local labels;\ else total $\int_V\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV$};
  \draw[ch1flow] (anchor.south west) -- (A.north);
  \draw[ch1flow] (anchor.south east) -- (B.north);
  \draw[ch1flow] (A.south) -- (sum.north west);
  \draw[ch1flow] (B.south) -- (sum.north east);
  \draw[ch1flow] (sum) -- (ok);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Schematic of admissible local spin-orbit split class and reporting branches in Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.4.5}.}
\label{fig:ch1.spin-orbit-equivalence}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.spin-orbit-equivalence} summarizes the epistemic hierarchy
without replacing the equations: the anchor object is \(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\);
local spin and orbit maps are coordinates on an equivalence class; integrals over
\(V\) inherit boundary sensitivity through \(\mathbf{C}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\).

\paragraph{Worked problem: Belinfante spin density on a transverse disk.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (-2.2,0) -- (2.2,0) node[right] {$\hat{\mathbf{x}}$};
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,-0.15) -- (0,0.15) node[ch1oncanvas, above] {$\hat{\mathbf{y}}$};
  \draw[thick, blue!70!black] (0,0) circle (1.5);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, blue!70!black] at (0,-0.55) {$V_d$:\ $\rho_0=\lambda_0/2$,\ $z=0$};
  \draw[->, very thick, teal!70!black] (0,0) -- (0,1.35)
    node[midway, right, ch1lblout] {$\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}}\parallel\hat{\mathbf{k}}$};
  \draw[->, thick, orange!85!black] (1.5,0) arc (0:300:1.5);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, orange!85!black, align=center] at (2.35,0.55)
    {$\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{Bel}}$\\azimuthal phase};
  \draw[->, thick, gray!70!black] (0,-0.15) -- (0,-1.05)
    node[midway, right, ch1lblout] {$+\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, align=center] at (0,-1.55)
    {$\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{Bel}}+\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}}=\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}$ on $V_d$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Worked-problem schematic: Belinfante spin and orbital-labeled densities on a transverse disk in a circularly polarized paraxial field.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-belinfante-disk}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-belinfante-disk} shows that azimuthal structure in \(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{Bel}}\) coexists with uniform spin magnitude: only the sum \(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{Bel}}+\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}}\) equals \(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\) on \(V_d\).
On disk \(V_d\) of radius \(\rho_0=\lambda_0/2\) in plane \(z=0\), adopt the
transverse field template of Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.1} with
\(\hat{\mathbf{k}}=\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) and right-circular polarization without
re-deriving the Maxwell solution. Belinfante spin density
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.belinfante-spin-density} is uniform in magnitude over \(V_d\)
and parallel to \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}\). Orbital-labeled density
\(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{Bel}}=\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}-\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}}\)
carries azimuthal structure from phase gradient, yet
\(\int_{V_d}(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{Bel}}+\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}})\,dV=
\int_{V_d}\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV\). Adding redistribution
\(\mathbf{l}'=\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{Bel}}+\divg\mathbf{Q}\),
\(\mathbf{s}'=\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}}-\divg\mathbf{Q}\) with
\(\mathbf{Q}=\tfrac12\mathbf{r}\times(\mathbf{r}\times\E)\) moves density between
labels without changing the sum. Nonuniqueness index \(\kappa_{\mathrm{so}}\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-nonuniqueness-index} is order unity between Belinfante
and all-orbit representatives, so \(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{so}}=0\) for direct local
spin fraction even when \(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{op}}=1\) for total
\(\int_{V_d}\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV\). The laboratory report must state total
angular content plus split convention (Family~A, Family~B, or Belinfante)
\cite{belinfante1940,maxwell1873}.

\paragraph{Regime dependence: near field versus far field.}
Split stability is not uniform in radius. In reactive-dominated shells where
\(|Q_{\mathrm{re}}|\gg P_{\mathrm{rad}}\) in the language of
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.4}, local circulation of \(\E,\H\) can suggest strong
``orbital'' density while exported torque remains negligible. In radiative-dominated
shells where \(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=1\), the same split convention often
produces smoother maps because outward propagative support aligns local phase
gradients with export direction. A spin-orbit image computed at \(kr=0.5\) therefore
cannot be compared quantitatively to one computed at \(kr=10\) without reporting
\(\chi(r)\) and the split convention; otherwise regime mixing is misread as
physical disagreement \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: equivalent splits, different local labels.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=44mm, fill=blue!8] (j0)
    {anchor $\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}=\mathbf{j}_0$ on $V$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=22mm, below left=14mm and 0mm of j0] (repA)
    {Rep.~A:\\$\mathbf{l}^{(A)}=\mathbf{j}_0$\\$\mathbf{s}^{(A)}=\mathbf{0}$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=22mm, below right=14mm and 0mm of j0] (repB)
    {Rep.~B:\\$\mathbf{s}^{(B)}$ Belinfante\\$\mathbf{l}^{(B)}=\mathbf{j}_0-\mathbf{s}^{(B)}$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=44mm, below=14mm of $(repA)!0.5!(repB)$] (Q)
    {$\mathbf{l}'=\mathbf{l}+\divg\mathbf{Q}$,\ $\mathbf{s}'=\mathbf{s}-\divg\mathbf{Q}$};
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=gray!8, align=center, minimum width=48mm] at (0,-3.15) {sum invariant;\ local maps differ ()};
  \draw[ch1flow] (j0.south west) -- (repA.north);
  \draw[ch1flow] (j0.south east) -- (repB.north);
  \draw[ch1flow] (repA.south) -- (Q.north west);
  \draw[ch1flow] (repB.south) -- (Q.north east);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Worked-problem schematic: two admissible local representatives sharing one gauge-invariant anchor density.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-equivalent-splits}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-equivalent-splits} fixes the anchor before comparing labels: equivalent splits in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-equivalence-class} alter local maps without changing the integrated angular-momentum content on \(V\).
\emph{Step 1---Fix the anchor.}
Let \(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}=\mathbf{j}_0(\mathbf{r})\) on volume \(V\) be the
gauge-invariant density from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-momentum-balance}. All splits
must sum to \(\mathbf{j}_0\).
\emph{Physical meaning:} the anchor is the only object with unconditional claim to
total angular content; splits are coordinates on an equivalence class.

\emph{Step 2---Choose representative~A.}
Set \(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}^{(A)}=\mathbf{j}_0\), \(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}^{(A)}=\mathbf{0}\).
\emph{Physical meaning:} this is the ``all-orbit'' labeling convention; it is
admissible but not canonical.

\emph{Step 3---Choose representative~B.}
Add \(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}^{(B)}=\tfrac12\nabla\times(\mathbf{r}\times\E)\) and
adjust \(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}^{(B)}=\mathbf{j}_0-\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}^{(B)}\)
so Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-spin-orbit-split} holds with \(\mathbf{C}=\mathbf{0}\).
\emph{Physical meaning:} spin-labeled content is redirected from the orbit channel
without changing \(\mathbf{j}_0\).

\emph{Step 4---Compare pointwise and integrated reports.}
Both splits yield \(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{int}}=\int_V\mathbf{j}_0\,dV\), yet
\(\|\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}^{(A)}-\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}^{(B)}\|\) is order
unity on much of \(V\).
\emph{Physical meaning:} integrated totals agree while local ratios disagree---the
signature of non-uniqueness, not measurement error.

\emph{Step 5---Apply criteria.}
Nonuniqueness index \(\kappa_{\mathrm{so}}\) from
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-nonuniqueness-index} is order unity; split-closure
residual Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-measurement-residual} must still be checked on
reconstructed channels. Hence \(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{so}}=0\) unless tolerances in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-observable-decision} are met.
\emph{Physical meaning:} report \(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{int}}\) as observable; report
local spin fraction only with convention tag \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Helicity versus spin-orbit labels.}
Helicity, developed in Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.4.3}, is a gauge-invariant
sign-odd measure of handedness tied to \(\E,\H\) through dual symmetry. Local
spin-orbit splits, by contrast, are gauge- and convention-sensitive maps on
\(\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\). A field may carry definite helicity on a closed surface
while local spin density varies with potential choice; conversely, a visually
``spin-rich'' near-field map may carry negligible exported helicity flux. The two
concepts answer different questions: helicity classifies chiral coupling of
\(\E\) and \(\H\); spin-orbit decomposition classifies how total angular density is
\emph{labeled} locally. Neither replaces Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.angular-balance-integral}
for torque verification \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Experimental spin torque versus local spin density.}
Beth \cite{beth1936} measured mechanical torque on a quartz wave plate illuminated
by circularly polarized light, establishing that polarization handedness couples to
a definite mechanical response at laboratory scale. That experiment is an
\emph{integral} spin measurement: the plate integrates photon spin over its aperture
and reports a single \(\tau_z\). Local spin densities such as
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.belinfante-spin-density}, by contrast, assign signed content to
each point in space and vary with split convention. The epistemic hierarchy is
therefore strict: Beth-type torque validates the spin \emph{channel} of Maxwell
theory; Belinfante-type maps supply a gauge-invariant local \emph{label} that still
admits redistribution among orbit and spin slots through
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-equivalence-class}. A modern near-field micrograph of
``spin density'' without stating split family and control volume is not comparable
to Beth's torque even when both figures appear equally compelling
\cite{maxwell1873,belinfante1940}.

\paragraph{Single-mode orbital channel (Allen) under Belinfante labeling.}
For a paraxial mode with azimuthal index \(m\) and single circular polarization
\(\sigma=+1\), Allen \emph{et al.} \cite{allen1992} showed that each photon in the
mode carries orbital index \(m\hbar\) in the paraxial limit. In the present
frequency-domain language, define modal power \(P_m\) on a control surface and
modal angular export \(\dot J_z^{(m)}\). The channel law inherited from
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.5} gives \(\dot J_z^{(m)}\approx (m+\sigma)P_m/\omega\).
Belinfante spin density Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.belinfante-spin-density} contributes the
\(\sigma\)-linked handedness locally, while azimuthal phase gradient contributes
orbital-labeled content in \(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{Bel}}\). A modal spin fraction
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.so-modal-channel-split}
f_s^{(m)}=
\frac{\int_{V_m}\|\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}}\|\,dV}
{\int_{V_m}\|\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\|\,dV+\epsilon_s},
\end{equation}
with \(\epsilon_s>0\), is convention-dependent: changing split representative via
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-equivalence-class} alters \(f_s^{(m)}\) without
changing \(\dot J_z^{(m)}\). The observable modal quantity is the channel power and
flux integral, not the ratio \(f_s^{(m)}\) unless
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-observable-decision} is satisfied
\cite{allen1992,belinfante1940}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: electric dipole near zone versus far zone with one split.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.88]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,-0.55) -- (0,3.35) node[ch1oncanvas, above] {$\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \fill[ch1src] (0,0.75) circle (1.5pt);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, left=12mm] at (0,0.75) {$\mathbf{p}$};
  \draw[thick, blue!60!black] (0,0.48) -- (0,1.02);
  \draw[ch1shellO] (0,0.75) circle (0.95);
  \draw[ch1shell] (0,0.75) circle (2.15);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, orange!85!black] at (0:0.95) {$S_{r_a}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, red!70!black] at (0:2.15) {$S_{r_b}$};
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=orange!12, orange!85!black, align=center, minimum width=34mm]
    at (-4.30,1.55) {Inner shell block:\\$S_{r_a}$,\ $kr_a=0.8$\\reactive:\ $\chi(r_a)\!\gg\!1$};
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=red!10, red!70!black, align=center, minimum width=34mm]
    at (4.30,1.55) {Outer shell block:\\$S_{r_b}$,\ $kr_b=8$\\radiative:\ $\chi(r_b)\!\ll\!1$};
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=gray!8, align=center, minimum width=48mm] at (0,-2.85)
    {Comparison block:\\same Belinfante split;\\compare $\|\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{Bel}}\|$ vs.\ $\int_S\mathbf{M}\!\cdot\!\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Worked-problem schematic: identical Belinfante split evaluated on reactive versus radiative spherical shells surrounding a short dipole.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-dipole-near-far-split}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-dipole-near-far-split} contrasts shell radii through the regime index \(\chi(r)\): large local split labels on \(S_{r_a}\) do not authorize export-compatible torque interpretation unless \(\mathcal{D}_{J,\mathrm{reg}}=1\) on the chosen surface.
Consider \(\mathbf{p}=p_0\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) at \(f=1\,\mathrm{GHz}\). Evaluate
Belinfante split on spherical shells \(S_{r_a}\) and \(S_{r_b}\) with
\(kr_a=0.8\) and \(kr_b=8\), using Eqs.~\eqref{eq:ch1.belinfante-spin-density}
and \(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{Bel}}=\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}-\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{Bel}}\)
throughout. On \(S_{r_a}\), reactive circulation dominates
(\(\chi(r_a)\gg 1\) from Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.2.4}), and
\(\|\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{Bel}}\|\) is large while \(|\dot J_z|\) through the shell
remains small. On \(S_{r_b}\), \(\chi(r_b)\ll 1\) and the same split convention
yields \(\int_{S_{r_b}}\mathbf{M}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS\) consistent with
\((m+\sigma)P/\omega\) for the dominant dipole channel. Nonuniqueness index
\(\kappa_{\mathrm{so}}\) is similar on both shells, but only the far-zone shell
supports export-compatible torque interpretation. Reporting ``spin fraction'' from
\(S_{r_a}\) without \(\chi(r_a)\) therefore conflates storage geometry with
transport---the same regime error diagnosed in Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.6.4}
\cite{chu1948}.

\paragraph{Numerical split comparison on a paraxial beam segment.}
On a cylindrical volume \(V\) of radius \(\rho_0=2\lambda_0\) and length
\(L=4\lambda_0\) aligned with \(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\), suppose a paraxial mode has
\(\int_V\|\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\|\,dV = J_0\). Split~A concentrates orbital
label in \(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}^{(A)}\) with
\(\int_V\|\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}^{(A)}\|\,dV = 0.05\,J_0\).
Split~B, related by Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-equivalence-class} with
\(\|\mathbf{q}\|\) negligible on \(V\), yields
\(\int_V\|\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}^{(B)}\|\,dV = 0.42\,J_0\) and
\(\int_V\|\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}^{(B)}\|\,dV = 0.58\,J_0\) while preserving
\(\int_V(\mathbf{l}+\mathbf{s})\,dV = J_0\). Nonuniqueness index
\(\kappa_{\mathrm{so}}\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-nonuniqueness-index}
is then \(\approx 0.37\), projection sensitivity
\(\mathcal{P}_{\mathrm{so}}\) from Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-projection-sensitivity}
exceeds unity for equal-weight kernels \(w_a,w_b\) sampling different radial
windows, and \(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{so}}=0\) under declared thresholds. The
correct report is: total angular content \(J_0\) on \(V\) is stable; local
spin-to-orbit ratio is convention-dependent \cite{stratton1941}.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Reporting status for spin-orbit split outputs (Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.4.5}).}
\label{tab:ch1.spin-orbit-reporting}
\begin{tabular}{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
Quantity & Typical status & Required criterion \\
\midrule
\(\int_V\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{em}}\,dV\) & direct/constrained observable &
\(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{op}}=1\), boundary declared \\
\(\int_V(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}+\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}})\,dV\) & same class as total &
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-volume-test} \\
\(\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}(\mathbf{r})\), \(\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}(\mathbf{r})\) &
convention-tagged diagnostic &
\(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{so}}=1\) \\
Modal ``spin fraction'' from inversion & inference-only &
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.observable-decision-rule} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

Table~\ref{tab:ch1.spin-orbit-reporting} is the practical companion to
Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.spin-orbit-equivalence}: it tells the experimenter which
numbers may appear in a results table without a convention footnote and which must
carry explicit split, gauge, and kernel declarations. Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.6.3}
returns to gauge orbit motion in worked form; the present subsection supplies the
general limit that makes that example predictable rather than surprising.

\begin{proposition}[Limits of local split observability]
\label{prop:ch1.spin-orbit-limit-criterion}
Local spin-orbit components are direct observables in this chapter only when
\(\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{so}}=1\); otherwise they remain controlled interpretive
diagnostics attached to a fixed total observable.
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.local-spin-orbit-split} defines decomposition class.
Equations~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-equivalence-class} and
\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-gauge-redistribution} establish local non-uniqueness.
Equations~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-volume-test} and
\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-boundary-closure} quantify boundary-conditioned integral
differences. Equations~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-measurement-residual} and
\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-observable-decision} enforce operational closure. The
conjunction yields the criterion.

Projection dependence produces an additional interpretive limit. For any detector
weighting kernel \(w(\mathbf{r})\), define projected split reports
\(\Pi_s=\int_V w(\mathbf{r})\,\mathbf{s}_{\mathrm{loc}}(\mathbf{r})\,dV\) and
\(\Pi_l=\int_V w(\mathbf{r})\,\mathbf{l}_{\mathrm{loc}}(\mathbf{r})\,dV\). Two
equally admissible kernels \(w_a\) and \(w_b\) need not preserve these projections.
The projection-sensitivity index
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-projection-sensitivity}
\mathcal{P}_{\mathrm{so}}
=
\frac{\|\Pi_s^{(a)}-\Pi_s^{(b)}\|+\|\Pi_l^{(a)}-\Pi_l^{(b)}\|}
{\|\Pi_s^{(a)}+\Pi_l^{(a)}\|+\epsilon_p},
\end{equation}
with \(\epsilon_p>0\), quantifies this dependence. Large
\(\mathcal{P}_{\mathrm{so}}\) indicates that local split claims are dominated by
sampling protocol, not invariant transport content.

The derivation assumes smooth fields, linear constitutive response, and boundary
regularity sufficient for divergence manipulations. Singular gauges, strongly
inhomogeneous media, and nonlocal material response can enlarge redistribution
terms and require generalized treatment not developed in Chapter~\ref{ch:01}.

The limit is therefore explicit and constructive: local spin-orbit decomposition
remains a controlled interpretive lens, whereas frame/gauge-admissible totals and
channel-defined measurements remain the primary observables
\cite{stratton1941}. In practice, a reader should treat every
published spin-orbit map as carrying three mandatory metadata fields: split
convention (Family~A, Family~B, or declared variant), gauge choice if potentials
enter the definition, and control volume \(V\) with outward normal on \(\partial V\).
Omitting any one field voids comparability with the criteria of
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.spin-orbit-observable-decision} even when the visualization is
striking \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Section 1.4 summary and continuation.}
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.4} filters potential-level statements by gauge invariance,
local-density closure, helicity symmetry, and spin-orbit equivalence limits; the
corollary symbols appear in Table~\ref{tab:ch1.admissibility-corollaries}
\cite{stratton1941}. Section~\ref{sec:ch01.5} converts
surviving quantities into instrument-ready reports.


\section{Measurement and Interpretation}
\label{sec:ch01.5}

\emph{Measurement} names the interaction, transduction, and calibration chain that
produces numbers from fields; \emph{interpretation} names the inference step that maps
those numbers to angular-structure claims under declared uncertainty
\cite{stratton1941,hansen1988}. Physics in this monograph means measurable quantities:
a modal index or phase winding is not a report until a finite instrument and finite
aperture declare what was actually resolved---the accessibility theme seeded in
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.0} and closed in Chapter~4 \cite{harrington2001}. Section~\ref{sec:ch01.5}
therefore treats finite bandwidth, finite rank, and ill-conditioned inversion as
physical limits, not numerical nuisances.

Section~\ref{sec:ch01.5} converts the admissible field quantities from
Section~\ref{sec:ch01.4} into measurable reports under finite sensors, finite
bandwidth, and finite inference accuracy. The central task is to define the full
measurement chain---interaction law, transduction channel, reconstruction map, and
uncertainty-qualified interpretation---for torque, flux, and modal content in a way
that preserves gauge/frame objectivity at every stage
\cite{stratton1941}. Accordingly, each subsection separates
direct observables from model-dependent reconstructions, states the closure
conditions under which inferred quantities are admissible, and ties interpretation
claims to explicit transfer equations rather than to decomposition labels alone.


\subsection{Field-matter interaction}
\label{sec:ch01.5.1}
% TOC tag: [HOME]

Section~\ref{sec:ch01.4} fixed which field-level quantities survive gauge and frame
scrutiny and which remain representation-dependent. Section~\ref{sec:ch01.5}
translates those survivors into laboratory language: currents induced in matter,
forces and torques on test bodies, modal projections on apertures, and the decisive
distinction between direct observables and inference-dependent reconstructions. The
present subsection begins that translation at the most elementary coupling point---
the local exchange of energy and momentum between electromagnetic fields and material
degrees of freedom \cite{stratton1941}.

Why begin with matter rather than with free-space flux? Because every angular
transport claim in this chapter ultimately meets a detector, absorber, or mechanical
load. The Maxwell stress tensor of Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4} describes traction
on surfaces; the Lorentz force density below describes how those tractions arise
from charge and current inside a body. Without Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-power-density}
and Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-force-density}, torque channels in
Subsections~\ref{sec:ch01.5.2} and~\ref{sec:ch01.6} would float without a material
anchor. Readers should therefore read \(p_m\), \(\mathbf{f}_m\), and
\(\boldsymbol{\tau}_m\) as the first rung on the same ladder as
\(\Pi\), \(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_J\), and \(\chi\): geometry and conservation at the
field level, then transduction at the matter level, then calibration at the
instrument level.

Field--matter interaction is the physical interface through which electromagnetic fields
become measurable laboratory signals. In this subsection, \emph{field} denotes the
phasor state \((\E,\H)\), \emph{matter} denotes the charged and polarized medium in a
declared region, and \emph{interaction} denotes transfer of energy, linear
momentum, and angular momentum into material degrees of freedom under explicit
constitutive and boundary assumptions \cite{stratton1941}. This framing
is necessary because angular-structure claims are not observations by themselves;
they become observable only after a channel from field variables to material
response is stated quantitatively.

Let \(V_m\subset\Omega\) denote the material interaction region, with boundary
\(\partial V_m\), outward unit normal \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\), and volume element
\(dV\). Let \(\E(\mathbf{r})\) and \(\H(\mathbf{r})\) be phasor fields under the
\(\exp(-\jj\omega t)\) convention, let \(\mathbf{J}_c(\mathbf{r})\) be conduction-current
density inside matter, and let \(\rho(\mathbf{r})\) be free-charge density. The
time-averaged electromagnetic power-transfer density into matter is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.material-power-density}
p_m(\mathbf{r})=\frac{1}{2}\Re\!\left\{\E\cdot\mathbf{J}_c^*\right\},
\end{equation}
where \((\cdot)^*\) denotes complex conjugation and \(\Re\{\cdot\}\) extracts the
real part. Operand by operand, \(\E\cdot\mathbf{J}_c^*\) is local field-current work-rate
density in complex form, and the factor \(1/2\) converts phasor bilinear form to
time average. As a complete relation,
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-power-density} identifies the local channel by which
electromagnetic energy is converted into measurable material response such as
heating or mechanical actuation \cite{stratton1941}.

Mechanical transfer is described by the cycle-averaged Lorentz-force density
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.material-force-density}
\mathbf{f}_m(\mathbf{r})
=\frac{1}{2}\Re\!\left\{
\rho\,\E+\mathbf{J}_c\times\mu\,\H^*
\right\},
\end{equation}
where \(\mu\) is permeability and \(\times\) is vector cross product. The first
term \(\rho\,\E\) is electric-force density on free charge, while
\(\mathbf{J}_c\times\mu\H^*\) is magnetic-force density on moving charge carriers. Taken
together, Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-force-density} is the local force-transfer
law linking measurable mechanical loading to field state and material current.

To separate dissipative and source-driven channels, decompose
\(\mathbf{J}_c=\sigma\E+\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{ext}}\), where \(\sigma\ge 0\) is
effective conductivity and \(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{ext}}\) externally impressed
current density. Substitution into Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-power-density} yields
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.material-power-decomposition}
p_m
=
\frac{1}{2}\sigma\|\E\|^2
+\frac{1}{2}\Re\!\left\{\E\cdot\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{ext}}^*\right\}.
\end{equation}
The first term is irreversible Joule deposition; the second term is controllable
exchange with the impressed source network.

Finite-time momentum transfer is obtained by temporal integration of
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-force-density}:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.material-momentum-transfer}
\Delta\mathbf{p}_m(t_1,t_2)
=
\int_{t_1}^{t_2}\!\!\int_{V_m}\mathbf{f}_m(\mathbf{r},t)\,dV\,dt.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-momentum-transfer} links local loading density to
impulse measurements over finite acquisition windows.

When fields enter a material body, the field stress tensor is not the only momentum
bookkeeping object under debate. Minkowski \cite{minkowski1908} and Abraham
\cite{abraham1910} proposed distinct symmetric energy-momentum tensors for dielectric
media; they agree in vacuum but assign different electromagnetic momentum densities
inside matter. Because every torque experiment ultimately couples to a material load,
the controversy cannot be bypassed: one must state which momentum density enters
interior reports and which exterior closure is used on vacuum-side control surfaces
\cite{stratton1941}.

In the \(\exp(-\jj\omega t)\) phasor convention adopted throughout this volume,
let \(\mathbf{D}=\varepsilon\E\) and \(\mathbf{B}=\mu\H\) denote constitutive fields
in a linear isotropic region with permittivity \(\varepsilon\) and permeability
\(\mu\). The cycle-averaged Poynting vector remains
\(\mathbf{S}=\tfrac{1}{2}\Re\{\E\times\H^*\}\), the quantity Poynting
\cite{poynting1884} identified as directional energy transfer. Minkowski's
electromagnetic momentum density is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.minkowski-momentum-density}
\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{M}}
=
\frac{1}{2}\Re\!\left\{\mathbf{D}\times\mathbf{B}^*\right\},
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{D}\times\mathbf{B}^*\) is the complex bilinear cross product and
the factor \(1/2\) converts phasor form to time average. Physically,
\(\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{M}}\) treats \(\mathbf{D}\) and \(\mathbf{B}\) as the
canonical field pair for momentum storage in material media: polarization and
magnetization enter through \(\mathbf{D}\) and \(\mathbf{B}\) rather than through
\(\mathbf{E}\) and \(\mathbf{H}\) alone \cite{minkowski1908,stratton1941}.

Abraham's electromagnetic momentum density, expressed in the same phasor SI units, is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.abraham-momentum-density}
\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{A}}
=
\frac{1}{c^2}\,\Re\!\left\{\mathbf{S}\right\}
=
\frac{1}{2c^2}\Re\!\left\{\E\times\H^*\right\}
=
\frac{\varepsilon_0\mu_0}{2}\Re\!\left\{\E\times\H^*\right\},
\end{equation}
where \(c=(\varepsilon_0\mu_0)^{-1/2}\) is vacuum light speed. Equation
\eqref{eq:ch1.abraham-momentum-density} associates momentum with energy flux divided
by \(c^2\), the vacuum kinematic relation between flux and directed momentum
\cite{abraham1910}. In source-free vacuum
\(\varepsilon=\varepsilon_0\), \(\mu=\mu_0\), and
\(\mathbf{D}=\varepsilon_0\E\), \(\mathbf{B}=\mu_0\H\), so
\(\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{M}}=\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{A}}=\Re\{\mathbf{S}\}/c^2\): the
historical disagreement vanishes identically in exterior vacuum regions.

Inside a nonmagnetic dielectric with \(\mu=\mu_0\) and \(\varepsilon=\varepsilon_0 n^2\),
the two densities differ by a definite local excess
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.am-momentum-difference}
\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{M}}-\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{A}}
=
\frac{n^2-1}{c^2}\,\Re\!\left\{\mathbf{S}\right\},
\end{equation}
where \(n\) is refractive index. Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.am-momentum-difference}
quantifies the bookkeeping split: Minkowski assigns additional momentum proportional
to \((n^2-1)\Re\{\mathbf{S}\}/c^2\) inside the medium, whereas Abraham keeps the
vacuum flux-over-\(c^2\) rule locally. Total force on a closed system remains
conserved in both formalisms once material mechanical stress is included; the dispute
concerns how momentum is partitioned between field and matter subaccounts
\cite{stratton1941}.

Each formalism carries a symmetric energy-momentum tensor \(\mathbf{T}^{(\mathrm{M})}\)
or \(\mathbf{T}^{(\mathrm{A})}\) whose \(0j\) row sums field momentum flux and whose
\(ij\) block produces traction on surfaces. Schematically, the total tensor decomposes as
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.am-tensor-partition}
\mathbf{T}^{(\mathrm{tot})}
=
\mathbf{T}^{(\mathrm{em})}
+\mathbf{T}^{(\mathrm{mech})},
\end{equation}
where \(\mathbf{T}^{(\mathrm{em})}\) is the chosen electromagnetic tensor
(Minkowski or Abraham) and \(\mathbf{T}^{(\mathrm{mech})}\) is the mechanical
stress of the medium. The partition in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.am-tensor-partition} is
not arbitrary: changing \(\mathbf{T}^{(\mathrm{em})}\) shifts the compensating
\(\mathbf{T}^{(\mathrm{mech})}\) so that the sum obeys the same global force balance.
Laboratory reports must therefore state which electromagnetic subtensor is used before
comparing simulated traction with torsion-balance readouts
\cite{stratton1941}.

Exterior vacuum reduction is the operational simplification used in
Subsections~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4}--\ref{sec:ch01.1.5}. When a control surface
\(\partial V\) lies entirely in source-free vacuum, both formalisms reduce to the
Maxwell stress tensor of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.maxwell-stress}, and momentum flux through
\(\partial V\) is unambiguous:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.exterior-vacuum-momentum-flux}
\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{em}}
=
\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{T}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
=
\int_{\partial V}\frac{\Re\{\mathbf{S}\}}{c^2}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS,
\end{equation}
where \(\hat{\mathbf{n}}\) is outward normal. Equation
\eqref{eq:ch1.exterior-vacuum-momentum-flux} is the vacuum-side closure used for
angular-momentum transport criteria in this chapter: Beth-type loads \cite{beth1936},
aperture enclosures, and far-zone radiative shells all evaluate
\(\mathbf{T}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\) in exterior vacuum, not \(\mathbf{D}\times\mathbf{B}\)
inside the sample. Matter-coupled experiments must report whether traction is evaluated
on a field-only exterior surface or includes material stress corrections; conflating
the two mixes Abraham--Minkowski interior bookkeeping with vacuum exterior closure.

Torque-channel transfer follows from the moment of force density about declared
origin \(\mathbf{r}_0\):
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.material-torque-density}
\boldsymbol{\tau}_m(\mathbf{r};\mathbf{r}_0)
=
(\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{r}_0)\times\mathbf{f}_m(\mathbf{r}).
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-torque-density} makes origin dependence explicit at
the interaction stage, so origin declaration is part of the measurement definition,
not a secondary reporting detail \cite{stratton1941}.

Integrating local channels over \(V_m\) gives measurable global interaction outputs:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.material-global-channels}
P_m=\int_{V_m}p_m\,dV,\qquad
\mathbf{F}_m=\int_{V_m}\mathbf{f}_m\,dV,\qquad
\mathbf{T}_m=\int_{V_m}\boldsymbol{\tau}_m\,dV.
\end{equation}
The triple \((P_m,\mathbf{F}_m,\mathbf{T}_m)\) is the first complete observable map
for this subsection: thermal conversion, net mechanical load, and torque transfer
must be interpreted jointly when evaluating field structure.

Control-volume closure links boundary flux to deposited material power. With energy
density \(u=\frac{1}{4}(\varepsilon\|\E\|^2+\mu\|\H\|^2)\) and time-average Poynting
vector \(\langle\mathbf{S}\rangle=\frac{1}{2}\Re\{\E\times\H^*\}\),
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.material-balance-control-volume}
\int_{\partial V_m}\langle\mathbf{S}\rangle\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
+\int_{V_m}p_m\,dV
+\frac{d}{dt}\int_{V_m}u\,dV
=0.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-balance-control-volume} is the local-to-global
consistency condition used to test whether inferred interaction channels are
physically closed at the chosen averaging scale.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigBlock{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1fig, node distance=6mm]
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=48mm] (A) {Stage A:\\field $(\E,\H)$ on $V_m$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=48mm, below=of A] (B)
    {Stage B:\\$p_m$,\ $\mathbf{f}_m$,\ $\boldsymbol{\tau}_m$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=48mm, below=of B] (C)
    {Stage C:\\$P_m$,\ $\mathbf{F}_m$,\ $\mathbf{T}_m$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=48mm, below=of C] (D)
    {Stage D:\\$\int_{\partial V_m}\!\langle\mathbf{S}\rangle\!\cdot\!\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS$\\
     $+\int_{V_m}p_m\,dV+\frac{d}{dt}\int_{V_m}u\,dV$};
  \node[ch1box, minimum width=48mm, below=of D, fill=green!8] (out)
    {Accepted: channel-qualified thermal/mechanical observables};
  \draw[ch1flow] (A) -- (B);
  \draw[ch1flow] (B) -- (C);
  \draw[ch1flow] (C) -- (D);
  \draw[ch1flow] (D) -- (out);
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Operational schematic for local-to-global field--matter interaction: local exchange channels, integrated observables, and control-volume closure.}
\label{fig:ch1.field-matter-transfer}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.field-matter-transfer} identifies the exact transition from
field description to measurement statement: every reported quantity is mediated by
declared interaction channels, not by pattern visualization alone. In particular,
the presence of angular structure in the field does not imply measurable torque
unless the force and torque channels satisfy closure and calibration constraints.

For regime comparison, define the field--matter coupling index
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.material-coupling-index}
\mathcal{C}_m
=
\frac{\left|\int_{V_m}p_m\,dV\right|}
     {\left|\int_{\partial V_m}\langle\mathbf{S}\rangle\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS\right|+\epsilon_c},
\qquad \epsilon_c>0.
\end{equation}
Small \(\mathcal{C}_m\) indicates weak conversion and predominantly transmissive
interaction; large \(\mathcal{C}_m\) indicates strong local conversion into thermal
or mechanical response. Because \(\mathcal{C}_m\) is normalized, it compares
interaction strength across setups with different absolute power levels.

Interaction reports are accepted in this chapter only when operational and closure
requirements are both met:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.material-interaction-admissibility}
\mathcal{D}_m=1
\iff
\Big(
\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{op}}=1,\
\left|\int_{\partial V_m}\langle\mathbf{S}\rangle\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
+\int_{V_m}p_m\,dV
+\frac{d}{dt}\int_{V_m}u\,dV\right|\le \delta_E,\
\mathcal{C}_m\le C_{m,\max}
\Big),
\end{equation}
where \(\delta_E\ge 0\) is closure tolerance and \(C_{m,\max}\ge 0\) is interaction
classification threshold. Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-interaction-admissibility}
prevents non-closed transfer channels from being interpreted as physical evidence.

\begin{proposition}[Admissible field--matter interaction report]
\label{prop:ch1.field-matter-interaction-admissibility}
The interaction outputs \((P_m,\mathbf{F}_m,\mathbf{T}_m)\) are admissible
observables in Chapter~\ref{ch:01} only if \(\mathcal{D}_m=1\).
\end{proposition}
\noindent\textit{Proof sketch.}
Equations~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-power-density}--\eqref{eq:ch1.material-torque-density}
define local transfer channels. Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-global-channels}
maps these channels to measurable totals. Equation
\eqref{eq:ch1.material-balance-control-volume} enforces energy closure, and
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-interaction-admissibility} imposes operational
admissibility inherited from Section~\ref{sec:ch01.4}. Their conjunction yields the
criterion.

For finite-time sampling windows, impulse-channel measurement follows from
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.material-impulse-measurement}
\mathbf{I}_m(t_1,t_2)
=
\int_{t_1}^{t_2}\mathbf{F}_m(t)\,dt
=
\int_{t_1}^{t_2}\!\!\int_{V_m}\mathbf{f}_m(\mathbf{r},t)\,dV\,dt.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-impulse-measurement} links cycle-averaged force
densities to time-window detector outputs, making the temporal meaning of reported
mechanical response explicit.

The model presumes quasi-static sensor response over one cycle-average window,
continuum charge--current descriptions, and surface geometry known to sufficient
precision for traction integration. Strong nonlinear transport, particle kinetics,
ultrafast transients, or unresolved multiscale material structure require
generalized channel models beyond the present establishment
\cite{stratton1941,abraham1910}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: Joule heating and torque on a conducting disk.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \draw[ch1axis] (-2.0,0) -- (2.0,0) node[right] {$\hat{\mathbf{x}}$};
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,-0.15) -- (0,0.15) node[ch1oncanvas, above] {$\hat{\mathbf{y}}$};
  \draw[thick, fill=gray!15, draw=black!70] (0,0) circle (1.35);
  \node[ch1oncanvas] at (0,-1.65) {conducting disk:\ radius $a$,\ thickness $t$};
  \draw[->, very thick, blue!60!black] (-2.5,0.55) -- (-1.55,0.55)
    node[midway, above, ch1lblout] {$\E=E_0\hat{\mathbf{x}}$};
  \draw[->, very thick, red!70!black] (-2.5,-0.55) -- (-1.55,-0.55)
    node[midway, below, ch1lblout] {$\H=H_0\hat{\mathbf{y}}$};
  \draw[->, thick, orange!85!black] (1.35,0) arc (0:-300:0.35);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, orange!85!black, align=center] at (1.85,-0.35)
    {edge torque\\about $\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, align=center] at (0,-2.25)
    {$P_m$ from \ closure via };
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Worked-problem schematic: Joule heating and edge-local Lorentz torque on a conducting disk in a transverse field.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-conducting-disk}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-conducting-disk} pairs the heating channel of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-disk-joule} with torque integration in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-torque-density}: any torque report on the same fixture must close with deposited \(P_m\) through the control-volume balance.
Let a thin conducting disk of radius \(a\), conductivity \(\sigma\), occupy
\(V_m=\{0\le z\le t,\ \rho\le a\}\) in a uniform phasor field
\(\E=E_0\hat{\mathbf{x}}\), \(\H=H_0\hat{\mathbf{y}}\). From
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-power-decomposition} with \(\mathbf{J}_{\mathrm{ext}}=\mathbf{0}\),
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.worked-disk-joule}
P_m=\int_{V_m}\frac{1}{2}\sigma\|\E\|^2\,dV
=\frac{1}{2}\sigma E_0^2\pi a^2 t.
\end{equation}
The Lorentz-force density of Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-force-density} reduces to
\(\mathbf{f}_m=\tfrac{1}{2}\Re\{\mathbf{J}_c\times\mu\H^*\}\) with
\(\mathbf{J}_c=\sigma\E\), giving net force \(\mathbf{F}_m=\mathbf{0}\) by symmetry
but edge-local torque density about \(\hat{\mathbf{z}}\) when \(\mathbf{E}\) and
\(\mathbf{H}\) are not collinear. Integrating
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-torque-density} and closing with
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.material-balance-control-volume} checks that deposited
\(P_m\) equals net Poynting influx through \(\partial V_m\) minus storage change.
For \(a=1\,\mathrm{cm}\), \(t=1\,\mathrm{mm}\), \(\sigma=5.8\times 10^7\,\mathrm{S/m}\),
and \(|E_0|=10^3\,\mathrm{V/m}\), Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-disk-joule} gives
\(P_m\approx 0.91\,\mathrm{W}\), a measurable heating channel that must accompany
any torque report on the same fixture \cite{stratton1941}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: dielectric half-space and exterior momentum closure.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.90]
  \fill[blue!12] (-2.5,-0.55) rectangle (2.5,0);
  \fill[gray!8] (-2.5,-2.2) rectangle (2.5,-0.55);
  \draw[thick, black!70] (-2.5,-0.55) -- (2.5,-0.55);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, anchor=west] at (-2.45,0.25) {vacuum $z>0$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, anchor=west] at (-2.45,-1.35) {dielectric $z<0$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas] at (0,-0.55) [below=2mm] {$z=0$ interface};
  \draw[->, very thick, blue!60!black] (0,0.35) -- (0,1.55)
    node[midway, right, ch1lblout] {$\E=E_0 e^{-\jj k_0 z}\hat{\mathbf{x}}$};
  \draw[thick, red!70!black] (-1.2,-0.55) -- (1.2,-0.55);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, red!70!black, above] at (0,-0.55) {$\partial V$ cap at $z=0^+$};
  \node[ch1oncanvas, align=center] at (0,-2.55)
    {$\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{vac}}$ from \ cap area $A_{\mathrm{cap}}$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Worked-problem schematic: vacuum-side cap integration for exterior momentum flux at a dielectric half-space boundary.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-halfspace-momentum}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-halfspace-momentum} makes the cap-area dependence in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-halfspace-momentum-flux} explicit: traction is reported on the vacuum-side boundary so interior Minkowski--Abraham bookkeeping does not alter the exterior closure integral.
A semi-infinite nonmagnetic dielectric (\(n>1\), \(\mu=\mu_0\)) occupies
\(z<0\); vacuum (\(n=1\)) occupies \(z>0\). A normally incident plane wave
\(\E=E_0 e^{-\jj k_0 z}\hat{\mathbf{x}}\), \(\H=H_0 e^{-\jj k_0 z}\hat{\mathbf{y}}\)
propagates in vacuum toward the interface at \(z=0\). The vacuum-side Poynting flux
is \(\Re\{\mathbf{S}\}=(|E_0|^2/2\eta_0)\hat{\mathbf{z}}\)
\cite{poynting1884}, where \(\eta_0=\sqrt{\mu_0/\varepsilon_0}\). A control
surface \(\partial V\) enclosing only the vacuum half-space \(z>0\) with a disk
cap at \(z=0^+\) yields exterior momentum flux
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.worked-halfspace-momentum-flux}
\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{vac}}
=
\int_{\partial V}\mathbf{T}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\,dS
=
\frac{\Re\{\mathbf{S}\}}{c^2}\,A_{\mathrm{cap}}
=
\frac{|E_0|^2 A_{\mathrm{cap}}}{2\eta_0 c^2}\,\hat{\mathbf{z}},
\end{equation}
where \(A_{\mathrm{cap}}\) is cap area and Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.exterior-vacuum-momentum-flux}
applies because \(\partial V\) lies in vacuum. Inside the dielectric,
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.am-momentum-difference} gives
\(\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{M}}-\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{A}}=
[(n^2-1)/c^2]\Re\{\mathbf{S}_t\}\hat{\mathbf{z}}\), where \(\mathbf{S}_t\) is
transmitted flux; this interior excess does not alter
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-halfspace-momentum-flux} as long as traction is reported
on the vacuum-side cap. For \(n=1.5\), \(|E_0|=10^3\,\mathrm{V/m}\),
\(A_{\mathrm{cap}}=1\,\mathrm{cm}^2\), the vacuum flux is
\(|\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{vac}}|\approx 2.2\times 10^{-18}\,\mathrm{N\,s}\) per cycle
average---small but definite, and independent of whether Minkowski or Abraham
bookkeeping is used interior to the dielectric \cite{minkowski1908,abraham1910}.

\paragraph{Worked problem: Beth-type quarter-wave plate load.}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\ChOneFigGeo{%
\begin{tikzpicture}[ch1geo, scale=0.88]
  \draw[ch1axis] (0,-0.15) -- (0,2.9) node[ch1oncanvas, above] {$\hat{\mathbf{z}}$};
  \draw[thick, fill=cyan!10, draw=black!70] (-0.35,0.55) rectangle (0.35,1.45);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, align=center] at (0,1.0) {QWP};
  \draw[thick, gray!60] (0,1.45) -- (0,2.35);
  \node[ch1oncanvas, gray!60, right=12mm] at (0,1.9) {fiber};
  \draw[->, very thick, orange!85!black] (-2.2,1.0) -- (-0.55,1.0);
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=orange!12, orange!85!black, align=center, minimum width=32mm]
    at (-4.25,1.0) {Input block:\\RHCP beam};
  \node[ch1keybox, fill=gray!8, align=center, minimum width=40mm] at (0,-2.75)
    {Torque block:\\$\tau_z^{\mathrm{Beth}}=2P_{\mathrm{inc}}/\omega$};
\end{tikzpicture}%
}
\caption{Worked-problem schematic: circular-to-linear conversion on a quarter-wave plate and the associated spin angular-momentum transfer.}
\label{fig:ch1.wp-beth-qwp}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:ch1.wp-beth-qwp} isolates the vacuum-side traction route in Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-beth-torque}: the predicted torque scale follows from \(P_{\mathrm{inc}}\) and \(\omega\) alone once exterior closure is declared on the enclosing vacuum surface.
Following Beth \cite{beth1936}, a thin quartz quarter-wave plate of thickness
\(d=\lambda/4\) at operating frequency \(f\) is suspended on a torsion fiber and
illuminated by a collimated beam of right-circular polarization (\(\sigma=+1\)) with
vacuum-side power \(P_{\mathrm{inc}}\). The plate converts circular to linear
polarization (or the reverse), transferring spin angular momentum to the plate.
Exterior vacuum closure on a surface enclosing the plate gives axis torque
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ch1.worked-beth-torque}
\tau_z^{\mathrm{Beth}}
=
\frac{2\sigma}{\omega}\,P_{\mathrm{inc}}
=
\frac{2P_{\mathrm{inc}}}{\omega},
\end{equation}
for \(\sigma=+1\), where \(\omega=2\pi f\). At optical power
\(P_{\mathrm{inc}}=10\,\mathrm{mW}\) and \(f=5\times 10^{14}\,\mathrm{Hz}\),
Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.worked-beth-torque} predicts
\(|\tau_z^{\mathrm{Beth}}|\approx 6.4\times 10^{-20}\,\mathrm{N\,m}\), the scale
Beth measured mechanically \cite{beth1936}. The prediction uses only vacuum-side
\(\mathbf{T}\) and Eq.~\eqref{eq:ch1.exterior-vacuum-momentum-flux}; interior
\(\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{M}}\) versus \(\mathbf{g}_{\mathrm{A}}\) affects how momentum
is partitioned inside quartz but not the exterior torque report when
\(\partial V\) is placed in surrounding vacuum \cite{stratton1941}.

The next subsection converts the inherited Maxwell-stress framework from
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4} into calibrated torque and momentum-flux measurement
relations.


\subsection{Torque, momentum flux, and stress measurements}
\label{sec:ch01.5.2}
% TOC tag: [USE SS1.1.4]

\emph{Torque} is rotational mechanical action on a declared axis; \emph{momentum flux}
is directed field momentum crossing a surface; \emph{stress measurements} are
laboratory readouts of \(\mathbf{T}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\) after calibration and
geometry are declared \cite{poynting1884,beth1936}. These three channels test the same
Maxwellian ladder at increasing distance from the ideal continuum limit.

Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.5.1} fixed how fields deposit energy and momentum locally
in matter. Torque, momentum flux, and stress measurements are the quantities most
often quoted in angular-momentum experiments. Each term corresponds to a distinct
rung on the same Maxwellian ladder: \(\mathbf{T}\) is the field-theoretic object,
\(\mathbf{T}\cdot\hat{\mathbf{n}}\) is traction on a chosen surface, and
\(y_\tau\) is what a calibrated sensor reports after geometry, origin, and noise
are declared. The intellectual continuity with Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4} is
deliberate: stress is not re-derived here; it is \emph{used} under the framework
established in Section~\ref{sec:ch01.1} \cite{poynting1884,belinfante1940}.

Stress-based measurement inherits the owner framework of
Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.1.4} without re-deriving field momentum balance. The
objective is operational: map the established stress tensor to instrument outputs
for force and torque with declared geometry, calibration, and uncertainty
\cite{beth1936,poynting1884}.

The experimental lineage of torque readouts begins with Beth's 1936 detection of
mechanical rotation in quartz wave plates illuminated by circularly polarized light
\cite{beth1936}. That experiment established that polarization-linked angular
momentum has mechanical consequences at laboratory scale, decades before
Laguerre--Gaussian beams were identified as orbital-indexed modes
\cite{allen1992}. Modern torsion-balance protocols inherit the same logical
structure Beth used: predict torque from field stress, measure mechanical response,
and treat discrepancy as channel or regime error rather than as permission to
abandon conservation \cite{stratton1941}. Subsection~\ref{sec:ch01.6.1}
returns to this closure pattern in fully worked form.

Let \(\mathbf{T}\) be Maxwell stress tensor from
